Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
6 min
Refuting the Fall of Obligations for Saints
Refutes the claim of falling obligations for saints.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that perfect saints are exempt from religious duties is false, and how the states of prophetic perfection and saintly absorption clarify the issue.

Core Idea
Spiritual perfection increases responsibility, not diminishes it.

Some have claimed that perfect saints (awliyā') are no longer bound by religious obligations due to their high spiritual state. The author refutes this by pointing to the generality of divine commands and the example of the prophets, who bear the fullest obligations despite their perfection. He also addresses an exceptional case and explains the nature of saintly absorption that may lead to involuntary neglect, all while emphasizing the superiority of the prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice the author's argumentative style: he gives general proofs, then addresses a counterexample, then refines the discussion.
2
Identify the main proofs
Find the two proofs the author offers against the falling of obligations: the generality of address (العموم) and the example of the prophets, especially Muhammad.
3
Analyze the exceptional story
Examine the anecdote about the saint who asked for exemption. Note that Allah did not exempt him but removed his reason, making him non-obligated. This shows exemption is not a reward but a removal of capacity.
4
Understand the state of absorption
Learn the concept of istighrāq (absorption) where a saint becomes oblivious to the world and may neglect obligations unintentionally, being like a sleeper. This is not sinful but is a form of 'divine insanity'.
5
Grasp the conclusion on prophetic superiority
See how the discussion proves that prophets are superior to saints because they maintain perfect obedience even in the height of absorption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بطلان القول بسقوط الأمر والنهي فلعموم الخطابات، ولأن أكمل الناس في المحبة والإخلاص هم الأنبياء، سيما حبيب الله ، مع أن التكاليف في حقهم أتم وأكمل ، حتى يعاتبون بأدنى زلة، بل بترك الأفضل. نعم حكي عن بعض الأولياء أنه استعفى الله عن التكاليف، وسأله الإعتاق عن ظواهر العبادات، فأجابه إلى ذلك بأن سلبه العقل الذي هو مناط التكليف، ومع ذلك كان من علو المرتبة على ماكان . وأنت خبير بأن العارف لا يسأم من العبادة، ولا يفتر في الطاعة ولا يسأل الهبوط من أوج الكمال إلى حضيض النقصان، والنزول من معارج الملك إلى منازل الحيوان، بل ربما يحصل له كمال الانجذاب إلى عالم القدس والاستغراق في ملاحظة جناب الحق ، بحيث يذهب عن هذا العالم، ويخل بالتكاليف من غير تأثم بذلك لكونه في حكم غير المكلف كالنائم ، وذلك لعجزه عن مراعاة الأمرين وملاحظة الجانبين، فربما يسأل دوام تلك الحالة، وعدم العودة إلى عالم الظاهر، وهذا الذهول هو الجنون الذي ربما يرجح على بعض العقول، والمتسمون به هم المسمون بمجانين العقلاء. وبهذا يظهر فضل الأنبياء على الأولياء، فإنهم مع أن استغراقهم أكمل ، وانجذابهم أشمل لا يخلون بأدنى طاعة.
As for the falsity of the claim of the falling of command and prohibition, it is due to the generality of the address, and because the most perfect of people in love and sincerity are the prophets, especially the Beloved of Allah, while obligations in their regard are more complete and perfect, to the point that they are rebuked for the slightest lapse, even for leaving the better. Yes, it is narrated from some saint that he asked Allah for exemption from obligations and requested release from the outward forms of worship, and He responded to him by depriving him of reason, which is the basis of obligation, and despite that, he was of high rank as he was. And you are well aware that the gnostic does not tire of worship, nor slacken in obedience, nor ask to descend from the peak of perfection to the depths of deficiency, nor to come down from the ascents of sovereignty to the stations of animals. Rather, perhaps there occurs to him perfect attraction to the world of holiness and absorption in contemplating the Divine Presence, such that he departs from this world and neglects obligations without being sinful thereby, because he is in the ruling of one not obligated, like one asleep. This is due to his inability to observe both matters and consider both sides. Perhaps he asks for the continuation of that state and not returning to the world of appearance. This absent-mindedness is the insanity that may be preferred over some intellects, and those characterized by it are called the 'majānīn al-'uqalā' (wise fools). By this, the superiority of prophets over saints becomes apparent, for they, despite their more perfect absorption and more comprehensive attraction, do not neglect even the slightest act of obedience.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage refutes the libertine claim that obligations (takālīf) fall away from perfect saints. The author gives two main proofs: (1) The divine commands are general (to all believers); (2) The most perfect in love and sincerity are the prophets, especially Muhammad, yet their obligations are more complete, and they are even rebuked for minor lapses. The author then addresses an exceptional case: a saint who asked to be exempted from obligations was answered by Allah removing his reason, the basis of obligation, but this is not a normal state. True gnostics ('ārif) never tire of worship. However, there is a state of absorption (istighrāq) where a saint becomes oblivious to the world and neglects obligations unintentionally, like a sleeper; this is not sinful but is a form of 'divine insanity' (junūn al-'uqalā'). This state actually demonstrates the superiority of prophets over saints, because prophets maintain perfect obedience even in their absorption.

Key Terms
سقوط التكاليف
الاستغراق
مجانين العقلاء
الذهول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Spiritual perfection means one is no longer required to perform religious duties.
Correction: Perfection actually increases responsibility, as seen in the prophets who bear the fullest obligations. Even saints who become absorbed in divine contemplation are only excused due to temporary loss of capacity, not because of their rank.

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence the author uses to refute the claim that obligations fall from saints?
A) A verse that says saints are not obligated.
B) The example of prophets who have the most perfect obligations.
C) A hadith that states obligations fall from saints.
D) The story of a saint who was exempted.

Key Takeaway
The claim that obligations fall from perfect saints is false; the prophets are the most perfect yet bear the fullest duties, and any exemption is due to temporary incapacity, not rank.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly love Allah, why do you sometimes seek ease or shortcuts in worship? What does this lesson’s emphasis on the prophets’ complete obligations say about your own attitude toward responsibility?
?
The saint who asked for exemption from duties lost his reason as a consequence. Have you ever wished to be freed from some religious obligation? What does that desire reveal about your spiritual ambition compared to the prophets who were rebuked for even leaving the better?
?
The lesson describes a state of absorption where saints involuntarily neglect duties, but the prophets never do. When you are sincerely absorbed in love for Allah, do you find yourself more careful about obedience or more neglectful? What would it take for you to reach the prophetic level where no act of obedience is ever left aside?

The Opponent's First Argument: Hardship Without Benefit is Injustice
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes the first opponent argument regarding injustice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's first argument that imposing hardship without reward is unjust, and the Ash'ari refutation that the benefit can be gratitude or praise.

Core Idea
Hardship without reward is not unjust; it can serve purposes like gratitude.

The opponent argues that if reward were not obligatory, imposing hardship would be unjust. Al-Taftazani presents this argument and then refutes it by offering alternative purposes for hardship. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and refutation
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully to understand the opponent's claim and the response.
Notice that the refutation concedes a purpose but denies obligation.
2
Identify the opponent's assumption
The opponent assumes that hardship must be compensated by reward to avoid injustice. Locate this assumption in the text.
3
Analyze the refutation's alternatives
The refutation offers alternative benefits: gratitude for blessings or joy at praise. Consider how these suffice as purposes.
The refutation also mentions that hardship itself could be the basis for obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج المخالف بوجوه : الأول - إلزام المشاق بلا منفعة تقابلها - وهي الثواب ـ ظلم ، وبلا مضرة في تركها - وهي العقاب - مستلزم لوجوب النوافل لثبوت المنفعة في فعلها . ورد بعد تسليم لزوم الغرض بأنه يجوز أن يكون الشكر على النعم، أو السرور بالمدح على أداء الواجب ، وأن يكون إيجاب الفعل بناء على أن له وجه وجوب بصفة المشقة، أو جعل شاقاً لغرض آخر . الثاني - عدم وجوبهما يفضي إلى التواني في الطاعات والاجتراء على المعاصي . ورد بأن مجرد جواز الترك مع شمول الوعد والوعيد وكثرة النصوص في الوقوع غير قادح في المقصود . الثالث - لو لم يجبا، لزم الخلف والكذب في إخبار الصادق . ورد بأن الوقوع لا يستلزم الوجوب والاستحقاق من الله تعالى .
The opponent argued with several points: First - Imposing hardships without a corresponding benefit - which is reward - is injustice, and without harm in abandoning them - which is punishment - necessitates the obligation of supererogatory acts because benefit is established in performing them. It was refuted after conceding the necessity of purpose by saying that it could be gratitude for blessings, or joy at praise for performing an obligation, and that making the act obligatory could be based on it having an aspect of obligation due to hardship, or making it difficult for another purpose. Second - Their non-obligation leads to negligence in acts of obedience and audacity toward sins. It was refuted by saying that mere possibility of abandonment, given the inclusiveness of promise and threat and the abundance of texts about occurrence, does not undermine the objective. Third - If they were not obligatory, that would entail breach and lying in the report of the truthful one. It was refuted by saying that occurrence does not necessitate obligation and entitlement from God Almighty.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments from the opponent (the Mu'tazila and others) who hold that reward and punishment are obligatory upon God, along with their refutations. The first argument states that imposing hardship without a corresponding benefit (reward) is injustice, and without harm in abandoning it (punishment) would necessitate that supererogatory acts become obligatory since they have benefit. The refutation concedes the need for a purpose but offers alternatives: gratitude for blessings, pride in praise for fulfilling obligations, or the act being made obligatory due to hardship itself, or being made difficult for another purpose. The second argument says that if reward and punishment were not obligatory, people would become lazy in obedience and bold in sin. The refutation counters that mere possibility of abandoning obedience, given the clear promise and threat and abundant scriptural evidence of actual occurrence, does not harm the intended objective (of deterrence). The third argument claims that if reward and punishment were not obligatory, it would entail a breach of promise and lying on God's part, since He has promised reward for obedience and threatened punishment for sin. The refutation clarifies that actual occurrence does not imply obligation or entitlement on God's part; God fulfills His promise out of grace, not necessity. Al-Taftazani follows the Ash'ari position that nothing is obligatory upon God.

Key Terms
ثواب
ظلم
منفعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imposing hardship without reward is necessarily unjust.
Correction: It can be just if the benefit is gratitude or praise for blessings.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation of the first argument, what can be the benefit of imposing hardship (other than reward)?
A) Monetary compensation
B) Gratitude for blessings and joy at praise
C) Avoiding punishment
D) Increased worship

Key Takeaway
The opponent's first argument is refuted by showing that hardship can serve other legitimate purposes like gratitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial in obeying Allah, do you find yourself demanding an immediate reward to feel that it is just? How does the possibility that hardship itself may be a form of worship change that expectation?
?
Have you ever felt that your struggles are pointless because you don't see clear compensation? What would it mean for your relationship with Allah if you truly believed that your difficulties could be intended for gratitude or joy in His praise?
?
If the purpose of a hardship could be simply to increase your awareness of gratitude, how would you live differently this week? Will you commit to thanking Allah for the very act of striving in obedience?

The Opponent's Second Argument: Non-Obligation Leads to Negligence
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes the second opponent argument regarding deterrence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's second argument that non-obligatory reward/punishment leads to negligence, and the refutation that scriptural promises and threats suffice for deterrence.

Core Idea
Clear promise and threat in scripture are enough to motivate, even without obligation.

The opponent argues that if reward and punishment are not obligatory, people will become lazy in worship and bold in sin. Al-Taftazani refutes this by emphasizing that the mere possibility of abandonment does not undermine the deterrent effect of divine promises and threats.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument and refutation
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the opponent's claim and the response.
2
Identify the opponent's concern
The opponent fears that without obligation, people will lack motivation. Locate this in the text.
3
Examine the refutation's logic
The refutation states that the abundance of scriptural texts about occurrence, combined with promise and threat, is sufficient to deter. Consider why possibility of abandonment does not invalidate this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج المخالف بوجوه : الأول - إلزام المشاق بلا منفعة تقابلها - وهي الثواب ـ ظلم ، وبلا مضرة في تركها - وهي العقاب - مستلزم لوجوب النوافل لثبوت المنفعة في فعلها . ورد بعد تسليم لزوم الغرض بأنه يجوز أن يكون الشكر على النعم، أو السرور بالمدح على أداء الواجب ، وأن يكون إيجاب الفعل بناء على أن له وجه وجوب بصفة المشقة، أو جعل شاقاً لغرض آخر . الثاني - عدم وجوبهما يفضي إلى التواني في الطاعات والاجتراء على المعاصي . ورد بأن مجرد جواز الترك مع شمول الوعد والوعيد وكثرة النصوص في الوقوع غير قادح في المقصود . الثالث - لو لم يجبا، لزم الخلف والكذب في إخبار الصادق . ورد بأن الوقوع لا يستلزم الوجوب والاستحقاق من الله تعالى .
The opponent argued with several points: First - Imposing hardships without a corresponding benefit - which is reward - is injustice, and without harm in abandoning them - which is punishment - necessitates the obligation of supererogatory acts because benefit is established in performing them. It was refuted after conceding the necessity of purpose by saying that it could be gratitude for blessings, or joy at praise for performing an obligation, and that making the act obligatory could be based on it having an aspect of obligation due to hardship, or making it difficult for another purpose. Second - Their non-obligation leads to negligence in acts of obedience and audacity toward sins. It was refuted by saying that mere possibility of abandonment, given the inclusiveness of promise and threat and the abundance of texts about occurrence, does not undermine the objective. Third - If they were not obligatory, that would entail breach and lying in the report of the truthful one. It was refuted by saying that occurrence does not necessitate obligation and entitlement from God Almighty.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments from the opponent (the Mu'tazila and others) who hold that reward and punishment are obligatory upon God, along with their refutations. The first argument states that imposing hardship without a corresponding benefit (reward) is injustice, and without harm in abandoning it (punishment) would necessitate that supererogatory acts become obligatory since they have benefit. The refutation concedes the need for a purpose but offers alternatives: gratitude for blessings, pride in praise for fulfilling obligations, or the act being made obligatory due to hardship itself, or being made difficult for another purpose. The second argument says that if reward and punishment were not obligatory, people would become lazy in obedience and bold in sin. The refutation counters that mere possibility of abandoning obedience, given the clear promise and threat and abundant scriptural evidence of actual occurrence, does not harm the intended objective (of deterrence). The third argument claims that if reward and punishment were not obligatory, it would entail a breach of promise and lying on God's part, since He has promised reward for obedience and threatened punishment for sin. The refutation clarifies that actual occurrence does not imply obligation or entitlement on God's part; God fulfills His promise out of grace, not necessity. Al-Taftazani follows the Ash'ari position that nothing is obligatory upon God.

Key Terms
وعد
وعيد
تواني
اجتراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If reward and punishment are not obligatory, people will inevitably become negligent and sinful.
Correction: The clear promise and threat in scripture are sufficient to motivate, even if not obligatory.

Checkpoint
How does the refutation of the second argument defend against the claim of negligence?
A) It denies that people become negligent
B) It argues that promise and threat are enough despite possibility
C) It says obligation is necessary to prevent negligence
D) It claims the opponent is mistaken

Key Takeaway
The opponent's second argument is refuted by emphasizing that scriptural promises and threats are powerful motivators even without necessitating obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own acts of obedience, do you rely more on a sense of obligation or on the trust that God's promise is certain? Are there times when you neglect worship because you doubt the certainty of reward? This lesson challenges that doubt. What specific steps will you take today to strengthen your trust in God's promise?
?
If you were told that God's reward and punishment are not obligatory upon Him, would that make you less careful about sin? The refutation says that the abundance of scripture suffices. How much do you actually read and internalize these scriptures? Can you honestly say that the threat of punishment deters you from a sin you struggle with? What one verse about punishment can you memorize this week to make the threat more real to you?
?
The opponent fears that non-obligation leads to negligence. Yet the refutation points to the power of divine promise. In your own life, has your obedience ever been driven by a deep conviction in God's promise rather than a sense of duty? Describe that moment. If not, why do you think you haven't experienced that? What needs to change in your heart?

The Opponent's Third Argument: Non-Obligation Entails Lying
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes the third opponent argument regarding breach of promise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's third argument that non-obligatory reward/punishment would imply God is lying, and the refutation that occurrence does not entail obligation.

Core Idea
God's promise can be true without it being obligatory upon Him.

The opponent argues that if reward and punishment were not obligatory, God would be lying because He has promised them. Al-Taftazani refutes by clarifying that actual occurrence does not imply that God is obligated to fulfill his promise; He does so out of grace.

Guided Path
1
Read the third argument and refutation
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the opponent's logical consequence and the response.
2
Understand the opponent's reasoning
The opponent assumes that if God promises something and it does not occur, that would be lying. But they also assume that non-obligation means non-occurrence.
3
Analyze the refutation's distinction
The refutation separates occurrence from obligation: God's promise will indeed occur, but that does not make it obligatory upon Him in a necessitating sense.
This preserves God's freedom and grace.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج المخالف بوجوه : الأول - إلزام المشاق بلا منفعة تقابلها - وهي الثواب ـ ظلم ، وبلا مضرة في تركها - وهي العقاب - مستلزم لوجوب النوافل لثبوت المنفعة في فعلها . ورد بعد تسليم لزوم الغرض بأنه يجوز أن يكون الشكر على النعم، أو السرور بالمدح على أداء الواجب ، وأن يكون إيجاب الفعل بناء على أن له وجه وجوب بصفة المشقة، أو جعل شاقاً لغرض آخر . الثاني - عدم وجوبهما يفضي إلى التواني في الطاعات والاجتراء على المعاصي . ورد بأن مجرد جواز الترك مع شمول الوعد والوعيد وكثرة النصوص في الوقوع غير قادح في المقصود . الثالث - لو لم يجبا، لزم الخلف والكذب في إخبار الصادق . ورد بأن الوقوع لا يستلزم الوجوب والاستحقاق من الله تعالى .
The opponent argued with several points: First - Imposing hardships without a corresponding benefit - which is reward - is injustice, and without harm in abandoning them - which is punishment - necessitates the obligation of supererogatory acts because benefit is established in performing them. It was refuted after conceding the necessity of purpose by saying that it could be gratitude for blessings, or joy at praise for performing an obligation, and that making the act obligatory could be based on it having an aspect of obligation due to hardship, or making it difficult for another purpose. Second - Their non-obligation leads to negligence in acts of obedience and audacity toward sins. It was refuted by saying that mere possibility of abandonment, given the inclusiveness of promise and threat and the abundance of texts about occurrence, does not undermine the objective. Commentary
This passage presents three arguments from the opponent (the Mu'tazila and others) who hold that reward and punishment are obligatory upon God, along with their refutations. The first argument states that imposing hardship without a corresponding benefit (reward) is injustice, and without harm in abandoning it (punishment) would necessitate that supererogatory acts become obligatory since they have benefit. Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If reward and punishment are not obligatory, God could lie about them.
Correction: God's promise is true and will occur, but that does not mean it is obligatory upon Him.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation of the third argument, why does non-obligation not imply lying?
A) Because God is not truthful
B) Because occurrence does not require obligation
C) Because the promise is conditional
D) Because God can change His mind

Key Takeaway
The opponent's third argument is refuted by distinguishing between occurrence and obligation: God's promise will be fulfilled without being obligatory upon Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly trust that God will fulfill His promises to you—even when you feel unworthy—knowing that He is not obligated to do so? How does this change the way you rely on Him today?
?
When you sin, do you fear punishment as something God 'owes' you because of justice, or do you throw yourself on His mercy? Which attitude is more in line with this lesson?
?
If God is not obligated to reward you for your good deeds, why do you still do them? Are you willing to serve Him purely out of love and gratitude, with no sense of entitlement?

The Justice of Punishment and Promise vs. Threat
Al-Taftazani explains justice of punishment and promise-threat distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari school considers punishment just without being obligatory on God, and how the distinction between God's promise and threat preserves His grace.

Core Idea
God may pardon the guilty by grace, but He always fulfills His promise of reward.

This passage from al-Taftazani clarifies a key Ash'ari doctrine: God's punishment is just, but not because He is obligated to punish or because the servant deserves it. Rather, justice here means that punishment is fitting and not unjust. The real controversy is about whether God must always fulfill His threat of punishment, or whether He can pardon. Al-Taftazani explains that breaking a promise would be a deficiency, so God must reward the obedient; but breaking a threat is an act of grace, so He may forgive the disobedient.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then read the literal translation. Notice how al-Taftazani contrasts the cases of reward and punishment.
The passage has a clear logical flow from justice to promise/threat distinction.
2
Identify the key positions
Al-Taftazani states two main points: (a) punishment is just without obligation, (b) breaking a promise is impermissible for God but breaking a threat is permissible. Find where each is stated.
He also mentions that some Mu'tazilites agreed on the second point.
3
Understand the meaning of 'entitlement'
Al-Taftazani clarifies that 'not entitled' means reward/punishment is not a binding right. But he affirms that actions are causes for reward/punishment in the sense of natural consequences, as supported by Quran and consensus.
This resolves a potential misunderstanding about causality.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding: if punishment is not obligatory, perhaps God could be unjust. The passage argues that punishment is 'just' without being obligatory—justice here means appropriateness, not a debt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعقاب عدل من غير وجوب عليه ولا استحقاق من العبد خلافاً للمعتزلة، إلا أن الخلف في الوعد نقص لا يجوز أن ينسب إلى الله تعالى، فَيُثيبُ المطيع البتة إنجازاً لوعده، بخلاف الخلف في الوعيد، فإنه فضل وكرم يجوز إسناده اليه ، فيجوز ان لا يعاقب العاصي. ووافقنا في ذلك البصريون من المعتزلة وكثير من البغداديين، ومعنى كون الثواب أو العقاب غير مستحق أنه ليس حقاً لازماً يقبح تركه . وأما الاستحقاق بمعنى ترتبهما على الأفعال والتروك ، وملائمة إضافتهما إليهما في مجاري العقول والعادات فمما لا نزاع فيه. كيف وقد ورد بذلك الكتاب والسنة في مواضع لا تحصى، وأجمع السلف على أن كلاً من فعل الواجب والمندوب ينتهض سبباً للثواب . ومن فعل الحرام، وترك الواجب سبباً للعقاب، وبنوا أمر الترغيب في اكتساب الحسنات، واجتناب السيئات على إفادتهما الثواب والعقاب .
And punishment is just without obligation upon Him and without entitlement from the servant, contrary to the Mu'tazila. However, breaking a promise is a deficiency that cannot be attributed to God Almighty, so He rewards the obedient absolutely as fulfillment of His promise, unlike breaking a threat, for that is grace and generosity that may be attributed to Him, so it is permissible that He does not punish the disobedient. The Basrans among the Mu'tazila and many of the Baghdadis agreed with us on this. The meaning of reward or punishment not being entitled is that it is not a binding right whose abandonment is deemed bad. As for entitlement in the sense of their being consequent upon actions and omissions, and the appropriateness of their attribution to them in the flow of intellects and customs, there is no dispute about that. How indeed, when the Book and the Sunnah have come with that in countless places, and the Salaf agreed that each of doing an obligatory or recommended act is a cause for reward, and doing a forbidden act or omitting an obligatory act is a cause for punishment, and they based the matter of encouraging acquisition of good deeds and avoidance of evil deeds on their resulting in reward and punishment.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage clarifies the Ash'ari position on punishment and the distinction between God's promise and threat. Al-Taftazani states that punishment is just (عدل) without being obligatory upon God, and without the servant having any entitlement to it, in opposition to the Mu'tazila. However, breaking a promise (وعد) is a deficiency and cannot be attributed to God, so He will certainly reward the obedient as fulfillment of His promise. In contrast, breaking a threat (وعيد) is permissible because it is an act of grace and generosity; God may or may not punish the disobedient. Some Mu'tazilites (Basrans and many Baghdadis) agreed with this distinction. Then he clarifies the meaning of 'not entitled': it means it is not a binding right that would be morally bad to leave. But entitlement in the sense of a natural consequence (reward follows obedience, punishment follows sin) is agreed upon, as proven by countless Quranic verses, hadiths, and consensus of the Salaf. The Salaf based encouragement of good and avoidance of evil on the fact that these actions lead to reward and punishment.

Key Terms
عدل
وعد
وعيد
استحقاق
فضل
كرم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If punishment is not obligatory on God, then He could act unjustly.
Correction: The passage explains that punishment is 'just' (عدل) without being obligatory. Justice here means it is fitting and not unjust. God's pardoning sinners is an act of grace, not injustice.

Checkpoint
Why is it permissible for God not to punish a disobedient person according to this passage?
A) Because God cannot punish
B) Because breaking a threat is a deficiency
C) Because not punishing is grace and generosity
D) Because the servant has no free will

Key Takeaway
God's punishment is just without being obligatory; He must fulfill His promise of reward but may pardon by grace, and actions are natural causes for reward or punishment by divine decree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God is not obligated to reward your good deeds, what is your deepest motive for obeying Him? Does that challenge your reliance on your own righteousness?
?
When you sin, do you secretly count on God's grace to cancel the punishment, or does the fact that He may still punish you move you to repentance? How does the promise-threat distinction shape your daily choices?
?
The Salaf based encouragement of good deeds on their causing reward. Does your heart truly believe that your small acts of obedience are causes for eternal reward, or do you treat them as meaningless rituals?

The First Proof: Nothing is Obligatory Upon God
Al-Taftazani states the foundational Ash'ari principle that nothing is obligatory upon God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational Ash'ari principle that nothing is obligatory upon God and why this is the main proof in the argument about reward and punishment.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari principle: God's actions are not bound by external obligation.

In this brief but pivotal passage, al-Taftazani lays down the cornerstone of the Ash'ari argument about divine reward and punishment. He asserts that nothing is obligatory upon God, not even rewarding the obedient or punishing the disobedient. This principle must be understood before any discussion of God's justice can proceed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Taftazani calls this proof 'al-`umda' (the main one).
2
Understand 'al-`umda'
Reflect on why this proof is described as the main or foundational proof. What does it establish that subsequent arguments depend on?
3
Explore the implications
If nothing is obligatory upon God, then reward and punishment are not debts God owes. Think about how this changes the understanding of divine justice.
4
Address the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that this implies God is unjust. The passage itself does not elaborate, but it sets the foundation for why this is not the case.
The Ash'ari view holds that God's actions are just by definition, not by meeting an external standard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا وجوه : الأول - وهو العمدة ما مر أنه لا يجب على الله تعالى شيء، لا الثواب على الطاعة ولا العقاب على المعصية .
We have proofs: The first - and it is the main one - is what has preceded, that nothing is obligatory upon God Almighty, neither reward for obedience nor punishment for disobedience.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents the first and most fundamental proof (العمدة) for the Ash'ari position that reward and punishment are not obligatory upon God. It simply states the principle already established: nothing is obligatory upon God Almighty, neither reward for obedience nor punishment for disobedience. This principle underlies all subsequent arguments in this section.

Key Terms

وجوب
ثواب
عقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this means God is unjust or arbitrary.
Correction: The Ash'ari position is that God's actions are not subject to external obligation; His justice is inherent in His actions, not measured by a standard outside Himself.

Checkpoint

A) That reward is necessary for gratitude
B) That nothing is obligatory upon God
C) That punishment is a deterrent
D) That human acts cannot repay God

Key Takeaway
The core Ash'ari principle is that nothing is obligatory upon God, not even reward or punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is not obligated to reward your obedience, what then is the foundation of your hope for Paradise? Is it your own deeds or His mercy? And how does that change the way you pray and worship today?
?
Think of a moment when you felt entitled to God's forgiveness or blessings. After understanding that nothing is obligatory upon Him, how does that feeling of entitlement appear to you now? Will you consciously replace it with heartfelt gratitude and humility?
?
If God were bound by obligation, you could rely on your own merits. But since He is not, what does that demand from your relationship with Him? Are you willing to surrender your expectations and trust solely in His free generosity, even when it is difficult?

The Second Proof: Human Acts Cannot Repay God's Blessings
Al-Taftazani argues that human acts cannot repay God's blessings, so reward cannot be obligatory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Taftazani argues that human acts cannot repay God's blessings, and how he refutes the objection that obedience cannot be considered gratitude.

Core Idea
No human act can repay even a single blessing from God, so reward is not obligatory.

This lesson examines al-Taftazani's second proof against the Mu'tazilite claim that God must reward human obedience. He argues that even the most abundant acts of worship cannot repay the gratitude due for God's countless blessings. Furthermore, he anticipates and refutes a potential objection regarding the nature of gratitude and hardship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the flow from the main argument to the objection and response.
The passage uses analogies to reinforce the point.
2
Identify the core argument
The key claim: human acts cannot fulfill gratitude for God's blessings, so entitlement to reward is impossible.
Look for the phrase 'لا تفي بشكر'.
3
Examine the analogies
Al-Taftazani compares the servant's relationship to God with a servant to his master and a child to his father. Ask: what do these analogies show?
They show that no extra compensation is due beyond what is already provided.
4
Understand the objection and response
The objection claims obedience cannot be gratitude because imposing hardship requires compensation. The response shows that the obligation to thank does not imply the favor was done to impose that obligation.
The response concludes that compensation is God's grace itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - أن طاعات العبد - وإن كثرت - لا تفي بشكر بعض ما أنعم الله عليه. فكيف يتصور استحقاق عوض عليها . ولو استحق العبد بشكره الواجب عوضاً، لاستحق الرب على ما يوليه من الثواب عوضاً، وكذا العبد على خدمته لسيده الذي يقوم بمؤونته ، وإزاحة علله ، والولد على خدمته لأبيه الذي يربيه، وعلى مراعاته، وتوخي مرضاته لا يقال : لا يجوز أن تكون الطاعة شكراً للنعمة، لأن العقلاء يستقبحون الإحسان إلى الغير لتكليفه الشكر ، ولأن الشكر يتصور بدون تكليف المشاق والمضاد كشكر أهل الجنة، فلا بد لتكليف المشاق من عوض ليخرج عن العبث ، لأنا نقول بعد تسليم قاعدة الحسن و القبح، ولزوم العوض، وقبح الإحسان لتكليف الشكر . فوجوب الشكر على الإحسان لا يوجب كون الإحسان لأجله حتى يقبح ، وكون تكليف المشاق لغرض لا يوجب كونه لغرض. ولو سلم لكفى بترتب التفضل عليه عوضاً.
Second - That the servant's acts of obedience, even if numerous, do not fulfill the gratitude for some of what God has bestowed upon him. So how can entitlement to a compensation be imagined? And if the servant were entitled to compensation for his obligatory gratitude, then the Lord would be entitled to compensation for what He bestows of reward, and likewise the servant for his service to his master who provides for him and removes his ailments, and the child for his service to his father who raises him, for his care and seeking his pleasure. It cannot be said: It is not permissible for obedience to be gratitude for the blessing, because the rational consider it ugly to do good to someone in order to impose gratitude on him, and because gratitude can be conceived without imposing hardships and difficulties like the gratitude of the people of Paradise, so imposing hardships requires compensation to avoid being futile. For we say after conceding the basis of good and evil, the necessity of compensation, and the ugliness of doing good for the purpose of imposing gratitude: The obligation of gratitude for a favor does not require that the favor be done for that purpose so that it would be ugly, and the imposition of hardships for a purpose does not require that it be for that purpose. Even if conceded, it would suffice that the compensation is the bestowal of favor.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against obligatory reward. It argues that even the many acts of obedience a servant performs cannot repay gratitude for even a portion of God's blessings. Therefore, how could one imagine entitlement to compensation? Furthermore, if the servant were entitled to compensation for his obligatory gratitude, then God would also be entitled to compensation for giving reward, leading to an infinite regress. Analogies are given: a servant serving a master who provides for him, or a child serving a father who raises him, do not entitle them to compensation beyond what they already receive. Then the passage anticipates an objection: some might say that obedience cannot be considered gratitude because rational people consider it repugnant to do good to someone with the intention of imposing gratitude, and because gratitude is possible without hardship (like the gratitude of Paradise). Thus, imposing hardship requires compensation to avoid futility. The refutation (قلنا) responds: even if we accept the basis of good and evil, the necessity of compensation, and the ugliness of doing good for the sake of imposing gratitude, the obligation to show gratitude for a favor does not imply that the favor was done for that purpose. Similarly, imposing hardship for a purpose does not mean it must be for that specific purpose. And even if one concedes, the compensation is the bestowal of favor itself (i.e., God's grace).

Key Terms
طاعة
شكر
نعمة
عوض
حسن وقبح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that human acts of obedience are so valuable that they repay God's blessings and therefore God owes a reward.
Correction: The proof shows that even numerous acts cannot repay even a single blessing, so no entitlement to reward exists.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, why is it impossible for the servant to be entitled to reward for his acts?
A) Because God does not reward anyone
B) Because the servant's acts are too few to repay even one blessing
C) Because the servant does not truly obey
D) Because reward is only for prophets

Key Takeaway
Human acts cannot repay even one of God's infinite blessings; therefore, reward is entirely a matter of God's grace, not obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one blessing you received today that you normally take for granted. Given that your best acts cannot repay even that single blessing, what does that do to your sense of entitlement before God? Will you now approach your next prayer not as a transaction but as a humble acknowledgment of your utter poverty before Him?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you secretly feel that God 'owes' you something in return? This passage demolishes that assumption. What would change in your daily worship if you truly believed that no act of yours could ever put God in your debt?
?
If the compensation for your obedience is not a wage but a pure gift from God, how does that reshape the way you seek His pleasure? Are you willing to serve Him without expecting anything in return, trusting that whatever He gives is sheer grace?

The Conditional Argument Against Obligatory Reward
Al-Taftazani begins a conditional proof against obligatory reward/punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditional argument that if reward and punishment were obligatory through entitlement, absurd consequences would follow, specifically involving a lifelong believer who apostatizes or a lifelong disbeliever who repents.

Core Idea
If reward were obligatory, a lifelong believer who apostatizes would still be rewarded — an impossible outcome.

Al-Taftazani continues his refutation of obligatory reward and punishment with a conditional proof. He asks us to consider what would necessarily follow if reward were based on strict cause-and-effect entitlement. The result would be absurd — someone who obeys all his life but apostatizes at the end would still be rewarded, and a lifelong disbeliever who sincerely repents would still be punished. This passage presents the logical consequences that make the premise untenable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the conditional structure beginning with 'if reward and punishment were obligatory... followed by 'it would necessitate that...'.
The passage ends abruptly; the conclusion is not given here.
2
Identify the two hypothetical cases
The passage presents two scenarios: (1) a lifelong obedient person who apostatizes, and (2) a lifelong disbeliever who repents. What would the obligatory reward/punishment model require in each case?
The first case yields reward, the second yields punishment.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The argument relies on the idea that if reward is obligatory 'through entitlement' and 'the effect follows the cause', then the cause (obedience / disbelief) must inevitably produce the effect (reward / punishment) regardless of later changes.
This is what the kalam scholars call 'cause-effect determinism'.
4
Reflect on the incompleteness
The passage cuts off before stating the conclusion. Based on the argument, what conclusion do you think follows? How does this refute the idea of obligatory reward?
The conclusion would be that since the consequences are absurd, reward and punishment cannot be obligatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث - أنه لو وجب الثواب والعقاب بطريق الاستحقاق، وترتب المسبب على السبب. لزم أن يثاب من واظب طول عمره على الطاعات وارتد - نعوذ بالله تعالى - في آخر الحياة، وأن يعاقب من أصر دهراً على كفره وتبرأ وأخلص
Third - That if reward and punishment were obligatory through entitlement, and the effect follows the cause, it would necessitate that one who consistently performed acts of obedience all his life and then apostatized - we seek refuge with God Almighty - at the end of life would be rewarded, and that one who persisted for ages in his disbelief and then repented and became sincere
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage begins the third proof against obligatory reward and punishment. It argues by conditional reasoning: if reward and punishment were obligatory (بِطريق الاستحقاق) such that the effect (reward/punishment) necessarily follows the cause (obedience/disobedience), then a person who obeys all his life but then apostatizes at the end would still be rewarded for his previous acts, and a person who persists in disbelief for a long time but then sincerely repents would still be punished for his past sins. This consequence is considered untenable because apostasy nullifies previous good deeds, and repentance erases previous sins. However, the argument is cut off mid-sentence, lacking the conclusion. The lesson is incomplete.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the argument denies any form of reward or punishment, or that it suggests actions have no consequences.
Correction: The argument only denies that reward/punishment are obligatory by entitlement. It does not deny that Allah may reward or punish by His will and grace.

Checkpoint

A) They are obligatory but only for the final state
B) They cannot be obligatory through entitlement because absurd consequences follow
C) They are necessary for justice
D) They are determined by the first and last actions

Key Takeaway
The conditional argument refutes obligatory reward/punishment by showing it would lead to absurd outcomes like rewarding a lifelong believer who apostatizes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If your good deeds do not obligate Allah to reward you, then what do you truly rely on for salvation? Does that make you feel vulnerable or free? How would your daily prayers change if you remembered that they don't 'earn' you anything?
?
The argument shows that a lifetime of obedience does not guarantee reward if the final state is bad. Do you fear your final state? What can you do today to ensure your faith endures until the very last breath?

Second Objection: Without Obligatory Reward, People Would Neglect Devotion
Sanusi presents and refutes the argument that without necessary reward, people would become lazy in worship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the necessity of reward and punishment, and how Sanusi refutes it by appealing to God's promises and the predominant certainty of their fulfillment.

Core Idea
God's promises alone, even if not obligatory, suffice to motivate sincere worship.

In the preceding lesson, Sanusi argued that reward and punishment are not rationally necessary for God. Here he addresses a practical objection: without guaranteed reward, wouldn't people become lazy in worship? He presents the objection and then refutes it by pointing to the comprehensive nature of divine promises and the weight of religious testimony.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the structure: first the objection, then the refutation starting with 'ورد بأن'.
Pay attention to the contrast between the two parts.
2
Understand the objection's logic
The objection claims that without obligatory reward, people will neglect worship because it is hard. Only certainty of benefit motivates the soul.
This is a pragmatic argument based on human psychology.
3
Analyze the refutation
Sanusi responds that God's comprehensive promise and threat, along with predominant certainty from many reports, are sufficient. The mere possibility of non-fulfillment does not undermine this.
Notice the terms: شمول الوعد والوعيد, غلبة ظن الوفاء, كثرة الأخبار والآثار.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - أنه لو لم يجب الثواب والعقاب لأفضى ذلك إلى التواني في الطاعات، والاجتراء على المعاصي، لأن الطاعات مشاق ومخالفات للهوى، لا تميل إليها النفس إلا بعد القطع بلذات ومنافع تربى عليها . ورد بأن شمول الوعد والوعيد للكل وغلبة ظن الوفاء بهما، وكثرة الأخبار والآثار في ذلك كاف في الترغيب والترهيب ومجرد جواز الترك غير قادح .
The second: If reward and punishment were not obligatory, this would lead to negligence (tawani) in acts of obedience and audacity in committing sins, because acts of obedience are hardships and contrary to desires, and the soul does not lean towards them except after being certain of pleasures and benefits that exceed them. And it was refuted by [saying] that the comprehensiveness of the promise (wa'd) and threat (wa'id) for all, and the predominant certainty of their fulfillment, and the abundance of reports (akhbar) and traditions (athar) regarding that, are sufficient for encouragement (targhib) and deterrence (tarhib), and the mere possibility of leaving [them] does not impair [this].
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection from the opponents: pragmatic necessity. They argue that if reward and punishment were not guaranteed, people would become negligent in worship and bold in sin, because worship is hard and contrary to desires; only the certainty of benefit motivates. Sanusi's refutation is that the comprehensiveness of God's promises and threats, the predominant certainty that they will be fulfilled, and the many religious texts are sufficient to encourage and deter. The mere logical possibility that God might not fulfill them (since no obligation) does not undermine this practical certainty.

Key Terms
وعد
وعيد
ترغيب
ترهيب
تواني
ظن غالب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sanusi's refutation relies on philosophical optimism about human nature.
Correction: His refutation is based on the trustworthiness of God's revelation and the abundance of religious reports, not on an optimistic view of human nature.

Checkpoint
What is Sanusi's response to the claim that without obligatory reward people would neglect worship?
A) He says people naturally incline to worship regardless.
B) He says God's promises and threats are sufficient to motivate.
C) He says reward is still likely even if not obligatory.
D) He says punishment deters completely.

Key Takeaway
God's promise and threat, supported by abundant revelation, are sufficient to encourage obedience and deter sin, even without rational obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform acts of worship that are difficult, like praying at dawn or giving charity, is your hope of Paradise and fear of Hell truly the driving force, or do other motives like social approval or habit play a larger role? What would change if you took God's promise and threat as your sole motivation?
?
Consider a sin you find yourself repeatedly committing. Do you truly believe that God will punish it, or does a small doubt allow you to become audacious? How can you strengthen the predominant certainty of His threat in your heart?
?
If God had not revealed His promise and threat in the Qur'an, would you still be motivated to obey Him purely out of love and gratitude? Be honest: does the fear of punishment or hope of reward dominate your worship, and is that a problem?

The Ash'ari Position on Sinful Believers
Author states Ash'ari position and contrasts with other schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position on the fate of sinful believers and how it differs from the Mu'tazila and Murji'a.

Core Idea
Ash'aris affirm divine will but deny eternal punishment for sinful believers.

The fate of sinful believers is a major point of contention in Islamic theology. In this passage, the author lays out the Ash'ari position clearly, distinguishing it from the extreme views of the Mu'tazila who affirm eternal punishment and some Murji'a who deny any punishment. This lesson establishes the Ash'ari stance as one of hope without presumption.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the precise wording of the Ash'ari position.
Notice the phrase 'لا يخلد في النار' – he will not be eternally in the Fire.
2
Identify the three positions
Find the Ash'ari, Mu'tazila, and Murji'a positions in the text. Note how each is described.
The Mu'tazila are certain of eternal stay; the Murji'a deny any punishment.
3
Reflect on the middle path
Consider why the Ash'ari position avoids extremes and balances divine will with justice and mercy.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm your grasp of the Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
توبة والمذهب عندنا عدم القطع بالعفو أو العقاب، بل إن شاء الله عفا، وإن شاء عذب، لكن لا يخلد في النار. وعند المعتزلة القطع بالخلود في النار. ولا عبر لقول مقاتل . ولبعض المرجئة (١) : إن عصاة المؤمنين لا يعذبون أصلاً ، وإنما النار للكفار لنا وجوه . الأول - النصوص الدالة على دخول المؤمنين الجنة وليس قبل دخول النار وفاقاً، بل بعده أو بدونه الثاني - النصوص الدالة على خروجهم من النار. الثالث - أن من واظب على الطاعات مائة سنة وشرب جرعة من الخمر، فلو لم يكن تخليده في النار ظلماً عندكم، فلا ظلم . الرابع - أن المعصية متناهية زماناً وقدراً ، فجزاؤها كذلك تحقيقاً للعدل. الخامس أن استحقاقه الثواب وعداً أو عقلاً لا يزول بالكبيرة لما سيأتي، ولا يتصور إلا بالخروج من النار.
[The topic of] repentance? and our school is [that] we do not assert certainty of pardon or punishment, but if God wills He pardons, and if He wills He punishes, but he will not be eternally in the Fire. And with the Mu'tazila is the certainty of eternal stay in the Fire. And no regard is given to the statement of Muqatil. And some of the Murji'a: that sinful believers are not punished at all, and the Fire is only for the disbelievers. For us are proofs. First: the texts indicating the entry of believers into Paradise and not before entering the Fire by agreement, but after it or without it. Second: the texts indicating their exit from the Fire. Third: that one who persevered in acts of obedience for a hundred years and drank a gulp of wine - if his eternal stay in the Fire were not unjust in your view, then there is no injustice. Fourth: that the sin is finite in time and magnitude, so its punishment is likewise, as a realization of justice. Fifth: that his entitlement to reward by promise or reason does not cease due to the major sin, according to what will come, and it is not conceivable except by exiting the Fire.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari position on the fate of sinful believers (fuṣṣāq). The author begins by stating the school's position: we do not assert certainty regarding God's pardon or punishment; rather, it is up to God's will, but the sinful believer will not be eternally in Hell. This is contrasted with the Mu'tazila who affirm eternal punishment, and with some Murji'a who deny any punishment at all for sinful believers. The author then presents five proofs for the Ash'ari position. The first proof appeals to scriptural texts that indicate believers will enter Paradise, even if after some punishment. The second proof cites texts showing their exit from Hell. The third is an argument from fairness: punishing a finite sin with eternal punishment would be unjust. The fourth proof argues that since sin is finite in time and degree, its punishment must be finite for justice to be realized. The fifth proof states that the entitlement to reward (by promise or reason) is not nullified by committing a major sin, and this can only be realized by exiting Hell. The author implies that further details will follow.

Key Terms
خلود
فاسق
معتزلة
مرجئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'aris are certain that all sinful believers will be forgiven.
Correction: The text explicitly says 'لا قطع بالعفو أو العقاب' – there is no certainty of pardon or punishment; it is up to God's will.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari position on eternal punishment for sinful believers?
A) They will certainly be punished eternally.
B) They will certainly be pardoned.
C) They may be punished but not eternally.
D) They will be punished like disbelievers.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position holds that sinful believers are not eternally punished, though their ultimate fate is subject to God's will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own sins, do you secretly lean more towards despair (like the Mu'tazila) or presumption (like the Murji'a)? How does the Ash'ari position force you to hold both hope and fear without either extreme?
?
If you truly believed that your ultimate fate is left to God's will—not guaranteed by any deed—would your daily choices change? What would you do differently today if you lived in that uncertainty?
?
You claim to trust God's mercy, but do you actually live as if you might be punished? Or do you assume leniency? What single action today can you take that reflects the Ash'ari balance of fear and hope?

Five Proofs for Finite Punishment
Author enumerates five proofs for the Ash'ari position on sinful believers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five rational and scriptural proofs that the Ash'aris use to argue that sinful believers will not be eternally punished.

Core Idea
Sin is finite, so its punishment cannot be infinite without injustice.

After establishing the Ash'ari position, the author now provides five proofs to support it. These proofs range from scriptural evidence to rational arguments about justice and the nature of sin. This lesson walks through each proof and explains its logical force.

Guided Path
1
Read the five proofs
Read the Arabic text and translation of the five proofs. Note the numbering.
Each proof begins with 'الأول', 'الثاني', etc.
2
Analyze each proof
For each proof, identify the main argument and how it supports non-eternal punishment.
The third proof is a reductio ad absurdum targeting the Mu'tazila.
3
Connect proofs to position
Consider how these proofs together strengthen the Ash'ari stance against eternal punishment.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm your grasp of one of the proofs.

Source Text

Mu'tazila Proof and Ash'ari Concession-Exclusion Analogy
Mu'tazila cite universal threat verses; Ash'ari answers with concession and analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari response begins by conceding the generality of threat verses but then uses the existing exceptions (repentant, minor sins) to exclude major sins by analogy.

Core Idea
Threat verses are not absolute; they already exclude repentant and minor sins, so major sins can also be excluded.

The Mu'tazila argue that Qur'anic verses threatening eternal punishment apply universally to all sinners. The Ash'ari response does not deny these verses but instead examines their scope. This lesson covers the first part of the response: conceding generality but showing that exceptions already exist, so the verses cannot be used to prove eternal punishment for all major sinners.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila proof
Read the Arabic verses cited by the Mu'tazila and their translation. Notice the universal language used in each verse.
Verses include threats of eternal stay (khulūd) in Hell.
2
Study the Ash'ari response
Read the response that begins 'والجواب...' The author first concedes the generality of these formulations (عموم الصيغ).
Concession is a rhetorical strategy to build common ground.
3
Identify the exceptions
The response notes that the verses already exclude two groups: the repentant (التائب) and those with minor sins (صاحب الصغائر). This means the verses are not absolute.
These exceptions are agreed upon (وفاقاً) by both sides.
4
Understand the analogy
The author then argues: if these exceptions exist, why cannot a major sinner also be excluded? The verse's generality is broken, so it does not prove eternal punishment for every sinner.
This is a logical move: if A does not apply to B and C, it may not apply to D either.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجت المعتزلة بوجوه : الأول - عمومات الوعيد بالخلود ومن يعص الله ورسوله فإن له نار جهنم خالداً فيها ) (٢) ومن يقتل مؤمناً متعمداً فجزاؤه جهنم خالداً فيها) (٣) وأما الذين فسقوا فمأواهم النار كلما ارادوا أن يخرجوا منها أعيدوا فيها (٤) وإن الفجار لفي جحيم يصلونها يوم الدين وما هم عنها بغائبين (٥) ومن يعص الله ورسوله ويتعد حدوده يدخله ناراً خالداً فيها) (١) بلى من كسب سيئة وأحاطت به خطيئته فأولئك أصحاب النار هم فيها خالدون) (٢) والجواب بعد تسليم عموم الصيغ أنه قد أخرج من الأول التائب. وصاحب الصغائر، فلم تبق قطعية ،وفاقاً، فليخرج منها مرتكب الكبيرة أيضاً. على أن الاستحقاق فيها مغياً بغاية رؤية العذاب لقوله تعالى: (حتى إذا رأوا ما يوعدون) (٣) ولو سلم فاستحقاق العذاب المؤبد لا يوجب وقوعه، وأن معنى متعمداً مستحلاً قبله على ما فسره ابن عباس (رضي الله عنه أو المراد بالخلود المكث الطويل جمعاً بين الأدلة، وأن المراد بالذين فسقوا الكفار المنكرون للحشر، بقرينة قوله تعالى : ( ذوقوا عذاب النار التي كنتم بها تكذبون ) (٤) والباقي مختصة بالكفار جمعاً بين الأدلة، أو المراد بعدم غيبتهم سلب العموم أو المبالغة في المكث، وكذا الخلود، والمراد تعدي حدود الإسلام وإحاطة الخطية بحيث لا يبقى الإيمان.
The Mu'tazila argued with proofs: First - the generalities of the threat of eternal stay: 'And whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger, then indeed for him is the Fire of Hell, abiding eternally therein' (Quran 72:23); 'And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell, abiding eternally therein' (Quran 4:93); 'And as for those who are defiantly disobedient, their refuge is the Fire; every time they wish to emerge from it, they are returned to it' (Quran 32:20); 'And indeed the wicked are in Hellfire; they will burn on the Day of Recompense, and they will not be absent from it' (Quran 82:14-16); 'And whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger and transgresses His limits, He will put him into Fire, abiding eternally therein' (Quran 4:14); 'Yes, whoever earns evil and his sin has encompassed him – those are the companions of the Fire; they will abide therein eternally' (Quran 2:81). And the answer: after conceding the generality of the formulations, that from the first has been excluded the repentant one and the one of minor sins, so they are no longer certain, by agreement; so let the perpetrator of a major sin also be excluded from it. Moreover, the entitlement therein is conditioned by the sight of punishment according to His saying: 'Until when they see what they are promised' (Quran 19:75). Even if conceded, the entitlement to eternal punishment does not necessitate its occurrence; and that the meaning of 'intentionally' is [as] considering it permissible before it, according to the interpretation of Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him); or that the intended meaning of 'eternal stay' is a long stay, as a reconciliation between evidences; and that the intended meaning of 'those who are defiantly disobedient' (alladhīna fasaqū) are the disbelievers who deny the resurrection, by the context of His saying: 'Taste the punishment of the Fire which you used to deny' (Quran 34:42); and the remaining [verses] are specific to disbelievers by way of reconciliation between evidences; or that the intended meaning of their not being absent from it is negation of universality or exaggeration in staying, and likewise eternity; and the intended meaning of 'transgressing the limits of Islam' and 'encompassing of sin' is such that faith does not remain.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof of the Mu'tazila and the author's detailed response. The Mu'tazila cite several Qur'anic verses that threaten eternal punishment for sinners, including for those who disobey Allah and His Messenger, kill a believer intentionally, are defiantly disobedient, are wicked, and so on. The author's answer begins by granting the generality of these formulations but then restricts them in multiple ways. First, the verses already exclude the repentant and those who commit minor sins; thus they are not absolute, and by analogy the perpetrator of a major sin can also be excluded. Second, the entitlement to punishment is conditioned upon the actual sight of punishment (i.e., it may cease afterward). Third, even if entitlement to eternal punishment is conceded, it does not necessitate actual occurrence. Fourth, the word 'intentionally' (mutʿamiddan) is interpreted by Ibn Abbas as meaning considering it lawful beforehand, which restricts the scope. Fifth, the term 'khulūd' (eternity) may mean a long stay, not absolute eternity, to reconcile conflicting evidences. Sixth, the phrase 'those who are defiantly disobedient' is interpreted as referring specifically to disbelievers who deny the resurrection, based on contextual evidence. Seventh, the remaining verses are said to be specific to disbelievers when all evidences are reconciled. Eighth, the negation of being 'absent' from Fire may be a negation of universality or an exaggeration. The author concludes by reinterpreting 'transgressing the limits of Islam' and 'encompassing of sin' as conditions where faith no longer remains. These multiple interpretations and restrictions neutralize the Mu'tazila's proof.

Key Terms
عمومات الوعيد
خلود
تائب
صغائر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila's verses are unanswerable and conclusively prove eternal punishment for all major sinners.
Correction: The Ash'ari response shows that even accepting the verses' generality, existing exceptions (repentant, minor sins) prevent them from being used as a universal proof.

Checkpoint
What is the first step in the Ash'ari response to the Mu'tazila's proof?
A) Denying the authenticity of the verses.
B) Conceding the generality of the verses but noting that they exclude the repentant and those with minor sins.
C) Arguing that the verses refer only to disbelievers.
D) Claiming that 'eternal' means a long stay.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's threat verses are not absolute; they already have exceptions, so they cannot prove eternal punishment for all major sinners without further evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses like 'whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger, then indeed for him is the Fire of Hell, abiding eternally therein,' do you immediately apply that to yourself as a warning, or do you assume it applies to others? After this lesson, how will you check your heart when you encounter such verses to avoid judging others without considering the exceptions? Commit to one specific change in how you read threat verses.
?
The Ash'ari response relies on an analogy: if repentant and minor sinners are excluded by agreement, then major sinners can also be excluded. Do you truly believe that God's mercy can cover even your major sins, or do you secretly think some sins are too great? How will you balance fear and hope of Allah in your daily life based on the lesson's point that threat verses are not absolute?

Conditional Entitlement and Necessity vs. Actuality
Ash'ari adds conditional entitlement and non-necessity of actual punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two further Ash'ari responses: that entitlement to eternal punishment is conditioned on the sight of punishment, and that entitlement does not necessitate actual occurrence.

Core Idea
Even if a sinner is entitled to eternal punishment, it may not actually happen, and it is conditioned on seeing punishment.

After establishing that the threat verses are not absolute, the Ash'ari author offers two more sophisticated arguments. First, the Quran itself ties the punishment to the moment when sinners see what they are promised, implying a temporary stay. Second, even if the verses prove entitlement to eternal punishment, that does not mean Allah must actually inflict it. This lesson explores these nuanced points.

Guided Path
1
Read the two arguments
Read the Arabic and translation of the second part of the response. It contains two distinct points introduced by 'على أن' and 'ولو سلم'.
Notice the shift from concession to conditional reasoning.
2
Understand the first point: conditional entitlement
The author cites a verse that conditions punishment on actually seeing it. This implies that once the sinner sees the punishment, it may end.
Verse reference: Quran 19:75 'حتى إذا رأوا ما يوعدون'.
3
Understand the second point: entitlement does not necessitate actuality
Even if the verses show that a sinner deserves eternal punishment (استحقاق), it does not mean that Allah will carry it out. Forgiveness and mercy are always possible.
This distinguishes between hypothetical entitlement and actual event.
4
Connect to broader Ash'ari theology
These points reflect Ash'ari emphasis on Allah's will and mercy being unrestricted, even when justice demands punishment.
Punishment is always subject to Allah's discretion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

These multiple interpretations and restrictions neutralize the Mu'tazila's proof.

استحقاق
رؤية العذاب
موعود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if a verse says a sinner 'deserves' eternal punishment, then Allah must punish them eternally.
Correction: The Ash'ari response clarifies that entitlement (استحقاق) does not create a necessity for Allah; He may forgive or mitigate the punishment.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does the verse 'حتى إذا رأوا ما يوعدون' imply?
A) Punishment will start after seeing the promise.
B) Entitlement to punishment ends when the sinner sees it.
C) The verse is not relevant to the argument.
D) Punishment is unconditional.

Key Takeaway
Even if threat verses indicate entitlement to eternal punishment, this entitlement is conditioned and does not obligate Allah to actually inflict it.

?

?

?
If you are certain that your sins entitle you to eternal punishment, but also that God may not actualize it, how does that affect your repentance and your relationship with Him today?

Further Interpretations: Lexical and Contextual Restrictions
Ash'ari offers lexical and contextual reinterpretations to restrict threat verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the remaining Ash'ari responses: reinterpretation of key terms (intentionally, eternal, defiantly disobedient) and contextual specification of verses to disbelievers.

Core Idea
Key terms in the threat verses can be reinterpreted or specified to limit their scope to disbelievers only.

The Ash'ari author continues his response with multiple interpretative strategies. He reexamines the meaning of 'intentionally' (متعمداً), suggests that 'eternal' (خلود) may mean a long stay, and argues that the verse about 'those who are defiantly disobedient' refers to disbelievers who deny the resurrection. These moves aim to show that the verses do not apply to believing sinners.

Guided Path
1
Read the multiple interpretations
Read the remaining Arabic passage. It contains several separate points, each introduced by 'أن' or 'أو'.
The author offers multiple possible interpretations, not all necessarily his own view, but as possible reconciliations.
2
Understand the reinterpretation of 'intentionally'
The author reports that Ibn Abbas interpreted 'intentionally' (متعمداً) in the murder verse as meaning considering murder lawful beforehand, which is essentially disbelief.
This narrows the verse to disbelievers.
3
Understand the reinterpretation of 'eternal'
The term 'khulūd' (خلود) can mean a long duration, not necessarily absolute eternity. This reconciles conflicting evidence.
This is a lexical move to avoid literalism.
4
Understand the specification to disbelievers
The author argues that the phrase 'الذين فسقوا' (those who are defiantly disobedient) refers specifically to disbelievers who deny the resurrection, based on the context of a following verse.
Contextual evidence (قرينة) is used.
5
See the final overarching reinterpretation
Finally, the author suggests that 'transgressing limits' and 'encompassing sin' mean losing faith entirely, so the verses apply only to those who lose faith.
This ties back to the idea that only disbelievers are threatened.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجت المعتزلة بوجوه : الأول - عمومات الوعيد بالخلود ومن يعص الله ورسوله فإن له نار جهنم خالداً فيها ) (٢) ومن يقتل مؤمناً متعمداً فجزاؤه جهنم خالداً فيها) (٣) وأما الذين فسقوا فمأواهم النار كلما ارادوا أن يخرجوا منها أعيدوا فيها (٤) وإن الفجار لفي جحيم يصلونها يوم الدين وما هم عنها بغائبين (٥) ومن يعص الله ورسوله ويتعد حدوده يدخله ناراً خالداً فيها) (١) بلى من كسب سيئة وأحاطت به خطيئته فأولئك أصحاب النار هم فيها خالدون) (٢) والجواب بعد تسليم عموم الصيغ أنه قد أخرج من الأول التائب. وصاحب الصغائر، فلم تبق قطعية ،وفاقاً، فليخرج منها مرتكب الكبيرة أيضاً. على أن الاستحقاق فيها مغياً بغاية رؤية العذاب لقوله تعالى: (حتى إذا رأوا ما يوعدون) (٣) ولو سلم فاستحقاق العذاب المؤبد لا يوجب وقوعه، وأن معنى متعمداً مستحلاً قبله على ما فسره ابن عباس (رضي الله عنه أو المراد بالخلود المكث الطويل جمعاً بين الأدلة، وأن المراد بالذين فسقوا الكفار المنكرون للحشر، بقرينة قوله تعالى : ( ذوقوا عذاب النار التي كنتم بها تكذبون ) (٤) والباقي مختصة بالكفار جمعاً بين الأدلة، أو المراد بعدم غيبتهم سلب العموم أو المبالغة في المكث، وكذا الخلود، والمراد تعدي حدود الإسلام وإحاطة الخطية بحيث لا يبقى الإيمان.
The Mu'tazila argued with proofs: First - the generalities of the threat of eternal stay: 'And whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger, then indeed for him is the Fire of Hell, abiding eternally therein' (Quran 72:23); 'And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell, abiding eternally therein' (Quran 4:93); 'And as for those who are defiantly disobedient, their refuge is the Fire; every time they wish to emerge from it, they are returned to it' (Quran 32:20); 'And indeed the wicked are in Hellfire; they will burn on the Day of Recompense, and they will not be absent from it' (Quran 82:14-16); 'And whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger and transgresses His limits, He will put him into Fire, abiding eternally therein' (Quran 4:14); 'Yes, whoever earns evil and his sin has encompassed him – those are the companions of the Fire; they will abide therein eternally' (Quran 2:81). And the answer: after conceding the generality of the formulations, that from the first has been excluded the repentant one and the one of minor sins, so they are no longer certain, by agreement; so let the perpetrator of a major sin also be excluded from it. Moreover, the entitlement therein is conditioned by the sight of punishment according to His saying: 'Until when they see what they are promised' (Quran 19:75). Even if conceded, the entitlement to eternal punishment does not necessitate its occurrence; and that the meaning of 'intentionally' is [as] considering it permissible before it, according to the interpretation of Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him); or that the intended meaning of 'eternal stay' is a long stay, as a reconciliation between evidences; and that the intended meaning of 'those who are defiantly disobedient' (alladhīna fasaqū) are the disbelievers who deny the resurrection, by the context of His saying: 'Taste the punishment of the Fire which you used to deny' (Quran 34:42); and the remaining [verses] are specific to disbelievers by way of reconciliation between evidences; or that the intended meaning of their not being absent from it is negation of universality or exaggeration in staying, and likewise eternity; and the intended meaning of 'transgressing the limits of Islam' and 'encompassing of sin' is such that faith does not remain.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof of the Mu'tazila and the author's detailed response. The Mu'tazila cite several Qur'anic verses that threaten eternal punishment for sinners, including for those who disobey Allah and His Messenger, kill a believer intentionally, are defiantly disobedient, are wicked, and so on. The author's answer begins by granting the generality of these formulations but then restricts them in multiple ways. First, the verses already exclude the repentant and those who commit minor sins; thus they are not absolute, and by analogy the perpetrator of a major sin can also be excluded. Second, the entitlement to punishment is conditioned upon the actual sight of punishment (i.e., it may cease afterward). Third, even if entitlement to eternal punishment is conceded, it does not necessitate actual occurrence. Fourth, the word 'intentionally' (mutʿamiddan) is interpreted by Ibn Abbas as meaning considering it lawful beforehand, which restricts the scope. Fifth, the term 'khulūd' (eternity) may mean a long stay, not absolute eternity, to reconcile conflicting evidences. Sixth, the phrase 'those who are defiantly disobedient' is interpreted as referring specifically to disbelievers who deny the resurrection, based on contextual evidence. Seventh, the remaining verses are said to be specific to disbelievers when all evidences are reconciled. Eighth, the negation of being 'absent' from Fire may be a negation of universality or an exaggeration. The author concludes by reinterpreting 'transgressing the limits of Islam' and 'encompassing of sin' as conditions where faith no longer remains. These multiple interpretations and restrictions neutralize the Mu'tazila's proof.
