مستحل
ابن عباس
فسق
المكث الطويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the threat verses must be interpreted literally and universally, leaving no room for mercy.
Correction: The Ash'ari response shows that through lexical and contextual analysis, these verses can be limited to disbelievers only.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response, what does Ibn Abbas's interpretation of 'intentionally' (متعمداً) in the murder verse imply?
A) It applies to any intentional act.
B) It applies only if the murderer considered the act lawful beforehand.
C) It applies only to disbelievers who deny the resurrection.
D) It is not relevant to the argument.

Key Takeaway
By reinterpreting key terms and using contextual clues, the Ash'ari response shows that the threat verses primarily target disbelievers, not believing sinners.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you read a warning verse about hellfire, do you instinctively assume it is for 'other people'? What does that assumption reveal about your heart's posture toward Allah's warnings?

Mu'tazila's Second Proof: Merit, Nullification, and the Ash'ari Answer
Mu'tazila argument and Ash'ari answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's argument that sin nullifies merit, and the Ash'ari response denying both premises.

Core Idea
Paradise does not require merit; God may grant it by grace.

The Mu'tazila argued that a sinful believer cannot enter Paradise because entry requires merit, and sin cancels merit. However, the Ash'ari school rejects both the necessity of merit and the automatic nullification of deeds by sin. This passage presents that key debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
This is a very concise argument; note the structure of premise-conclusion and the response.
2
Identify the two premises of the Mu'tazila argument
The passage states that if a sinner entered Paradise, it would be by merit. Then it says that merit is negated by nullification or balancing. These are the two premises: entry requires merit, and sin negates merit.
The Mu'tazila conclude that the sinner cannot enter Paradise.
3
Understand the Ash'ari response
The author's answer is to deny both premises. Reflect on what it means to deny that entry requires merit and that sin necessarily nullifies merit.
This is a fundamental difference between Mu'tazila and Ash'ari theology.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider how this argument relates to the broader debate about faith and works. Why does it matter whether Paradise is by merit or grace?
This connects to the Islamic concept of God's mercy and justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - أن الفاسق لو دخل الجنة لكان باستحقاق، وقد انتفى بالإحباط أو الموازنة على ما سيجيء . والجواب - منع المقدمتين .
Second - that if the sinner entered Paradise, it would be by merit, and it [merit] has been negated by [the doctrine of] nullification (iḥbāṭ) or balancing (muwāzana), according to what will come. And the answer - denial of both premises.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's second proof. They argue that if a sinful believer (fāsiq) were to enter Paradise, it would have to be by merit (istiḥqāq). However, that merit is negated either by the doctrine of iḥbāṭ (nullification of good deeds by major sins) or by muwāzana (balancing of good and bad deeds), which will be discussed later. Therefore, the sinner cannot enter Paradise. The author's answer is a simple denial of both premises: first, that entry into Paradise requires merit (it could be by God's grace), and second, that merit is necessarily nullified by sin. This refutes the proof by rejecting its foundations.

Key Terms
استحقاق
إحباط
موازنة
فاسق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that entering Paradise always requires merit, and sin always nullifies good deeds.
Correction: Ash'aris hold that God may admit believers by His grace even if their good deeds are outweighed by sins, and sin does not necessarily nullify all merit.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazila's second proof regarding the sinful believer entering Paradise?
A) The sinner cannot enter Paradise because he has no faith.
B) The sinner would need to have merit, but that merit is nullified by sin, so he cannot enter.
C) The sinner will be punished eternally because of his sin.
D) The sinner will be purified in Hell before entering Paradise.

Key Takeaway
Mu'tazila's second proof fails because neither merit is required for Paradise nor is it automatically nullified by sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Consider the Ash'ari denial that merit is automatically nullified by sin. Does this make you feel more secure in God's mercy, or does it tempt you to take your sins lightly? How will you guard against both despair and presumption?

Refuting the Mu'tazila's Analogy Between Sinner and Disbeliever
Mu'tazila proof and Ash'ari threefold refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari answer denies causality, finitude of disbelief, and the validity of analogy.

Core Idea
An analogy is only as strong as its shared cause — and the Ash'aris deny it.

The Mu'tazila argued that if the sinful believer's punishment ends, then the disbeliever's should also end, because both have finite sin. The Ash'ari response attacks the analogy at its root: the shared feature is not the true cause. In this lesson, we examine this third proof and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the argument.
The Arabic is short but dense; pay attention to the three denials.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila argument
Notice the analogy: if believer's punishment ceases, then disbeliever's should too, because both have finite sin.
The shared feature is 'finitude of sin'.
3
Examine the three denials
The answer denies (1) that finitude is the cause, (2) that disbelief is finite, (3) that the analogy is valid. List them.
Each denial targets a different premise.
4
Reflect on effectiveness
Ask yourself why these denials together refute the analogy. The commentary will help you connect them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث - لو انقطع عذاب الفاسق لانقطع عذاب الكافر بجامع تناهي المعصية . والجواب - منع علية التناهي، ومنع تناهي الكفر قدراً، ومنع صحة القياس في المقابل.
Third - if the punishment of the sinner were to cease, then the punishment of the disbeliever would cease due to the common factor of the finitude of sin. And the answer - denial of causality of finitude, denial of the finitude of disbelief in degree, and denial of the validity of analogy in the opposite.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's third proof, which is an analogy. They argue that if the punishment of the sinful believer (fāsiq) were to cease at some point, then by analogy the punishment of the disbeliever (kāfir) should also cease, because both share the common factor that sin is finite (mutanāhiya). Since the punishment of the disbeliever does not cease, the punishment of the sinner must also be eternal. The author answers by denying three things: first, that finitude is the effective cause (ʿilliyya) of the cessation of punishment; second, that disbelief is finite in degree (it is infinitely grave because it rejects the fundamentals); third, that the analogy is valid in this case (the two cases are not parallel). This refutes the analogy by attacking its premises.

Key Terms
انقطاع العذاب
تناهي المعصية
علية التناهي
قياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a sin is finite, the punishment must be finite.
Correction: Disbelief is not finite in magnitude; it is an infinite sin because it rejects the fundamentals, so its punishment is eternal.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the Mu'tazila's third proof that if the sinner's punishment ceases, the disbeliever's should also cease?
A) He concedes the analogy but says that the disbeliever's punishment is also finite.
B) He denies that the sin of the believer is finite.
C) He denies that finitude is the cause, that disbelief is finite, and that the analogy is valid.
D) He says that the disbeliever's punishment is greater and thus cannot cease.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari answer refutes the analogy by denying that finitude causes cessation, that disbelief is finite, and that the analogy holds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever compared your own sin to that of a non-believer, thinking both are 'finite' and therefore similar? How does the Ash'ari denial that disbelief is infinite in degree challenge that comparison and your heart's attitude towards your own sins?
?
When you sin repeatedly, do you ever assume that because it is 'just one sin' and thus finite, God's forgiveness is automatic? If finitude does not cause punishment to cease, what does that mean for your urgency to repent and change?
?
The Mu'tazila used an analogy to argue for eternal punishment of sinners. Have you ever used a similar logical trick to justify harsh judgment of others while excusing yourself? How can the threefold Ash'ari denial guard your heart against such self-deception and lead you to genuine humility before God?

The Second Objection: Can a Fasiq Enter Paradise by Merit?
Second objection and its answer regarding the fasiq's merit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the second objection against the view that a fasiq can enter Paradise, and the concise answer that denies both premises.

Core Idea
The fasiq's entry to Paradise is not a matter of merit.

The second objection challenges the possibility of a grave sinner (fasiq) entering Paradise, arguing that if he does, it must be due to his own merit. But that merit, the objection claims, is cancelled either by nullification or by balancing. The answer is a crisp rejection of both premises. Let's examine this short but dense exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection states that if the fasiq enters Paradise, it must be by his own merit (istihqaq). But that merit is negated by either ihbat (nullification) or muwazana (balancing).
These two mechanisms will be explained later in the text.
2
Identify the two premises
The objection has two premises: (1) Entry to Paradise requires merit. (2) The fasiq's merit is negated. The answer denies both.
3
Understand the answer
The answer simply says 'Denial of both premises' (man' al-muqaddimatayn). This means the author rejects the underlying assumptions without further elaboration here.
The full justification for the denial will come later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني - أن الفاسق لو دخل الجنة لكان باستحقاق، وقد انتفى بالإحباط أو الموازنة على ما سيجيء . والجواب - منع المقدمتين .
Second: That the fasiq (grave sinner), if he were to enter Paradise, he would do so by merit, and that [merit] is negated by nullification (al-ihbat) or balancing (al-muwazana) as will come. And the answer: Denial of both premises.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This is a short objection-answer pair. The objection states that if the fasiq enters Paradise, it must be because he deserves it by his deeds, but that merit is negated either by nullification (where sins nullify good deeds) or by balancing (where good and bad deeds are weighed), as will be explained later. So he cannot enter. The answer is simply to deny both premises: that he would enter by merit and that merit is negated. The author rejects the underlying assumptions.

الموازنة
الفاسق

Misconception: One might think that the fasiq automatically enters Paradise due to his faith, but the objection shows that merit is required; the answer rejects that requirement.
Correction: The author does not accept that entry to Paradise must be by merit; it can be by divine grace.

The Unbeliever Who Sought Truth but Failed
Refutes the idea that sincere seekers who fail are excused.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to the author, a disbeliever who exhaustively seeks truth but fails is not excused from punishment, contrary to the view of al-Jahiz and al-Anbari.

Core Idea
Sincere effort does not automatically excuse disbelief.

This lesson tackles a controversial theological question: if a person genuinely and exhaustively seeks the truth but fails to find it, are they excused in the sight of God? The author takes a firm stance against the view of al-Jahiz and al-Anbari, who argued that such a person should not be punished. We'll examine the author's refutation using Quranic verses and logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author first states the ruling for two types of disbelievers: the ignorant obstinate one, and the one who overexerted himself in seeking truth but failed.
The passage begins with 'This is the ruling of the disbeliever who is ignorant [and] obstinate...'
2
Identify the opposing view
Find where the author mentions al-Jahiz and al-Anbari. They claimed that the person who exhausted effort is excused because punishing him would contradict divine wisdom.
3
Understand the author's rebuttal
The author responds with Quranic verses about no hardship in religion, arguing that the perplexed one's incapacity is even greater, so the verses do not exempt them. Then he condemns the opposing view as breaking consensus and abandoning texts.
Focus on the logical flow: the verses are used to show that religion does not impose undue burden, but the author argues that this does not mean the seeker is excused.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the author is so emphatic. This teaches that in Sunni theology, disbelief is a culpable state, and sincere effort does not automatically remove culpability if the truth was accessible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا حكم الكافر الجاهل المعاند وكذا من بالغ في الطلب والنظر، واستفرغ المجهود ولم ينل المقصود خلافاً للجاحظ والعنبري، حيث زعما أنه معذور، إذ لا يليق بحكمة الحكيم أن يعذبه مع بذل الجهد والطاقة من غير جرم وتقصير. كيف وقد قال الله تعالى : وما جعل عليكم في الدين من حرج (۲) ليس على الأعمى حرج ولا على الأعرج حرج ولا على المريض حرج (۳) ولا شك أن عجز المتحير أشد . وهذا الفرق خرق للإجماع وترك للنصوص الواردة في هذا الباب. هذا في حق الكفار عناداً واعتقاداً، وأما الكفار حكماً كأطفال المشركين، فكذلك عند الأكثرين لدخولهم في العمومات، ولما روي أن ...
This is the ruling of the disbeliever who is ignorant [and] obstinate, and likewise one who went to extremes in seeking and inquiry and exhausted all effort yet did not attain the goal, contrary to al-Jahiz and al-Anbari, who claimed that he is excused, because it is not befitting to the wisdom of the Wise to punish him given his exertion of effort and energy without sin and shortcoming. How so when Allah said: 'And He has not placed upon you in the religion any hardship' (22:78), 'There is no blame upon the blind, nor upon the lame, nor upon the sick' (24:61, 48:17). And there is no doubt that the incapacity of the perplexed one is greater. And this divergence breaks consensus and abandons the revealed texts on this matter. This is regarding the disbelievers out of obstinacy and belief. As for the disbelievers by ruling (hukman), like the children of polytheists, they are likewise according to the majority due to their inclusion in the general texts, and due to what was narrated that...
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling of the disbeliever who is obstinate and ignorant, as well as the one who made great effort to seek truth but failed. Against al-Jahiz and al-Anbari, the author states that such a person is not excused; it is not below God's wisdom to punish him despite his effort, because there is no hardship in religion for those who are unable. The incapacity of the perplexed is even greater (so they are not excused). This view breaks consensus and ignores texts. Then the author distinguishes between disbelievers due to obstinacy and belief, and disbelievers by ruling such as children of polytheists. The majority holds that they enter hell as well because they are included in general texts and a narration.

Key Terms
المعاند
الجاهل
المتحير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person who exhaustively seeks truth but fails is excused and will not be punished.
Correction: The author argues that such a person is not excused, because the incapacity of the perplexed is greater and the Quranic verses about no hardship do not exempt them from punishment.

Checkpoint
What did al-Jahiz and al-Anbari claim about the person who exhausted effort in seeking truth?
A) He is a disbeliever and will be punished
B) He is excused and will not be punished
C) He is considered a Muslim
D) His status is unknown

Key Takeaway
Exhaustive effort in seeking truth does not excuse disbelief; the perplexed seeker is still culpable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely assumed that your sincerity and effort in seeking Allah will automatically excuse your shortcomings. But this lesson says otherwise: even the one who exhausted all effort is not excused if he fails to attain the truth. What if your current understanding of Islam is still incomplete—are you relying on your own effort rather than humbly submitting to the clear texts? Are you truly seeking the truth as revealed, or are you content with your own conclusions? Will you examine your beliefs today and strip away any reliance on your own diligence, turning completely to the Quran and Sunnah as the sole criteria?
?
The opponent in this passage argued that punishing the perplexed seeker contradicts divine wisdom. Do you ever find yourself questioning Allah's justice when you see someone who seems sincere but is on misguidance? This lesson forces you to check your heart: do you trust that Allah's wisdom is perfect, even when He does not exempt those who tried but failed? Are you willing to accept that your own efforts do not earn you immunity from accountability? Repent from any hidden sense of entitlement before Allah and commit to seeking truth with full submission.

Children of Polytheists: The Majority View
States the majority view that children of polytheists are in hell.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to the majority of scholars, children of polytheists are considered disbelievers and subject to punishment.

Core Idea
Children of polytheists are included in general threats of punishment.

After discussing adult disbelievers, the author turns to the status of children of polytheists. This lesson presents the majority view that they are 'disbelievers by ruling' and thus share the same fate as their parents. The reasoning is based on their inclusion in general scriptural threats.

Guided Path
1
Read the short passage
Read the Arabic and translation. The author distinguishes between disbelievers out of obstinacy (adults) and disbelievers by ruling (children of polytheists).
The passage is very concise.
2
Note the majority opinion
The author says 'they are likewise according to the majority'. This means they are also in hell.
3
Understand the reasons
Two reasons are given: (1) inclusion in general texts (Quranic verses and hadith that threaten all disbelievers), and (2) a narration (not fully quoted).
The general texts are 'العمومات' – universal statements about punishment for disbelievers.
4
Consider the incomplete ending
The passage ends with 'ولما روي أن...' which suggests a hadith but is cut off. The point is that the majority relies on these evidences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا حكم الكافر الجاهل المعاند وكذا من بالغ في الطلب والنظر، واستفرغ المجهود ولم ينل المقصود خلافاً للجاحظ والعنبري، حيث زعما أنه معذور، إذ لا يليق بحكمة الحكيم أن يعذبه مع بذل الجهد والطاقة من غير جرم وتقصير. كيف وقد قال الله تعالى : وما جعل عليكم في الدين من حرج (۲) ليس على الأعمى حرج ولا على الأعرج حرج ولا على المريض حرج (۳) ولا شك أن عجز المتحير أشد . وهذا الفرق خرق للإجماع وترك للنصوص الواردة في هذا الباب. هذا في حق الكفار عناداً واعتقاداً، وأما الكفار حكماً كأطفال المشركين، فكذلك عند الأكثرين لدخولهم في العمومات، ولما روي أن ...
This is the ruling of the disbeliever who is ignorant [and] obstinate, and likewise one who went to extremes in seeking and inquiry and exhausted all effort yet did not attain the goal, contrary to al-Jahiz and al-Anbari, who claimed that he is excused, because it is not befitting to the wisdom of the Wise to punish him given his exertion of effort and energy without sin and shortcoming. How so when Allah said: 'And He has not placed upon you in the religion any hardship' (22:78), 'There is no blame upon the blind, nor upon the lame, nor upon the sick' (24:61, 48:17). And there is no doubt that the incapacity of the perplexed one is greater. And this divergence breaks consensus and abandons the revealed texts on this matter. This is regarding the disbelievers out of obstinacy and belief. As for the disbelievers by ruling (hukman), like the children of polytheists, they are likewise according to the majority due to their inclusion in the general texts, and due to what was narrated that...
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling of the disbeliever who is obstinate and ignorant, as well as the one who made great effort to seek truth but failed. Against al-Jahiz and al-Anbari, the author states that such a person is not excused; it is not below God's wisdom to punish him despite his effort, because there is no hardship in religion for those who are unable. The incapacity of the perplexed is even greater (so they are not excused). This view breaks consensus and ignores texts. Then the author distinguishes between disbelievers due to obstinacy and belief, and disbelievers by ruling such as children of polytheists. The majority holds that they enter hell as well because they are included in general texts and a narration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: The majority holds that they are punished because they are included in general threats, though there is a discussion about the age of accountability.

Ash'ari Position on Grave Sinners
The author states the Ash'ari position on grave sinners and clarifies the concept of manzila bayn al-manzilatayn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position on the fate of believers who die without repenting from major sins, and how it differs from the Mu'tazila view, including the correct meaning of manzila bayn al-manzilatayn.

Core Idea
Ash'aris leave the fate of grave sinners to Allah's will, affirming eventual salvation.

The fate of a believer who commits a major sin and dies without repentance is a classic point of difference between Ash'aris and Mu'tazila. In this passage, the author states the Ash'ari position clearly: we do not definitively say the sinner will be forgiven or punished, but if punished, the punishment is not eternal. He also corrects a common misunderstanding about the Mu'tazila concept of 'a station between the two stations.'

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without pausing. Note the key terms like 'الكبيرة', 'التوبة', 'التخليد', 'المنزلة بين المنزلتين'.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage contrasts the Ash'ari and Mu'tazila views. Underline the phrases that describe each school's stance on forgiveness, punishment, and eternity.
The Ash'ari view avoids both extremes.
3
Examine the clarification on manzila
Focus on the last part where the author corrects a mistake. Understand what 'manzila bayn al-manzilatayn' actually means according to the Mu'tazila.
It is a state, not a physical location.
4
Connect to the broader theological context
Reflect on why this issue matters: it relates to divine justice, mercy, and the nature of faith. The Ash'ari position upholds God's absolute will while preserving the promise of salvation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلف أهل الإسلام فيمن ارتكب الكبيرة من المؤمنين ومات قبل التوبة ، فالمذهب عندنا عدم القطع بالعفو ولا بالعقاب بل كلاهما في مشيئة الله تعالى لكن على تقدير التعذيب نقطع بأنه لا يخلد في النار، بل يخرج البتة، لا بطريق الوجوب على الله تعالى، بل بمقتضى ما سبق من الوعد . وثبت بالدليل كتخليد أهل الجنة . وعند المعتزلة القطع بالعذاب الدائم من غير عفو ولا إخراج من النار، ويعبر عن هذا بمسألة وعيد الفساق، وعقوبة العصاة، وانقطاع عذاب أهل الكبائر، ونحو ذلك . وليس في مسألة الاستحقاق ووجوب العقاب غنى عن ذلك، لأن التخليد أمر زائد على التعذيب. ولا في مسألة العفو، لأنه بطريق الاحتمال دون القطع ، ولأنه شاع في ترك العقاب بالكلية، وهذا قطع بالخروج بعد الدخول، وما وقع في كلام البعض من أن صاحب الكبيرة عند المعتزلة ليس في الجنة ولا في النار فغلط نشأ من قولهم : إن له المنزلة بين المنزلتين. أي حالة غير الإيمان والكفر .
And the people of Islam differed concerning the one who commits a major sin among the believers and dies before repentance. Our school (madhhab) is that there is no definitive judgment of forgiveness nor of punishment; rather both are in the will of Allah. However, on the assumption of punishment, we are certain that he will not be eternally (yukhallad) in the Fire; rather he will definitely come out, not by way of obligation (wujub) upon Allah, but by virtue of what has preceded of the promise. And it is established by evidence like the eternal abiding of the people of Paradise. And according to the Mu'tazila, there is a definitive judgment of eternal punishment without forgiveness and without removal from the Fire. This is expressed as the issue of the threat (wa'id) against the transgressors, the punishment of the disobedient, and the non-interruption of the punishment of the people of major sins, and similar. And the issue of entitlement (istihqaq) and obligation of punishment is not independent of that, because eternity (takhlid) is something additional to punishment. Nor is the issue of forgiveness (afw), because it is by way of probability (ihtimal) without certainty, and because it is commonly used for the complete abandonment of punishment, while this is a definitive judgment of exit after entry. And what has occurred in the speech of some that the perpetrator of a major sin according to the Mu'tazila is neither in Paradise nor in the Fire is a mistake arising from their statement that he has a station between the two stations (manzila bayn al-manzilatayn), that is, a state other than faith and disbelief.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari position on the fate of a believer who commits a major sin and dies without repentance. The author states that according to the Ash'aris (when we say 'our school'), we do not make a definitive judgment either of forgiveness or of punishment. Both possibilities remain within Allah's will. However, if Allah does punish, we are certain that the punishment will not be eternal; the sinner will definitely be brought out of Hell. This is not because Allah is obliged to do so, but because He has promised it. This is supported by evidence, just as the eternal abiding of the people of Paradise is established. In contrast, the Mu'tazila hold that the punishment is definitive and eternal, with no forgiveness or exit. They refer to this as the issue of threat (wa'id) against sinners. The author then clarifies that the Mu'tazila concept of 'manzila bayn al-manzilatayn' (a station between the two stations) is misunderstood by some. Properly, it refers to a state that is neither faith nor disbelief, not that the sinner is neither in Paradise nor in Hell. The Ash'ari position is thus distinct: it avoids both the extreme of guaranteed forgiveness and the extreme of eternal punishment.

Key Terms
الكبيرة
التوبة
العفو
العقاب
المشيئة
التخليد
المنزلة بين المنزلتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that according to the Mu'tazila, a major sinner is physically neither in Paradise nor in Hell (i.e., an intermediate location).
Correction: The phrase 'manzila bayn al-manzilatayn' actually means a spiritual state that is neither faith nor disbelief, not a physical place.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position in this passage, what can be said about a believer who dies without repenting from a major sin if Allah punishes him?
A) He will be punished eternally.
B) He will be forgiven without punishment.
C) He will be punished but eventually exit Hell.
D) He will remain in a state between faith and disbelief.

Key Takeaway
Ash'aris affirm that a believer's ultimate fate for major sins rests in Allah's will, but if punished, it will not be eternal, while the Mu'tazila hold to eternal punishment; the 'between two stations' concept refers to a spiritual state, not a physical realm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own sins, which stance do you secretly lean toward—the Mu'tazila insistence on strict justice or the Ash'ari hope in forgiveness? What does that reveal about your understanding of Allah's mercy and your own accountability? Will you let this uncertainty compel you to repent without delay, or will you presume upon Allah's mercy?
?
The passage says we cannot be certain of forgiveness nor punishment. How does this uncertainty affect the way you view others who have committed major sins? Do you judge them with certainty about their fate? What would it take for you to leave that judgment to Allah?
?
If you truly believe that a believer might be punished but eventually saved, does that make you more cautious about sinning or more presumptuous? Examine your heart right now: are you relying on Allah's promise or on your own deeds?

Refutation of the Merit Objection
Taftazani refutes the second objection on merit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fasiq's entry into Paradise is by divine grace, not personal merit, and how Taftazani refutes the Mu'tazilite claim of necessary merit.

Core Idea
Salvation is by Allah's grace, not human merit.

The Mu'tazilites argued that if a fasiq (grave sinner) entered Paradise, it must be because he deserved it, like others who enter. But Taftazani denies both premises of this argument, affirming that entry is solely by Allah's bounty and mercy. This passage also introduces the concepts of ihbat and muwazana, which will be discussed later.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its premises
Read the Arabic passage identifying the Mu'tazilite claim: the fasiq would enter Paradise only if deserving, like the disbeliever cannot enter undeserving.
2
Examine the refutation
Notice that Taftazani denies both premises: the fasiq enters by Allah's bounty, not merit, and the premise that the disbeliever cannot enter undeserving is irrelevant.
3
Note the mention of ihbat and muwazana
These concepts will be explained later; they deal with nullification and balancing of deeds.
These are key terms in Mu'tazilite thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا الخامسة والسادسة حملاً للحدود على حدود الإسلام، ولإحاطة الخطيئة على غلبتها ، بحيث لا يبقى معها الإيمان هذا مع ما في الخلود من الاحتمال. الثاني - أن الفاسق لو دخل الجنة لكان باستحقاق لامتناع دخول غير المستحق كالكافر. واللازم منتف لبطلان الاستحقاق بالإحباط أو الموازنة على ما سيجيء. ورد بمنع المقدمتين، بل إنما يدخل بفضل الله ورحمته ووعده ومغفرته . وسنتكلم على الإحباط والموازنة . الثالث - لو انقطع عذاب الفاسق لانقطع عذاب الكافر قياساً عليه بجامع تناهي المعصية . ورد بمنع غلبة التناهي، ومنع تناهي الكفر قدراً، ومنع اعتبار القياس في مقابلة النص والإجماع وفي الاعتقادات. الرابع - أن الوعيد بالعقاب الدائم لطف بالعباد لكونه أزجر على المعاصي. فإن منهم من لا يكترث بالعذاب المنقطع عند الميل إلى المستلذات ، ثم لا بد من تحقيق الوعيد تصديقاً للخبر وصوناً للقول عن التبديل. ورد بمنع وجوب اللطف ومنع إنحصاره في الدوام، فإن من لا يكترث باللبث في الجحيم أحقاباً ، قلما يستكثر الخلود فيها عقاباً، وإذ قد كان كل وعيد لطفاً، ولا شيء من الوعيد بلطف للكل فليكن لطف الخلود في النار مختصاً بالكفار، وكفى بوعيد النيران، بل وعد الجنات لطفاً ومزجرة لأهل الإيمان . ولو وجب ما هو الغاية في اللطف والزجر ، لما صح الاكتفاء بوعيد الخلود في النار لإمكان المزيد.
And likewise the fifth and sixth (objections) by carrying the boundaries to the boundaries of Islam, and by the encompassing of sin dominating, such that faith does not remain with it, despite what is in eternity of possibility. The second: that if the fasiq entered Paradise, it would be by merit, because it is impossible for one who is not deserving to enter like the disbeliever. And the consequent is false because of the nullification of merit by ihbat or muwazana as will come. And it is refuted by denying both premises; rather he enters only by Allah's bounty and mercy and promise and forgiveness. And we will discuss ihbat and muwazana. The third: if the punishment of the fasiq were to cease, the punishment of the disbeliever would cease by analogy on it due to the commonality of the finitude of disobedience. And it is refuted by denying the predominance of finitude, and denying the finitude of disbelief in magnitude, and denying the consideration of analogy in opposition to the text and consensus and in matters of belief. The fourth: that the threat of eternal punishment is a kindness (luṭf) to the servants because it is more deterring against sins. For among them are those who do not care about finite punishment when inclining to pleasures, then the threat must be fulfilled to confirm the report and safeguard the statement from alteration. And it is refuted by denying the obligation of kindness and its exclusivity to perpetuity, for one who does not care about remaining in Hell for ages rarely considers eternity therein as severe punishment, and since every threat is a kindness, and no threat is a kindness for everyone, then let the kindness of eternity in the Fire be specific to the disbelievers, and sufficient is the threat of the Fires, nay the promise of Gardens is a kindness and deterrent for the people of faith. And if what is utmost in kindness and deterrence were obligatory, then being satisfied with the threat of eternity in the Fire would not be valid because of the possibility of more.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage refutes three Mu'tazilite objections that argue for the eternal punishment of the fasiq (the grave sinner who is a believer). The refutations are based on logical and scriptural grounds.

The second objection claims that if the fasiq entered Paradise, it would be by merit, but entering Paradise without merit is impossible. The response denies both premises: entry is by Allah's bounty, not merit, and the assertion that merit is nullified by ihbat or muwazana is not accepted.

The third objection uses analogy: if the fasiq's punishment ends, so should the disbeliever's, since both have finite sins. The response rejects that sin is finite in every case, especially disbelief, and denies that analogy applies in matters of faith against explicit texts and consensus.

The fourth objection argues that eternal threat is a kindness (luṭf) because it deters more effectively, and threats must be fulfilled. The response denies that kindness is obligatory or exclusive to eternity; some are not deterred even by eternal threat, and such a threat is only a kindness for disbelievers. If utmost kindness were obligatory, a more severe punishment could be imagined, so no threat is final.

Key Terms
فاسق
استحقاق
إحباط
موازنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person enters Paradise only through their own merit and deserving.
Correction: Entry into Paradise is by Allah's grace, mercy, promise, and forgiveness, not merely by human merit.

Checkpoint
How does Taftazani refute the claim that the fasiq would enter Paradise by merit?
A) By denying that merit is possible for anyone
B) By denying both premises of the argument
C) By affirming that the fasiq's sins are forgiven automatically
D) By stating that the fasiq never enters Paradise

Key Takeaway
The fasiq's entry into Paradise is by Allah's grace, not human merit, refuting the Mu'tazilite objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you stand before Allah, will you count on your own deeds to save you, or will you plead for His mercy? How does this passage expose the danger of relying on your own merit?
?
If the fasiq enters Paradise by pure grace, what keeps you from despairing when you sin? Does your hope in Allah's promise outweigh your fear of His justice?
?
Are there areas in your life where you act as if you deserve Allah's reward? How can you cultivate a heart that expects nothing but relies on His bounty and forgiveness?

Refutation of the Analogy Objection
Taftazani refutes the third objection using analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why analogy (qiyas) cannot be used to equate the fasiq's punishment with the disbeliever's, and the three reasons Taftazani gives for rejecting it.

Core Idea
Analogy fails against scripture and reason in matters of faith.

The third objection uses an analogy: if the fasiq's punishment ends, so should the disbeliever's, since both sins are finite. Taftazani refutes this by denying the finitude of disbelief and rejecting analogy in theological matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its analogy
Identify the analogy: cessation of fasiq's punishment implies cessation of disbeliever's punishment because both sins are finite.
2
Examine the three refutations
Taftazani denies: (1) that sin is always finite, (2) that disbelief is finite in magnitude, and (3) that analogy is valid against text and consensus.
3
Reflect on the role of analogy in theology
Analogy is not a source in matters of belief; it cannot override nass (scripture) and ijma' (consensus).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And likewise the fifth and sixth (objections) by carrying the boundaries to the boundaries of Islam, and by the encompassing of sin dominating, such that faith does not remain with it, despite what is in eternity of possibility. The second: that if the fasiq entered Paradise, it would be by merit, because it is impossible for one who is not deserving to enter like the disbeliever. And the consequent is false because of the nullification of merit by ihbat or muwazana as will come. And it is refuted by denying both premises; rather he enters only by Allah's bounty and mercy and promise and forgiveness. And we will discuss ihbat and muwazana. The third: if the punishment of the fasiq were to cease, the punishment of the disbeliever would cease by analogy on it due to the commonality of the finitude of disobedience. And it is refuted by denying the predominance of finitude, and denying the finitude of disbelief in magnitude, and denying the consideration of analogy in opposition to the text and consensus and in matters of belief. The fourth: that the threat of eternal punishment is a kindness (luṭf) to the servants because it is more deterring against sins. For among them are those who do not care about finite punishment when inclining to pleasures, then the threat must be fulfilled to confirm the report and safeguard the statement from alteration. And it is refuted by denying the obligation of kindness and its exclusivity to perpetuity, for one who does not care about remaining in Hell for ages rarely considers eternity therein as severe punishment, and since every threat is a kindness, and no threat is a kindness for everyone, then let the kindness of eternity in the Fire be specific to the disbelievers, and sufficient is the threat of the Fires, nay the promise of Gardens is a kindness and deterrent for the people of faith. And if what is utmost in kindness and deterrence were obligatory, then being satisfied with the threat of eternity in the Fire would not be valid because of the possibility of more.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage refutes three Mu'tazilite objections that argue for the eternal punishment of the fasiq (the grave sinner who is a believer). The refutations are based on logical and scriptural grounds.

The second objection claims that if the fasiq entered Paradise, it would be by merit, but entering Paradise without merit is impossible. The response denies both premises: entry is by Allah's bounty, not merit, and the assertion that merit is nullified by ihbat or muwazana is not accepted.

The third objection uses analogy: if the fasiq's punishment ends, so should the disbeliever's, since both have finite sins. The response rejects that sin is finite in every case, especially disbelief, and denies that analogy applies in matters of faith against explicit texts and consensus.

The fourth objection argues that eternal threat is a kindness (luṭf) because it deters more effectively, and threats must be fulfilled. The response denies that kindness is obligatory or exclusive to eternity; some are not deterred even by eternal threat, and such a threat is only a kindness for disbelievers. If utmost kindness were obligatory, a more severe punishment could be imagined, so no threat is final.

Key Terms
قياس
تناهي
معصية
نص
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fasiq and the disbeliever are analogous because both have finite sins, so their punishments should be alike.
Correction: Disbelief is not finite in magnitude, and analogy cannot override explicit texts and consensus in theology.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a reason Taftazani gives for rejecting the analogy?
A) Sin is not always finite
B) The disbelief is not finite in magnitude
C) Analogy is valid in matters of belief
D) Analogy cannot contradict text and consensus

Key Takeaway
Analogy is not a valid tool in theology when it contradicts explicit revelation and consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used your own reasoning to justify a belief or action that deep down you knew contradicted the clear words of Allah? What would it mean for you to truly submit to His text even when your mind resists?
?
This lesson warns against using analogy to limit Allah's punishment. Is there an area of your life where you have placed limits on Allah's commands—thinking 'this is enough' or 'that is too far'—based on your own logic? How would your life change if you surrendered that logic entirely to revelation?
?
The Mu'tazilites' error was to assume that because sins are similar in one aspect, they must be similar in all aspects. Are there people or situations in your life where you have judged by a single commonality rather than by the full truth of Allah's word? Will you repent of that and seek His guidance instead?

Refutation of the Lutf Objection
Taftazani refutes the fourth objection on kindness (luṭf).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that eternal punishment is a kindness (luṭf) and Taftazani's response that it is neither obligatory nor exclusive.

Core Idea
Eternal punishment is not a universal kindness; it only deters some.

The fourth objection claims that the threat of eternal punishment is a kindness (luṭf) from Allah to deter sin, and it must be fulfilled. Taftazani responds by denying the obligation of such kindness and showing it applies only to disbelievers. He also argues that if utmost kindness were obligatory, a more severe punishment could be imagined, so the threat cannot be final.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection defining lutf
Understand the Mu'tazilite view: eternal threat is a kindness because it deters; threats must be fulfilled.
2
Examine the refutation
Taftazani denies that kindness is obligatory and that it is exclusive to eternal punishment; some are not deterred even by eternal threat.
3
Note the final argument
If the utmost kindness were necessary, an even more severe threat could always be imagined, so no threat can be sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا الخامسة والسادسة حملاً للحدود على حدود الإسلام، ولإحاطة الخطيئة على غلبتها ، بحيث لا يبقى معها الإيمان هذا مع ما في الخلود من الاحتمال. الثاني - أن الفاسق لو دخل الجنة لكان باستحقاق لامتناع دخول غير المستحق كالكافر. واللازم منتف لبطلان الاستحقاق بالإحباط أو الموازنة على ما سيجيء. ورد بمنع المقدمتين، بل إنما يدخل بفضل الله ورحمته ووعده ومغفرته . وسنتكلم على الإحباط والموازنة . الثالث - لو انقطع عذاب الفاسق لانقطع عذاب الكافر قياساً عليه بجامع تناهي المعصية . ورد بمنع غلبة التناهي، ومنع تناهي الكفر قدراً، ومنع اعتبار القياس في مقابلة النص والإجماع وفي الاعتقادات. الرابع - أن الوعيد بالعقاب الدائم لطف بالعباد لكونه أزجر على المعاصي. فإن منهم من لا يكترث بالعذاب المنقطع عند الميل إلى المستلذات ، ثم لا بد من تحقيق الوعيد تصديقاً للخبر وصوناً للقول عن التبديل. ورد بمنع وجوب اللطف ومنع إنحصاره في الدوام، فإن من لا يكترث باللبث في الجحيم أحقاباً ، قلما يستكثر الخلود فيها عقاباً، وإذ قد كان كل وعيد لطفاً، ولا شيء من الوعيد بلطف للكل فليكن لطف الخلود في النار مختصاً بالكفار، وكفى بوعيد النيران، بل وعد الجنات لطفاً ومزجرة لأهل الإيمان . ولو وجب ما هو الغاية في اللطف والزجر ، لما صح الاكتفاء بوعيد الخلود في النار لإمكان المزيد.
And likewise the fifth and sixth (objections) by carrying the boundaries to the boundaries of Islam, and by the encompassing of sin dominating, such that faith does not remain with it, despite what is in eternity of possibility. The second: that if the fasiq entered Paradise, it would be by merit, because it is impossible for one who is not deserving to enter like the disbeliever. And the consequent is false because of the nullification of merit by ihbat or muwazana as will come. And it is refuted by denying both premises; rather he enters only by Allah's bounty and mercy and promise and forgiveness. And we will discuss ihbat and muwazana. The third: if the punishment of the fasiq were to cease, the punishment of the disbeliever would cease by analogy on it due to the commonality of the finitude of disobedience. And it is refuted by denying the predominance of finitude, and denying the finitude of disbelief in magnitude, and denying the consideration of analogy in opposition to the text and consensus and in matters of belief. The fourth: that the threat of eternal punishment is a kindness (luṭf) to the servants because it is more deterring against sins. For among them are those who do not care about finite punishment when inclining to pleasures, then the threat must be fulfilled to confirm the report and safeguard the statement from alteration. And it is refuted by denying the obligation of kindness and its exclusivity to perpetuity, for one who does not care about remaining in Hell for ages rarely considers eternity therein as severe punishment, and since every threat is a kindness, and no threat is a kindness for everyone, then let the kindness of eternity in the Fire be specific to the disbelievers, and sufficient is the threat of the Fires, nay the promise of Gardens is a kindness and deterrent for the people of faith. And if what is utmost in kindness and deterrence were obligatory, then being satisfied with the threat of eternity in the Fire would not be valid because of the possibility of more.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage refutes three Mu'tazilite objections that argue for the eternal punishment of the fasiq (the grave sinner who is a believer). The refutations are based on logical and scriptural grounds.

The second objection claims that if the fasiq entered Paradise, it would be by merit, but entering Paradise without merit is impossible. The response denies both premises: entry is by Allah's bounty, not merit, and the assertion that merit is nullified by ihbat or muwazana is not accepted.

If utmost kindness were obligatory, a more severe punishment could be imagined, so no threat is final.

The Ash'ari and Mu'tazilite Views on the Believer's Fate
Taftazani states the two positions and gives the transmitted argument for the Ash'ari view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the contrasting Ash'ari and Mu'tazilite positions on the believer who mixes good and bad deeds, and the transmitted evidence supporting the Ash'ari view.

Core Idea
The believer who mixes good and bad deeds will ultimately enter Paradise, even after punishment.

This lesson introduces the central disagreement between the Ash'ari and Mu'tazilite schools on the fate of the sinful believer. The Ash'aris affirm eventual salvation, while the Mu'tazila insist on eternal punishment. We begin with the transmitted argument from the Qur'an that Allah does not waste the reward of the good-doers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two positions
Read the Arabic passage once to see how Taftazani states both the Ash'ari and Mu'tazilite views.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim of ihbat
Notice that the Mu'tazila believe sins annul (ihbat) all good deeds, even a single major sin annuls all acts of obedience.
Their extreme position makes the refutation easier.
3
Examine the transmitted (sam'an) refutation
The Ash'ari refute by citing texts that Allah does not waste reward. Reflect on how this principle protects good deeds from being nullified.
4
Reflect on how the verse supports the Ash'ari view
Though only mentioned here briefly, the idea that Allah does not waste reward is a key Qur'anic principle that undercuts the Mu'tazilite claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المبحث الحادي عشر - المؤمن إذا خلط الحسنات بالسيئات فعندنا في الجنة، ولو بعد النار. وعند المعتزلة مخلد في النار ذهاباً إلى أن السيئات تحبط الحسنات. حتى ذهب الجمهور منهم إلى أن الكبيرة الواحدة تحبط جميع الطاعات. فاسد سمعاً وهو للنصوص الدالة على أن الله لا يضيع أجر المحسنين، وعقلاً للقطع بقبح إبطال ثواب طاعة مائة سنة بشرب جرعة من الخمر ولأن جهة الاستحقاق عندهم ، وهو كون الفعل حسنة وامتثالاً باق، ولأنه يوجب منافاة الكبيرة لصحة الطاعة كالردة . قالوا : الثواب منفعة خالصة دائمة مع التعظيم والعقاب، مضرة خالصة دائمة مع الإهانة، فلا يجتمعان استحقاقاً. قلنا : لو سلم لزوم قيد الخلوص والدوام، فلا يوجب تنافي الاستحقاقين، ولو سلم فليس إبطال الحسنة بالسيئة أولى من العكس، كيف وقد قال الله تعالى : إن الحسنات يذهبن السيئات
He said: The eleventh topic - The believer when he mixes good deeds with bad deeds is, according to us, in Paradise, even if after the Fire. According to the Mu'tazila, he is eternally in the Fire, going to the view that bad deeds annul good deeds. Until the majority among them went to the view that a single major sin annuls all acts of obedience. It is invalid by transmission because of the texts indicating that Allah does not waste the reward of the good-doers, and by reason because of the certainty of the ugliness of nullifying the reward of a hundred years of obedience by drinking a gulp of wine, and because the aspect of merit according to them - which is the act being good and an act of obedience - remains, and because it necessitates that the major sin conflicts with the validity of obedience like apostasy. They said: Reward is a pure permanent benefit with glorification, and punishment is a pure permanent harm with humiliation, so they cannot be combined in terms of merit. We said: Even if the restriction of purity and permanence is conceded, it does not necessitate incompatibility of the two merits, and even if conceded, then nullifying the good deed by the bad deed is not more plausible than the opposite, especially since Allah said: 'Indeed good deeds remove bad deeds.'
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari doctrine on the fate of the believer who mixes good and bad deeds: he will ultimately enter Paradise, even if he first undergoes punishment in the Fire. In contrast, the Mu'tazila hold that such a person is eternally in the Fire, believing that sins annul all good deeds. Some Mu'tazila even claim that a single major sin annuls all acts of obedience.

The Ash'ari refutation is twofold: transmitted (sam'an) and rational ('aqlan). Transmitted evidence includes verses indicating that Allah does not waste the reward of the good-doers. Rational evidence includes the absurdity of nullifying a century of worship by one sin, the fact that the act's quality as good and obedient remains, and that treating a major sin like apostasy in nullifying all good deeds is invalid.

The Mu'tazila then object: reward is a pure permanent benefit with honor, and punishment is a pure permanent harm with humiliation; they cannot coexist in one person simultaneously as deserved. The Ash'ari response: even if we grant the conditions of purity and permanence, it does not necessitate that the two merits are incompatible. Moreover, it is not more reasonable that the bad deed nullifies the good deed rather than the opposite, especially since the Qur'an states: 'Indeed good deeds remove bad deeds.'

Key Terms
مخلط
إحباط
كبيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari position on the believer who mixes good and bad deeds?
A) He is eternally in the Fire
B) He is in Paradise, possibly after punishment
C) His good deeds are annulled by his sins
D) He will never enter Paradise

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari school affirms that the sinful believer will ultimately enter Paradise, supported by the principle that Allah does not waste reward.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that a single major sin could erase all your good deeds, how would that change the way you approach repentance and hope in Allah's mercy? What does this lesson challenge in that view?
?
When you look at your own life and see a mix of good and bad deeds, do you tend to despair that your sins outweigh your good, or do you assume your good deeds are worthless? How does the Ash'ari position (based on the Quranic statement in this source) address that fear?
?
If you knew someone who committed a major sin while striving to do good, would you judge them as doomed to hell forever based on the Mu'tazilite view, or would you hope for mercy based on the scriptural evidence cited? What does this tell you about your own understanding of Allah's justice and mercy?

The Rational Arguments for the Ash'ari Position
Taftazani gives three rational arguments against the Mu'tazilite ihbat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proofs the Ash'aris use to refute the Mu'tazilite doctrine of ihbat.

Core Idea
It is rationally absurd to nullify a lifetime of worship for a single sin.

After the transmitted argument, Taftazani presents three rational arguments that demonstrate the irrationality of the Mu'tazilite claim that a single major sin annuls all good deeds. These arguments rely on the absurdity of the consequence, the persistence of merit, and the inconsistency with valid obedience.

Guided Path
1
Read the three rational arguments
Read the Arabic passage for the rational arguments and see how Taftazani structures them.
2
Analyze the argument from absurdity
Nullifying a hundred years of obedience for a gulp of wine is clearly unreasonable. Ask yourself why this is intuitive.
3
Note the argument that the aspect of merit remains
The act of obedience is good in itself, so its merit should not disappear even if the person commits sins.
4
Consider the argument that major sin cannot be like apostasy
Apostasy annuls all deeds, but major sins are not apostasy. Therefore, a major sin should not nullify all good deeds.
This highlights the difference between major sins and disbelief.

The Mu'tazilite Objection and Ash'ari Response
Taftazani presents the Mu'tazilite objection and the Ash'ari response with a Quranic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite objection that reward and punishment cannot coexist, and the Ash'ari refutation using logical analysis and the Quran.

Core Idea
Reward and punishment are not contradictory in essence, and good deeds erase bad ones.

The Mu'tazila object that reward and punishment are mutually exclusive because one is pure benefit and the other pure harm. The Ash'aris respond by challenging the assumption of incompatibility and countering with a Qur'anic verse that states good deeds remove bad deeds.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite objection about purity and permanence
Read how the Mu'tazila define reward and punishment as pure and permanent opposites that cannot be deserved together.
2
Understand the first Ash'ari response
Even if we grant the definitions, the Ash'ari argue that this does not entail incompatibility of the two states of merit.
The objection does not prove that the two merits cannot coexist.
3
Note the second Ash'ari point
If there is conflict, it is not more plausible that the bad deed nullifies the good deed than the reverse. This is a logical symmetry argument.
4
Reflect on the Quranic verse as final evidence
The verse 'Indeed good deeds remove bad deeds' directly supports the Ash'ari view that good deeds can cancel bad ones.
This is a definitive scriptural proof.

We said: Even if the restriction of purity and permanence is conceded, it does not necessitate incompatibility of the two merits, and even if conceded, then nullifying the good deed by the bad deed is not more plausible than the opposite, especially since Allah said: 'Indeed good deeds remove bad deeds.'
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari doctrine on the fate of the believer who mixes good and bad deeds: he will ultimately enter Paradise, even if he first undergoes punishment in the Fire. In contrast, the Mu'tazila hold that such a person is eternally in the Fire, believing that sins annul all good deeds. Some Mu'tazila even claim that a single major sin annuls all acts of obedience.

The Ash'ari refutation is twofold: transmitted (sam'an) and rational ('aqlan). Transmitted evidence includes verses indicating that Allah does not waste the reward of the good-doers. Rational evidence includes the absurdity of nullifying a century of worship by one sin, the fact that the act's quality as good and obedient remains, and that treating a major sin like apostasy in nullifying all good deeds is invalid.

The Mu'tazila then object: reward is a pure permanent benefit with honor, and punishment is a pure permanent harm with humiliation; they cannot coexist in one person simultaneously as deserved. The Ash'ari response: even if we grant the conditions of purity and permanence, it does not necessitate that the two merits are incompatible. Moreover, it is not more reasonable that the bad deed nullifies the good deed rather than the opposite, especially since the Qur'an states: 'Indeed good deeds remove bad deeds.'

Key Terms
ثواب
عقاب
استحقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That reward and punishment cannot be combined in any sense.
Correction: The Ash'ari argue that they can coexist in different respects and that good deeds have priority.

Checkpoint
What Quranic verse does the Ash'ari cite to support their position?
A) 'Indeed good deeds remove bad deeds'
B) 'Allah does not waste reward'
C) 'Whoever does evil will be requited'
D) 'Paradise is for the righteous'

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite objection fails because the incompatibility is not necessary, and the Quran explicitly states that good deeds erase bad deeds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your small, consistent good deeds can erase your major sins? What specific sin have you been holding onto that you now realize can be erased by focusing on good deeds? How will you shift your actions starting today?
?
Are you living as if the scales of justice are balanced against you, or have you internalized that Allah's mercy through good deeds overcomes your failings? What one good deed will you commit today to actively erase a past mistake?
?
If you knew that a single sincere good deed could wipe away a habit of disobedience, what would you do differently tomorrow? What is stopping you from acting on that knowledge right now?

Coexistence of Reward and Punishment: Mu'tazilite Objection and Sunni Response
Mu'tazilites argue reward and punishment cannot coexist; Sunnis refute with Quranic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that reward and punishment cannot coexist and the Sunni response using logical parity and the Quran.

Core Idea
Reward and punishment are not mutually exclusive; good deeds erase bad ones.

The Mu'tazilites raised a logical objection: if reward is pure perpetual benefit and punishment is pure perpetual harm, how can they be deserved by the same person? The Sunni response here uses both logical reasoning and a clear Quranic verse to refute this. Let's examine the argument and its refutation carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it too deeply. Then read the English translation to get the overall flow of the debate.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite premise and conclusion
Find where the Mu'tazilites state their definition of reward and punishment and their conclusion that they cannot be combined.
Pay attention to the words 'thawab' and 'iqab'.
3
Understand the Sunni concession and counter-argument
Notice that the Sunnis first concede the conditions of purity and perpetuity, then argue that it does not necessitate exclusion. Then they use a parity argument: invalidating good by bad is no more valid than the reverse.
4
Reflect on the Quranic proof text
Consider the verse 'Indeed good deeds erase bad deeds' and how it supports the Sunni position that good deeds can overcome bad ones, allowing for both reward and punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

That the act remains good and an act of obedience, and because it necessitates that a major sin contradicts the validity of obedience like apostasy. They said: Reward is a pure, perpetual benefit accompanied by glorification, and punishment is a pure, perpetual harm accompanied by humiliation, so they cannot be together as entitlement. We said: If the condition of purity and perpetuity is granted, it does not necessitate the mutual exclusion of the two entitlements, and if it is granted, then invalidating the good deed by the bad deed is not more appropriate than the opposite, especially since Allah the Exalted said: 'Indeed good deeds erase bad deeds.'
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage begins with a Sunni statement that a good act remains good and obedience, and that a major sin does not invalidate it like apostasy. Then the Mu'tazilites object that reward is pure perpetual benefit with glorification, while punishment is pure perpetual harm with humiliation, so they cannot both be deserved simultaneously. The Sunni response concedes the point about purity and perpetuity but argues that it does not necessitate mutual exclusion of entitlements. Even if it did, it is not more appropriate to cancel the good by the bad than the opposite. The Sunnis cite the Quranic verse 'Indeed good deeds erase bad deeds' to support their position.

Good deeds can erase bad deeds, allowing for the coexistence of reward and punishment in the same person.

Mu'tazilite Evidence for Ihbat and Its Refutation
Mu'tazilites cite Quranic verses; author critiques their insufficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Quranic verses cited by the Mu'tazilites are insufficient to prove their specific claim about ihbat.

Core Idea
The Quranic verses on nullification are too general to prove ihbat.

The Mu'tazilites attempt to support their doctrine of ihbat by quoting several Quranic verses that speak of deeds being nullified. However, as the author points out, these verses do not address the crux of the dispute: whether a single sin can nullify an entire good deed. In this lesson, we examine the evidence and identify its shortcoming.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the three Quranic references and the author's quick critique.
2
Identify the cited verses
Note each Quranic verse mentioned and what they literally say about nullification. The verses are general warnings against deeds becoming null.
The verses do not specify which deeds or under what conditions.
3
Understand the author's objection
The author argues that these verses do not establish the specific disputed claim: that a complete good deed is nullified by a prior or subsequent sin, nor do they address weighing the two sides.
The gap is between general nullification and the specific Mu'tazilite claim.
4
Recognize the logical gap
See that the verses only state that deeds can become null, but they don't prove that a single sin automatically nullifies a good deed, especially in a believer who has both.
This is a common point of contention between the Mu'tazilites and the Ash'aris.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستدلوا على الإحباط في الجملة بمثل قوله تعالى : أن تحبط أعمالكم (١) أولئك حبطت أعمالهم ) (۲) لا تبطلوا صدقاتكم بالمن والأذى) (۳) لكنه لا يثبت ما هو المتنازع من بطلان حسنة كاملة بسيئة سابقة أو لاحقة، فضلاً عن تفضيل الجانبين
And they adduced as evidence for ihbat in general such as His saying the Exalted: 'lest your deeds become null' (1), 'those, their deeds have become null' (2), 'do not nullify your charities by reproach and injury' (3). But it does not establish what is disputed: the nullification of a complete good deed by a prior or subsequent sin, let alone the weighing of the two sides.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite evidence for ihbat in general, citing three Quranic verses. The author then critiques that these verses do not prove the specific point of contention: that a single major sin nullifies a complete good deed, whether before or after it. The verses speak generally about nullification but do not address the case of a believer who has both good and bad deeds, nor do they show a weighing of the two sides.

Correction: The verses speak only generally about deeds becoming null; they do not show that a single sin nullifies a complete good deed or that the two sides are weighed.

Refuting Abu Ali's Ihbat: Injustice, Quran, and Preponderance
Refutation of Abu Ali's ihbat theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's rational and Quranic arguments against the Mu'tazilite view of ihbat, which nullifies prior good deeds.

Core Idea
Nullifying good deeds is unjust, contradicts the Quran, and lacks a rational basis.

Imam al-Razi begins his critique of the Mu'tazilite theories by first targeting Abu Ali's version of ihbat. He argues that nullifying prior obedience is unjust according to the Mu'tazilites' own principles, contradicts the Quranic promise of seeing even an atom's weight of good, and involves an arbitrary preponderance without a preponderator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for Abu Ali's view
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Imam al-Razi presents two distinct arguments against Abu Ali: one from injustice and Quran, and one from preponderance without a preponderator.
The Arabic phrase 'الترجح بلا مرجح' is key to understanding the logical flaw.
2
Analyze the first argument: injustice and Quran
Identify why nullifying previous obedience is considered injustice (ظلم) by the Mu'tazilites themselves, and how the Quranic verse (99:7) refutes the idea of total nullification.
The verse implies that every good deed will be seen, contradicting the annihilation of good deeds.
3
Analyze the second argument: preponderance without a preponderator
Understand the concept of 'tarajjuh bila murajjih' – that there is no reason to prefer the nullification of good over sin, making the theory arbitrary.
This is a standard kalam argument against any claim of arbitrary preference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستدل الإمام على بطلان، أما على رأي أبي علي فلأنه تلغو الطاعة السابقة، وهو ظلم عندكم . وينتفى بقوله تعالى : فمن يعمل مثقال ذرة خيراً يره) مع ما فيه من الترجح بلا مرجح وأما على رأي أبي هاشم فلأن طر أن الحادث مشروط بزوال السابق، فزواله به دور لأنه لا أولوية لبعض أجزاء الكبير، فيلزم أن يفنى بكليته، ولأن زوال كل بالآخر دفعة يوجب وجودهما حال عدمهما لوجود العلة حال حدوث المعلول. وعلى التعاقب يوجب حدوث المعلول بلا علته.
And the Imam argued for its invalidity: As for Abu Ali's view, because it nullifies the prior obedience, and that is injustice according to you. And it is refuted by His saying the Exalted: 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' (99:7), along with what is in it of preponderance without a preponderator. And as for Abu Hashim's view, because it is [apparent?] that the later event is conditioned by the removal of the prior, so its removal by it is circular because there is no priority for some parts of the greater, so it necessarily follows that it is entirely annihilated. And because the removal of each by the other at once necessitates their existence at the time of their non-existence, due to the existence of the cause at the occurrence of the effect. And sequential removal necessitates the occurrence of the effect without its cause.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Razi's rational arguments against both Mu'tazilite views. First, against Abu Ali's pure ihbat: if a prior good deed is nullified by a later sin, that is injustice, and it contradicts the Quranic verse that even an atom's weight of good will be seen. Moreover, it involves preponderance without a preponderator (tarajjuh bila murajjih), meaning there is no reason to prefer one over the other. Second, against Abu Hashim's muwazana: if a later sin is conditioned upon the removal of the prior good deed, then its removal by the sin is circular because there is no priority among parts of the greater deed. Consequently, the entire good deed must be annihilated. Also, if each removes the other simultaneously, that would require them to exist at the moment of their non-existence, which is absurd. If the removal is sequential, then the later removal would occur without its cause, which is also absurd.

Key Terms
ظلم
ترجح بلا مرجح
أبو علي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is one reason Imam al-Razi gives against Abu Ali's theory of ihbat?
A) It contradicts the Quranic verse about seeing good deeds.
B) It involves a circular dependency.
C) It requires simultaneous existence and non-existence.
D) It violates the principle of causality.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi refutes Abu Ali's ihbat by showing it is unjust, contradicts the Quran, and involves an arbitrary preponderance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you assume that your good deeds will outweigh your sins, relying on a mental accounting? This lesson refutes the notion of automatic cancellation, but does that comfort you or challenge you to take each deed more seriously? What will you change in how you perform your daily acts of worship after knowing that every atom of good is seen and rewarded?
?
The Imam's argument uses both Quran and reason. Are you equally committed to grounding your beliefs on both revelation and rational consistency? When have you held a belief that felt good but lacked rational or scriptural support? What might you need to re-examine today?
?
Consider the verse 'Whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it.' Does this fill you with hope or with fear, knowing that even the smallest good is recorded? What small good deed have you been neglecting that you now feel compelled to perform?

Refuting Abu Hashim's Muwazana: Circularity and Absurdity
Refutation of Abu Hashim's muwazana theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's logical arguments showing that Abu Hashim's muwazana (balancing) theory leads to circularity and logical contradictions.

Core Idea
Muwazana leads to circularity, simultaneous existence and non-existence, and effects without causes.

After refuting Abu Ali's version, Imam al-Razi turns to Abu Hashim's more sophisticated theory of muwazana, where good and bad deeds cancel each other out partially. He presents three devastating logical arguments: a circular dependency between the prior good and later sin, an absurdity if removal happens simultaneously, and another absurdity if removal is sequential.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for Abu Hashim's view
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Identify the three distinct arguments: circularity, simultaneous removal, and sequential removal.
The text uses 'دور' (circularity) and 'علة' (cause) and 'معلول' (effect).
2
Analyze the argument from circularity
Understand why the later sin's existence being conditioned on the removal of the prior good deed leads to a circular dependency (دور).
The lack of priority among parts of the greater deed leads to total annihilation.
3
Analyze the simultaneous and sequential removal arguments
Examine why simultaneous removal requires existence at the time of non-existence, and sequential removal produces an effect without its cause.
Both are logically impossible according to kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the Imam argued for its invalidity: As for Abu Ali's view, because it nullifies the prior obedience, and that is injustice according to you. And it is refuted by His saying the Exalted: 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' (99:7), along with what is in it of preponderance without a preponderator. And as for Abu Hashim's view, because it is [apparent?] that the later event is conditioned by the removal of the prior, so its removal by it is circular because there is no priority for some parts of the greater, so it necessarily follows that it is entirely annihilated. And because the removal of each by the other at once necessitates their existence at the time of their non-existence, due to the existence of the cause at the occurrence of the effect. And sequential removal necessitates the occurrence of the effect without its cause.

This passage presents Imam al-Razi's rational arguments against both Mu'tazilite views. First, against Abu Ali's pure ihbat: if a prior good deed is nullified by a later sin, that is injustice, and it contradicts the Quranic verse that even an atom's weight of good will be seen. Moreover, it involves preponderance without a preponderator (tarajjuh bila murajjih), meaning there is no reason to prefer one over the other. Consequently, the entire good deed must be annihilated. Also, if each removes the other simultaneously, that would require them to exist at the moment of their non-existence, which is absurd. ?
Imam al-Razi shows that removing a good deed by a later sin leads to absurdities. What does this teach you about the seriousness of sin—especially sins committed after good deeds? Is there a specific good deed you have undone by a subsequent sin that you need to repent from right now?
?
The argument relies on causality and logical necessity. Are you convinced by logical proofs in matters of faith, or do you tend to rely on feelings? How can this deductive reasoning strengthen your certainty about Allah's justice, even when you cannot see the full picture?
