Ihbat as Legal Ruling, Not Literal Nullification
Objection and response on legal nature of ihbat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational objection to the concept of ihbat and the Ash'ari response that reinterprets it as a legal consideration rather than real causal nullification.

Core Idea
Ihbat is a legal ruling, not a physical cancellation of good deeds.

This lesson addresses a crucial objection against the concept of ihbat (nullification of good deeds by sins). Critics argue that entitlements are mere legal constructs with no real causal power, so talk of nullification is misguided. The response clarifies that ihbat simply means Allah withholds reward due to sin, not that the good deed is literally destroyed. Imam al-Haramayn adds a further point about the supreme value of knowing Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the passage that begins with the objection. Note the claim that entitlement (istihqaq) is merely a legal consideration with no real effect.
The objection uses the terms 'istihqaq', 'i'tibar shar'i'.
2
Understand the response
The response reinterprets 'ihbat' and 'muwazana' as legal outcomes, not literal annihilation. Identify the new definitions given.
Ihbat means Allah withholds reward; muwazana means He balances sin and good deeds.
3
Reflect on Imam al-Haramayn's point
Consider the argument that no major sin's weight exceeds the reward of knowing Allah, forcing the Mu'tazilites to an absurd conclusion.
This shows the importance of ma'rifat Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض بأن الاستحقاق اعتبار شرعي ليس له تأثير وتأثر حقيقي. والثواب والعقاب إنما يوجدان في الآخرة، والفعلان لا يتصور فناء أحدهما بالآخر . بل معنى الإحباط أن الله تعالى لا يثيب العاصي على الطاعة . ومعنى الموازنة أنه لا يثيب عليهما، ويترك العقاب على المعصية بقدرها . وقال إمام الحرمين : لا كبيرة يربى وزرها على أجر معرفة الله، فيلزمهم أن يدرأوا بها جميع الكبائر
And it was objected that entitlement is a legal consideration, having no real effect or being affected. And reward and punishment are only found in the afterlife, and the two acts cannot be conceived to annihilate one another. Rather, the meaning of ihbat is that Allah the Exalted does not reward the disobedient for the obedience. And the meaning of muwazana is that He does not reward for either, and He leaves punishment for the sin to its extent. And Imam al-Haramayn said: No major sin whose evil consequence exceeds the reward of knowing Allah, so they would be compelled to ward off all major sins by it.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the entire discussion of ihbat: that entitlement (istihqaq) is merely a legal consideration (i'tibar shar'i) with no real causal efficacy, so the talk of nullification is misguided. Reward and punishment are only enacted in the afterlife, and acts themselves cannot literally annihilate each other. The response then reinterprets ihbat and muwazana: ihbat means that Allah does not reward the sinner for his good deed because of the sin; muwazana means He neither rewards nor punishes fully, but balances. Then Imam al-Haramayn adds that no major sin's evil weight can outweigh the reward of knowing Allah (ma'rifat Allah), so the Mu'tazilites would have to claim that knowing Allah wipes out all sins, which is absurd.

Key Terms
استحقاق
اعتبار شرعي
إحباط
موازنة
إمام الحرمين
معرفة الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think ihbat means literal annihilation of good deeds by sins.
Correction: Ihbat is a legal decision by Allah not to reward, not a literal destruction of the good deed.

Checkpoint
According to the response to the objection, what does ihbat actually mean?
A) The good deed is literally destroyed by the sin.
B) Allah does not reward the sinner for the obedience due to the sin.
C) The sin increases the punishment.
D) The good deed and sin cancel each other out automatically.

Key Takeaway
Ihbat does not mean literal annihilation of good deeds; it is Allah's decision not to reward due to sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own good and bad deeds, do you assume they cancel out like a ledger? How does the truth that ihbat is a legal ruling by Allah change your hope and fear regarding your sins? Will you let this truth drive you to rely more on Allah's mercy than on a false arithmetic?
?
Imam al-Haramayn says no major sin outweighs the reward of knowing Allah. But honestly, what does your daily schedule reveal about your actual priority—are you truly seeking ma'rifat Allah more than avoiding sins? What one thing will you change today to make knowing Allah your supreme concern?
?
If ihbat is only a legal decision, then your good deeds are not automatically nullified by sin. Does this comfort you, or does it make you less careful? What would it mean for you to live with both hope in Allah's mercy and fear of His justice, without falling into presumption or despair?

Sunni Position on Mixed Deeds and Refutation of Ihbat
Consensus on clear fates; Sunni position on mixed deeds with refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni consensus on the final fate of believers and disbelievers, and the Sunni argument that a person with both good and bad deeds ultimately enters Paradise, with a refutation of the Mu'tazilite doctrine of nullification.

Core Idea
Both good and bad deeds matter, but faith ultimately secures Paradise.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid presents the clear Sunni position on eschatological outcomes. The author first states the consensus on simple cases—a believer enters Paradise, a disbeliever enters Hell—then addresses the complex case of a person with mixed deeds. The Sunni view affirms that such a person will ultimately be saved, even if punishment occurs first, and that good deeds are not nullified by sins. The Mu'tazilite contrary view is refuted with both scriptural and rational arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Note the structure: two clear cases, then the disputed case.
The author uses 'لا خلاف في' to introduce consensus matters.
2
Identify the three eschatological cases
The passage describes three scenarios: a believer after disbelief, a disbeliever after faith, and a person with mixed deeds. Notice which are agreed upon and which is disputed.
The first two are consensus; the third is the main discussion.
3
Understand the Sunni position on the mixed case
The Sunni position is that the person's final abode is Paradise, even if he enters Hell first for purification. His entitlements to reward and punishment remain intact.
This refutes the Mu'tazilite idea of total nullification.
4
Examine the Mu'tazilite argument and Sunni refutation
The Mu'tazila claim that a single major sin nullifies all good deeds, leading to eternal Hell. The author refutes this with Quranic verses and a rational analogy about a servant.
The analogy shows the absurdity of nullifying a lifetime of good for one mistake.
5
Reflect on the rational analogy
Consider the analogy of a servant who serves a generous master for a hundred years and then disobeys once. Would it be just to treat him as an enemy? This illustrates the Sunni argument.
This highlights the wisdom and justice of Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا خلاف في أن من آمن بعد الكفر والمعاصي ، فهو من أهل الجنة، بمنزلة من لا معصية له . ومن كفر - نعوذ بالله - بعد الإيمان والعمل الصالح فهو من أهل النار، بمنزلة من لا حسنة له وإنما الكلام فيمن آمن وعمل صالحاً وآخر سيئاً، واستمر على الطاعات والكبائر كما يشاهد من الناس، فعندنا مآله إلى الجنة، ولو بعد النار، واستحقاقه للثواب والعقاب بمقتضى الوعد والوعيد ثابت من غير حبوط. والمشهور من مذهب المعتزلة أنه من أهل الخلود في النار إذا مات قبل التوبة، فأشكل عليهم الأمر في إيمانه وطاعاته وما ثبت من استحقاقاته، أين طارت؟ وكيف زالت ؟ فقالوا بحبوط الطاعات . ومالوا إلى ان السيئات يذهبن الحسنات، حتى ذهب الجمهور منهم إلى أن الكبيرة الواحدة تحبط ثواب جميع العبادات، وفساده ظاهر، إما سمعاً فللنصوص الدالة على أن الله تعالى لا يضيع أجر من أحسن عملاً. وإما عقلاً فللقطع بأنه لا يحسن من الحكيم الكريم إبطال ثواب إيمان العبد ومواظبته على الطاعات طول العمر بتناول لقمة من الربا أو جرعة من الخمر، بمنزلة من خدم كريماً مائة سنة حق الخدمة ، ثم بدت منه مخالفة أمر من أوامره، فهل يحسن رفض حقوق تلك الخدمات ونقض ما عهد ووعد من الحسنات، وتعذيبه عذاب من واظب مدة الحياة على المخالفة والمعاداة
There is no dispute that whoever believes after disbelief and sins, he is among the people of Paradise, like one who has no sin. And whoever disbelieves—Allah forbid—after faith and righteous work, he is among the people of Hell, like one who has no good deed. The discussion is only about one who believed and did righteous work and also committed evil, and continued in acts of obedience and major sins as seen among people. According to us, his final abode is Paradise, even if after Hell. And his entitlement to reward and punishment according to the promise and threat is established without nullification. The well-known position of the Mu'tazila is that he is among the eternally abiding in Hell if he dies before repentance. Thus they were perplexed about his faith, his acts of obedience, and the established entitlements: where did they go? How did they disappear? So they said by the nullification of acts of obedience. And they inclined to the idea that evil deeds erase good deeds, until the majority of them held that a single major sin nullifies the reward of all acts of worship. Its corruption is apparent: from scripture, due to the texts indicating that Allah the Exalted does not waste the reward of one who does good work; and from reason, due to the certainty that it is not fitting from the Wise and Generous to invalidate the reward of a servant's faith and his persistence in acts of obedience all his life by consuming a morsel of usury or a sip of wine—like one who served a generous man for a hundred years with proper service, then a violation of one of his commands appeared from him. Would it be fitting to reject the rights of those services and break what was covenanted and promised of good deeds, and to punish him with the punishment of one who persisted in disobedience and enmity all his life?
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the consensus on two clear cases: a believer after disbelief enters Paradise, and a disbeliever after faith enters Hell. The disputed case is the person who has both good and bad deeds. The Sunni position is that his final destination is Paradise, even if he must be purified in Hell first, and his entitlements to reward and punishment remain without nullification. The Mu'tazilites hold that such a person will be eternally in Hell if he dies unrepentant, which led them to ask what happened to his good deeds—hence their theory of ihbat. The author then refutes this with both scriptural evidence (Quranic verses that Allah does not waste the reward of the doer of good) and rational analogy: it is absurd to think a lifetime of good deeds is nullified by a single sin, like a servant who serves a master for a hundred years and is then punished as an enemy for one mistake.

كفر
جنة

خلود
إحباط
وعد
وعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Sunni position implies that sin does not matter and everyone ends up in Paradise regardless.
Correction: The Sunni position affirms that punishment may occur in Hell for sins before entering Paradise, and that entitlement to punishment remains; it is not a license to sin.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazilite view on the fate of a person who died with both faith and major sins without repentance?
A) He is temporarily in Hell then goes to Paradise.
B) He is eternally in Hell.
C) His good deeds are completely cancelled, but he still enters Paradise.
D) He enters Paradise immediately.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position maintains that good deeds are never wasted; even with sins, a believer's ultimate end is Paradise, though punishment may precede it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This passage uses an analogy of a servant serving a master faithfully for a hundred years. In what area of your life might you be treating Allah as if He is quick to nullify your efforts, rather than as the Generous Lord He is? What needs to change in your heart?

Why Major Sins Do Not Nullify Good Deeds
The author argues that good deeds retain their goodness regardless of sin, so their reward cannot be nullified.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that major sins do not nullify the reward of good deeds, using two rational arguments.

Core Idea
Good deeds remain good and compliant even with sin; their reward cannot be nullified.

In this passage, the author presents two concise rational arguments against the Mu'tazilite doctrine of ihbat (nullification of reward by sin). The first argument focuses on the intrinsic quality of good deeds, while the second draws a contrast with apostasy. These arguments help solidify the Ash'ari position that sin does not cancel out the reward of obedience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the flow.
Notice the two separate arguments marked by 'wa-ayḍan' and 'wa-ayḍan'.
2
Identify the two arguments
Find the first argument about the nature of good deeds and the second argument comparing major sins to apostasy.
3
Understand the logic of each argument
For the first argument: reward is due to goodness and compliance, which remain despite sin. For the second: if major sin nullified reward, it would be like apostasy, but it isn't.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why this distinction matters for the Ash'ari view on sin, faith, and reward.
This supports the idea that major sins do not make one an unbeliever.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وايضاً استحقاق الثواب على الطاعة عندهم إنما هو لكونها حسنة وامتثالاً لأمر الباري. وهذا متحقق مع الكبيرة، فيتحقق أثره ، وأيضاً لو كانت الكبيرة محبطة لثواب الطاعة ، لكانت منافية لصحتها بمنزلة الردة
Also, the entitlement to reward for obedience according to them is only because it is good and an act of compliance with the command of the Creator. And this is realized along with the major sin, so its effect is realized. Also, if the major sin were to nullify the reward of obedience, it would be contradictory to its validity, like apostasy.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage adds two rational arguments. First, the reason for deserving reward is that the act is good (hasana) and an act of compliance (imtithal) with Allah's command. This quality remains even when a major sin is committed, so the effect (reward) should remain. Second, if a major sin could nullify the reward of obedience, it would be like apostasy (ridda), which nullifies all good deeds. But a major sin is not apostasy, so it should not nullify the reward. This shows that the Mu'tazilite position is inconsistent.

Key Terms
حسنة
امتثال
كبيرة
ردة
ثواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any sin, even a major one, nullifies all previous good deeds.
Correction: The author argues that major sins do not nullify reward because the good deed remains good and compliant; only apostasy nullifies all deeds.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why can't a major sin nullify the reward of a good deed?
A) Because good deeds are rewarded only in the afterlife.
B) Because the good deed remains good and compliance with Allah's command.
C) Because the sin is smaller than the good deed.
D) Because Allah promises to forgive all sins except shirk.

Key Takeaway
The reward of a good deed is based on its inherent goodness and compliance with Allah's command, which sin does not erase, unlike apostasy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Is there any sin in your life that you are treating as if it cuts you off from Allah completely, forgetting that your acts of obedience still count? What step can you take today to remind yourself of Allah's mercy while never belittling sin?

Defining Major Sins: Two Key Criteria
Two conceptual definitions of a major sin (kabira)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main conceptual definitions of a major sin (kabira) and why both are significant in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
A major sin is defined by its indication of religious indifference or by a specific divine threat.

This passage opens the conclusion on major sins. It presents two distinct yet complementary ways to identify a kabira: one based on the sin's inner quality (showing disregard for religion) and one based on external textual threat. Understanding both criteria helps us distinguish major from minor sins.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Read the Arabic carefully. Notice the two definitions separated by 'Wa qeel' (and it is said).
2
Analyze the first definition
The first definition defines a major sin as an act that indicates a lack of concern for religion. This is a qualitative measure.
Think about what kind of sin would show disregard for the entire religion.
3
Analyze the second definition
The second definition ties a major sin to a specific threat from the Lawgiver. This is a textual measure.
Now consider how both definitions work together.
4
Reflect on utility
Ask yourself: Why might scholars offer two definitions? How does each help in categorizing sins?
This connects to the broader purpose of identifying major sins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة الكبيرة المعصية التي تشعر بعلة الاكتراث بالدين . وقيل : التي توعد عليها الشارع بخصوصها. وقيل الشرك(۳) ، والقتل(٤)، والقذف(٥)، والزنا (٦) والفرار من الزحف والسحر، وأكل مال اليتيم والعقوق والإلحاد في الحرم، وقد يراد أكل الربا والسرقة وشرب الخمر .
Conclusion: The major sin (kabira) is the disobedience that indicates the reason for concern about religion. And it is said: that for which the Lawgiver has threatened specifically. And it is said: shirk (3), and murder (4), and slander (5), and fornication (6), and fleeing from the battle, and sorcery, and consuming the wealth of the orphan, and disobedience to parents, and heresy in the Sacred Sanctuary. And it may be intended: consuming usury, and theft, and drinking wine.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion (khatima) that defines the major sin (kabira). It provides three definitions: first, a sin that indicates a disregard for religion (i.e., shows the sinner's lack of concern). Second, a sin for which the Lawgiver (Shari') has specifically threatened punishment (e.g., a specific punishment in the Quran or hadith). Third, an enumeration of specific major sins: shirk (associating partners with Allah), murder, slander of chaste women, fornication, fleeing from the battlefield, sorcery, consuming the wealth of orphans, disobedience to parents, and heresy in the Sacred Sanctuary (Mecca). The passage also notes that some include consuming usury, theft, and drinking wine among major sins. This list serves as a practical reference for identifying major sins, though the author does not claim it is exhaustive.

Key Terms
كبيرة
المعصية
الشارع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think a major sin is only defined by severe punishment alone.
Correction: The passage shows that there is also a qualitative definition: a sin that indicates disregard for religion, regardless of specified punishment.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a definition of a major sin (kabira)?
A) Disobedience that indicates lack of concern for religion
B) Disobedience for which the Lawgiver has specifically threatened
C) Disobedience that is committed publicly
D) Both A and B are definitions

Key Takeaway
A major sin is defined either by its intrinsic indication of religious indifference or by a specific divine threat, providing both ethical and textual measures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you feel more accountable because of a threat in scripture or because your heart has become indifferent to Allah? Which weighs heavier on your conscience?
?
The first definition ties major sin to a lack of concern for religion. Honestly examine past sins: did any of them reveal that you simply did not care at that moment? How can you guard your heart against that indifference starting today?
?
If Allah judged your sins only by the second definition (specific threats), you might feel safe from some sins. But what does your daily negligence of small acts of worship say about your level of concern? Will you change something this week to show you care?

The Objection to Resolve as a Condition for Repentance
Presents the condition, objection, and answer with Imam al-Haramayn's support.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to requiring resolve (al-azm) for repentance when the sin is inconceivable or impossible, and the answer that resolve is conditional on ability.

Core Idea
Resolve is not always required for repentance; it is conditional on the possibility of repeating the sin.

Some theologians add resolve to never repeat the sin as a condition for repentance. But what if the sin cannot possibly be repeated due to madness, death, or physical incapacity? In this lesson, we examine the objection and the answer from Imam al-Haramayn, showing that resolve is only required when one has the ability to sin again.

Guided Path
1
Read the condition and objection
Read the first few lines of the passage: 'وقد يزاد قيد العزم...' and the objection that the sin may not occur to the mind or may be impossible due to incapacity.
Notice the examples: muteness for slander, paralysis for fornication.
2
Focus on the answer
Read the answer: 'فأجيب بأن المراد العزم على الترك على تقدير الخطور والاقتدار'. Understand that resolve is meant to be on the supposition of occurrence and ability.
3
Study Imam al-Haramayn's statement
Read the quote from Imam al-Haramayn. Note his distinction that resolve only applies when one is capable of the act; a eunuch cannot resolve to abandon fornication.
This is a key authority supporting the conditional view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يزاد قيد العزم على ترك المعاودة في المستقبل. واعترض بأن فعل المعصية في المستقبل قد لا يخطر بالبال لذهول أو جنون أو موت ونحو ذلك . وقد لا يقتدر عليه لعارض آفة كخرس في القذف وشلل أوجب في الزنا، فلا يتصور العزم على الترك لما فيه من الإشعار بالقدر والاختيار. فأجيب بأن المراد العزم على الترك على تقدير الخطور والاقتدار ، حتى لو سلب القدرة لم يشترط العزم على الترك. بهذا يشعر كلام إمام الحرمين حيث قال : إن العزم على ترك المعاودة إنما يقارن التوبة في بعض الأحوال، ولا يطرد في كل حال، إذا العزم إنما يصح فيمن يتمكن من مثل ما قدمه ولا يصح من المجبوب العزم على ترك الزنا، ولا من الأخرس العزم على ترك القذف فما ذكر في المواقف من أن قولنا : إذا قدر لأن من سلب القدرة على الزنا، وانقطع طمعه عن عود القوة إذا عزم على تركه ، لم يكن ذلك توبة منه ، ليس على ما ينبغي لإشعاره بأن العزم على الترك يصح مع عدم القدرة على الفعل وبأن الندم على الفعل مع العزم على الترك لا يكفي في التوبة، لكن لا بد من أمر ثالث هو بقاء القدرة وكلام الإمام وغيره أن عند عدم القدرة لا يشترط في التوبة العزم بل لا يصح ويكفي مجرد الندم . لا يقال : مراد المواقف أن مجرد هذا العزم بدون الندم ليس بتوبة، لأنا نقول : هذا لغو من الكلام، لا بيان لفائدة التقييد بالقدرة. وقد يتوهم أن تقديره القدرة قيد للترك، لا للعزم . أي يجب العزم على أن لا يفعل على تقدير القدرة حتى يجب على من عرض له الآفة أن يعزم على أن لا يفعل لو فرض وجود القدرة بهذا يشعر ما قال في المواقف أن الزاني المجبوب إذا ندم وعزم على أن لا يعود على تقدير القدرة، فهو توبة عندنا، خلافاً لأبي هاشم . ثم التحقيق أن ذكر العزم إنما للتقدير والبيان لا التقييد والاحتراز. إذ النادم على المعصية لقبحها لا يخرج
And the condition of resolve (al-azm) to leave off returning (to the sin) in the future may be added. And it was objected that the commission of the sin in the future may not occur to the mind due to heedlessness, insanity, death, or the like. And sometimes one is not able to do it due to an impediment of a defect, like muteness in the case of slander, or paralysis that prevents in the case of fornication. So resolve to abandon is inconceivable because it implies ability and choice. So it was answered that what is meant is resolve to abandon on the supposition of occurrence and ability, so that even if ability is removed, resolve to abandon is not required. The speech of Imam al-Haramayn indicates this, where he said: 'Indeed, the resolve to leave off returning accompanies repentance only in some circumstances, and does not apply in every case. For resolve is only valid for someone who is able to do something like what he previously did; it is not valid for a eunuch to resolve to abandon fornication, nor for a mute to resolve to abandon slander.' Thus what is mentioned in al-Mawaqif that 'our statement: if he is able... because one who is deprived of ability to fornicate and whose hope of returning of strength is cut off, if he resolves to abandon it, that is not repentance from him' is not as it should be, because it suggests that resolve to abandon is valid despite lack of ability to act, and that regret for the deed along with resolve to abandon is insufficient for repentance, but a third thing is necessary—the persistence of ability. But the speech of Imam and others is that when ability is absent, resolve (al-azm) is not required for repentance; rather it is not valid, and mere regret suffices. It should not be said: The meaning of al-Mawaqif is that this mere resolve without regret is not repentance, for we say: This is idle speech, no clarification of the benefit of restricting by ability. And it may be understood that the supposition of ability is a qualification of the abandonment, not of the resolve. That is, one must resolve not to do it on the supposition of ability, so that one who has an impediment is obliged to resolve not to do it if existence of ability were assumed. This is indicated by what is said in al-Mawaqif: 'If a eunuch fornicator regrets and resolves not to return on the supposition of ability, it is repentance according to us, contrary to Abu Hashim.' Then the verification is that the mention of resolve is only for estimation and clarification, not for restriction and delimitation. For the one who regrets the sin for its ugliness does not leave...
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage addresses whether resolve (al-azm) to never repeat the sin is a condition for repentance. It begins by noting that some scholars add this condition. Then it presents an objection: future sin may not even cross one's mind due to forgetfulness, insanity, or death; or one may be physically incapable, like a eunuch regarding fornication or a mute regarding slander. In such cases, resolve is impossible because it implies ability. The answer is that the intended resolve is conditional—on the supposition that the thought occurs and one has ability. If one lacks ability, resolve is not required. Imam al-Haramayn's statement is cited: resolve accompanies repentance only in some circumstances; it is not universal—a eunuch cannot validly resolve to abandon fornication. The text then critiques al-Mawaqif for implying that resolve is valid even without ability and that regret plus resolve is insufficient, requiring persistence of ability. The correct view, from Imam al-Haramayn and others, is that when ability is absent, resolve is neither required nor valid; mere regret suffices. The author dismisses a possible misinterpretation of al-Mawaqif as idle speech, then clarifies that the condition of ability is a qualifier of the abandonment, not of the resolve. He quotes al-Mawaqif itself to show that it agrees: a eunuch's resolve on supposition of ability is repentance. Finally, the author concludes that mentioning resolve is only for estimation and clarification, not as a necessary condition, because the genuinely regretful person already intends not to sin—the text ends mid-sentence but the point stands.

Correction: The passage shows that resolve is not required when one cannot physically or mentally repeat the sin; mere regret suffices.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Haramayn, why is a eunuch not required to resolve to abandon fornication?
A) Because a eunuch already regrets the sin.
B) Because a eunuch cannot physically commit fornication, so resolve is impossible.
C) Because a eunuch is not a sinner.
D) Because resolve is not required for any sin.

Key Takeaway
Resolve to abandon sin is only required when one has the ability and opportunity to commit it; otherwise, mere regret suffices for repentance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever avoided repentance because you felt you couldn't promise never to sin again, even though that sin is no longer accessible to you? How does this lesson relieve you of that false burden?
?
Consider a sin you committed but are now physically unable to repeat (illness, change of circumstance). Are you truly forgiven without a formal resolve? How does this affect your trust in Allah's mercy?
?
When you repent, do you instinctively demand a perfect, unshakeable resolve from yourself? Will you now examine whether that demand is even relevant to your situation, or is your regret enough to bring you back to Allah?

Critique of al-Mawaqif and the Final Position on Resolve
Critiques al-Mawaqif and establishes that resolve is not a condition but for clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critique of al-Mawaqif's position and the final verification that resolve is only for estimation, not as a necessary condition.

Core Idea
The mention of resolve in repentance is for clarification, not as a strict condition.

After establishing that resolve is conditional on ability, the author turns to critique a statement in al-Mawaqif that seemed to imply otherwise. This lesson unpacks the critique, clarifies the correct view of Imam al-Haramayn, and concludes that resolve is mentioned only for estimation—because the truly regretful person naturally intends not to sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique of al-Mawaqif
Read from 'فما ذكر في المواقف' to 'ليس على ما ينبغي'. Note the author's objection that al-Mawaqif's phrasing suggests resolve is valid without ability and that regret plus resolve is insufficient.
2
Understand the correct view
Read the clarification: 'وكلام الإمام وغيره أن عند عدم القدرة لا يشترط...'. The correct view is that when ability is absent, resolve is neither required nor valid; mere regret suffices.
This is from Imam al-Haramayn and others.
3
Resolve a misinterpretation
Consider the misinterpretation that al-Mawaqif meant resolve without regret is not repentance. The author dismisses this as idle talk. Then read the alternative understanding that ability is a qualifier of the abandonment, not of the resolve.
4
Final verification
Read the concluding 'ثم التحقيق' section. Understand that mentioning resolve is only for estimation and clarification, because a person who truly regrets sin naturally avoids it.
The text cuts off but the point is clear.

The Four Shari'ah Definitions of Iman
Author classifies iman into four shari'ah definitions and mentions the sects associated with each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four ways the Shari'ah defines iman (faith) and which sects hold each view.

Core Idea
Iman can be defined by heart, tongue, or both—and that choice shapes theology.

In this passage, the author presents a classification of the different definitions of iman within Islamic law. He outlines four possible scopes for iman: an act of the heart alone, the tongue alone, both heart and tongue without limbs, or all three together. Each position is associated with various theological schools.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four categories
The author lists four possible definitions based on what iman is considered to be. Note each one.
2
Understand the first two views
The first view (heart) has two sub-definitions: tasdiq (majority) and ma'rifah (Shia, etc.). The second view (tongue) is iqrar with conditions.
3
Note the third and fourth views
The third view combines heart and tongue. The fourth includes actions, with three positions: Khawarij (action required), Mu'tazila (middle position), Salaf (foundation salvation).
4
Reflect on the significance
Think why these definitions matter—they affect who is considered a believer.

According to the first view, it is a name for tasdiq (affirmation) according to the majority, meaning affirmation of the Prophet (peace be upon him) in what is known necessarily to have come from him. And for ma'rifah (knowledge) according to the Shia, Jahm, and al-Salihi. According to the third view, it is for the combination of tasdiq and iqrar, and upon this are most of the verifiers, except that often in their expressions they place ma'rifah or 'ilm (knowledge) or i'tiqad (belief) in place of tasdiq. According to the fourth view, it is for iqrar by the tongue, tasdiq by the heart, and action by the limbs, either making the abandoner of action outside of iman, inside of kufr — and upon this are the Khawarij — or not inside it — and upon this are the Mu'tazila, differing as to whether the actions are the performance of obligations and avoidance of prohibitions, or absolute acts of obedience — or not making him outside, and upon this are the majority of the Salaf. And it is transitive with the ba' (ب) to consider the meaning of iqrar and confession, as in His exalted saying: 'The Messenger believes in what has been revealed to him', and with the lam (ل) to consider the meaning of submission and acceptance, as in His exalted saying in the narrative: 'And you are not a believer in us, even if we were truthful.' And since in reality it returns to taking a thing as truthful, and truthfulness is described of the speaker, and speech and judgment are attached to a thing by different considerations, such as: I believe in Allah, meaning that He is one, qualified with what befits Him, transcendent above what does not befit Him; and I believe in the Messenger, meaning that he is sent by Allah Almighty, truthful in what he brought; and I believe in His angels, meaning that they are His honored servants, obedient, infallible, not described with masculinity or femininity, not daughters of Allah nor His partners; and I believe in His books and His words, meaning that they are sent down from Allah, truthful in what they contain of rulings; and I believe in the Last Day, meaning that it will certainly occur; and I believe in divine decree, meaning that good and evil are by Allah's decree and will; and the reference of all is to acceptance and recognition. And as for in the Shari'ah, opinions differed concerning the reality of iman and whether it is a name for the act of the heart only, or the act of the tongue only, or both of them alone, or with the rest of the limbs. And these are four ways. Then according to the first view, it may be made a name for tasdiq. I mean the affirmation of the Prophet concerning what is known necessarily to have come from him. That is, concerning what is known to be part of the religion, such that one knows it without need for reflection and deduction, like the oneness of the Creator, the obligation of prayer, the prohibition of wine, and the like. And general knowledge suffices for what is observed generally, and detail is required for what is observed in detail, so that if one does not affirm the obligation of prayer when asked about it, and the prohibition of wine when asked about it, he is a disbeliever. And this is the well-known view, and upon it are the majority. And it may be made a name for ma'rifah. I mean knowledge of what we mentioned, and it includes knowledge of Allah Almighty by His oneness and all that befits Him, and His transcendence above what does not befit Him. And this is the school of the Shia, Jahm ibn Safwan, and Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi from the Qadariyya. And al-Ash'ari tends toward it. And you will know the difference between ma'rifah and tasdiq. Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification and analysis of the various definitions of iman (faith) in the Shari'ah. The author first concludes the linguistic meaning (the last part of a preceding discussion) and then presents the four possible shari'ah definitions based on what iman is considered to be: an act of the heart only, an act of the tongue only, both heart and tongue without limbs, or all three including limbs. He then elaborates each view with the opinions of different sects and scholars.

For the first view (heart only), the majority hold that iman is tasdiq (affirmation) of the Prophet in matters known necessarily from him, while the Shia, Jahm ibn Safwan, and al-Salihi define it as ma'rifah (knowledge). For the second view (tongue only), it is iqrar (confession) but with different conditions: al-Ruqashi requires ma'rifah, al-Qattan requires tasdiq, and the Karramiyya require no condition. For the third view (heart and tongue), it is the combination of tasdiq and iqrar, held by most muhaqqiqun, though they often use 'knowledge' or 'belief' interchangeably with tasdiq. For the fourth view (heart, tongue, and limbs), there are further subdivisions: the Khawarij consider abandoning actions as exiting iman into kufr; the Mu'tazila consider the grave sinner neither inside iman nor kufr but in a middle position, differing on whether actions are only obligatory acts and prohibitions or all acts of obedience; the majority of the Salaf (including Malik and al-Shafi'i) hold that the sinner is not outside iman—iman may refer to the foundation of salvation (the heart's affirmation) or the complete saving faith (including actions).

The author then discusses the linguistic root of the verb 'āmana' (to believe), noting its transitivity with different prepositions (bi- for confession, li- for submission) and giving examples from the Quran for beliefs in Allah, the Prophet, angels, books, the Last Day, and divine decree, all returning to acceptance and recognition.

Finally, he reiterates the four shari'ah ways and elaborates further on the first view. He defines tasdiq as affirmation of what is necessarily known from the Prophet, such as the oneness of the Creator, obligation of prayer, and prohibition of wine. He notes that general affirmation suffices for general matters, but detailed belief is required for detailed matters—disbelief in a necessarily known matter like the obligation of prayer when asked is kufr. He mentions that the majority adhere to this. He then discusses the alternative view of ma'rifah, held by the Shia, Jahm ibn Safwan, and Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi, and notes that al-Ash'ari inclines toward it. The passage ends mid-sentence with a promise to explain the difference between ma'rifah and tasdiq.

The First View: Iman as Tasdiq vs. Ma'rifah
Author elaborates on the first view of iman, defining tasdiq and ma'rifah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed definition of iman as tasdiq (affirmation) and its criteria, as well as the alternative definition as ma'rifah (knowledge).

Core Idea
The first shari'ah definition of iman is either affirmation of necessary matters or knowledge of God's attributes.

After listing the four definitions, the author elaborates on the first view, which limits iman to an act of the heart. He explains that it is primarily understood as tasdiq (affirmation) of the Prophet in necessarily known matters, but some scholars define it as ma'rifah (knowledge) of God and His attributes.

Guided Path
1

2

The author explains that general affirmation suffices for general matters, but detailed belief is required for detailed matters. Denying a necessary belief when asked can lead to disbelief.
3
Learn about the alternative: ma'rifah
Some groups, like the Shia and Jahm, define iman as knowledge (ma'rifah). This includes knowledge of God's oneness and attributes.
The author notes that al-Ash'ari tends toward this view.

According to the first view, it is a name for tasdiq (affirmation) according to the majority, meaning affirmation of the Prophet (peace be upon him) in what is known necessarily to have come from him. And these are four ways. Then according to the first view, it may be made a name for tasdiq. I mean the affirmation of the Prophet concerning what is known necessarily to have come from him. That is, concerning what is known to be part of the religion, such that one knows it without need for reflection and deduction, like the oneness of the Creator, the obligation of prayer, the prohibition of wine, and the like. And general knowledge suffices for what is observed generally, and detail is required for what is observed in detail, so that if one does not affirm the obligation of prayer when asked about it, and the prohibition of wine when asked about it, he is a disbeliever. And this is the well-known view, and upon it are the majority. And it may be made a name for ma'rifah. I mean knowledge of what we mentioned, and it includes knowledge of Allah Almighty by His oneness and all that befits Him, and His transcendence above what does not befit Him. And this is the school of the Shia, Jahm ibn Safwan, and Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi from the Qadariyya. And al-Ash'ari tends toward it. And you will know the difference between ma'rifah and tasdiq. Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification and analysis of the various definitions of iman (faith) in the Shari'ah. The author first concludes the linguistic meaning (the last part of a preceding discussion) and then presents the four possible shari'ah definitions based on what iman is considered to be: an act of the heart only, an act of the tongue only, both heart and tongue without limbs, or all three including limbs. He then elaborates each view with the opinions of different sects and scholars.

For the first view (heart only), the majority hold that iman is tasdiq (affirmation) of the Prophet in matters known necessarily from him, while the Shia, Jahm ibn Safwan, and al-Salihi define it as ma'rifah (knowledge). For the second view (tongue only), it is iqrar (confession) but with different conditions: al-Ruqashi requires ma'rifah, al-Qattan requires tasdiq, and the Karramiyya require no condition. For the third view (heart and tongue), it is the combination of tasdiq and iqrar, held by most muhaqqiqun, though they often use 'knowledge' or 'belief' interchangeably with tasdiq. For the fourth view (heart, tongue, and limbs), there are further subdivisions: the Khawarij consider abandoning actions as exiting iman into kufr; the Mu'tazila consider the grave sinner neither inside iman nor kufr but in a middle position, differing on whether actions are only obligatory acts and prohibitions or all acts of obedience; the majority of the Salaf (including Malik and al-Shafi'i) hold that the sinner is not outside iman—iman may refer to the foundation of salvation (the heart's affirmation) or the complete saving faith (including actions).

The author then discusses the linguistic root of the verb 'āmana' (to believe), noting its transitivity with different prepositions (bi- for confession, li- for submission) and giving examples from the Quran for beliefs in Allah, the Prophet, angels, books, the Last Day, and divine decree, all returning to acceptance and recognition.

Finally, he reiterates the four shari'ah ways and elaborates further on the first view. He defines tasdiq as affirmation of what is necessarily known from the Prophet, such as the oneness of the Creator, obligation of prayer, and prohibition of wine. He notes that general affirmation suffices for general matters, but detailed belief is required for detailed matters—disbelief in a necessarily known matter like the obligation of prayer when asked is kufr. He mentions that the majority adhere to this. He then discusses the alternative view of ma'rifah, held by the Shia, Jahm ibn Safwan, and Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi, and notes that al-Ash'ari inclines toward it. The passage ends mid-sentence with a promise to explain the difference between ma'rifah and tasdiq.

The Linguistic Meaning of Iman
Al-Taftazani defines iman linguistically as tasdiq and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic meaning of iman as 'taking something as true' and see how it applies to different objects of faith.

Core Idea
Iman linguistically means accepting something as true, not just verbal confession.

Before exploring the precise legal definition of iman, Al-Taftazani begins with its linguistic root. He explains that iman involves an inner conviction of truthfulness (tasdiq), and then illustrates this with examples of belief in God, angels, and other fundamentals. This lesson unpacks that linguistic foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Taftazani defines iman and then provides examples. Let the examples illustrate the core meaning.
The passage opens with a Quranic quote (Surah Yusuf, verse 17).
2
Identify the linguistic meaning
Look for the phrase 'taking a thing as truthful'. That is the linguistic essence of iman: tasdiq (affirmation of truth).
3
Examine the examples
Go through each example: God, Messenger, angels, books, Last Day, destiny. For each, notice how the specific meaning of iman is defined according to the object.
This shows that the core act (tasdiq) is common, but the details vary.
4
Reflect on the transition
The passage ends this section by stating that all these boil down to 'acceptance and acknowledgment'. This bridges to the sharia discussion.
This linguistic foundation is the starting point for the scholarly debate.

Commentary

The Four Sharia Views on Iman
Al-Taftazani lists the four scholarly views on the sharia definition of iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four scholarly positions on the definition of iman in Islamic law.

Core Idea
Iman in the Sharia is disputed between heart, tongue, and deeds.

After establishing the linguistic meaning, Al-Taftazani turns to the Sharia definition of iman, where scholars disagree. He outlines four main views: that iman is an act of the heart only, an act of the tongue only, both, or both plus limbs. This lesson presents these four approaches as the foundation for further discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the sharia passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second part of the source. Notice the transition from linguistic to legal.
2
Identify the four views
List out the four possibilities mentioned: (1) heart only, (2) tongue only, (3) heart and tongue, (4) heart, tongue, and limbs.
The text says 'these are four ways'.
3
Understand the significance
Consider why this disagreement matters: the definition of iman determines who is a believer and the relationship between faith and actions.
This sets up detailed discussion in subsequent lessons.
4
Connect to linguistic meaning
Note that all four views share the concept of tasdiq from the linguistic definition, but differ on what else is required.
The linguistic meaning is the common ground.

We, and if we were truthful) (1). And because it in reality returns to taking a thing as truthful, and truthfulness is an attribute of the speaker, and speech and judgment are attached to a thing in different respects, such as: I believed in God, meaning that He is One, described with what befits Him, transcendent above what does not befit Him; and I believed in the Messenger, meaning that he is sent by God Most High, truthful in what he brought; and I believed in His angels, meaning that they are His honored obedient servants, infallible, not described as male or female, not daughters of God nor His partners; and I believed in His books and His words, meaning that they are revealed from God, truthful in what they contain of rulings; and I believed in the Last Day, meaning that it will certainly happen; and I believed in destiny, meaning that good and evil are by God's decree and will, and the reference of all is to acceptance and acknowledgment. As for in the Sharia, opinions differed concerning the reality of iman and whether it is a name for an act of the heart only, or an act of the tongue only, or for both of them, either alone or along with the other limbs. And these are four ways. (1) Surat Yusuf, verse 17.

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the linguistic definition of iman (faith). The author notes that linguistically, iman involves taking something as true (ṣidq). He then provides examples of the various objects of faith: belief in God, His messenger, angels, books, the Last Day, and divine decree. For each, he specifies the correct understanding—for example, belief in God means affirming His oneness, His attributes of perfection, and His transcendence. After this, he turns to the sharia (religious law) definition, noting that scholars differed on whether iman is primarily an act of the heart (e.g., ḥdq assent or knowledge), an act of the tongue (verbal confession), both, or both plus actions of the limbs. He states that there are four approaches, setting up the detailed discussion that follows.

Correction: The passage shows that one view restricts iman to the heart, and others combine heart and tongue, so inner conviction is at least part of the definition.

The First View: Iman as Tasdiq or Ma'rifa
Al-Taftazani details the first school: iman as tasdiq (assent) or ma'rifa (knowledge), and briefly mentions taslim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two major interpretations of iman in the Sharia—tasdiq (assent) and ma'rifa (knowledge)—and the conditions attached to tasdiq.

Core Idea
The majority define iman as heart-assent to necessary religious truths.

Al-Taftazani begins his detailed analysis of iman by presenting the first school of thought. This school itself splits into two main camps: those who equate iman with tasdiq (assent of the heart) and those who define it as ma'rifa (knowledge of God and His attributes). The majority of theologians follow the tasdiq view, with specific conditions for general and detailed matters. A third opinion, taslim (submission), is also mentioned but ultimately reduces to tasdiq.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow. Notice how the author first describes tasdiq with examples and conditions, then presents ma'rifa, and finally taslim.
The passage is dense—focus on the definitions of each term.
2
Identify the three definitions of iman
The passage mentions three ways iman is understood: tasdiq, ma'rifa, and taslim. Underline each mention and note who holds each view.
The majority holds tasdiq; the Shi'a and others hold ma'rifa; taslim is a minority view.
3
Understand the conditions for tasdiq
Notice that tasdiq is not vague—it applies to matters known necessarily from the Prophet. It requires detail for detailed matters and general assent for general matters. Failure to assent to a known obligation (e.g., prayer when asked) constitutes disbelief.
This shows that tasdiq is comprehensive and specific.
4
Reflect on the difference between tasdiq and ma'rifa
The author promises a later distinction between the two. For now, note that ma'rifa is broader, encompassing knowledge of God's oneness and attributes. The Shi'a and Jahm hold this view.
Both are about internal conviction, but their scope differs.
5
Consider the taslim opinion
The passage mentions a third view that iman is taslim (submission), but scholars of verification say it reduces to tasdiq. This shows how debates about iman often converge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعلى الأول قد يجعل اسماً للتصديق . أعني تصديق النبي فيما علم مجيئه به بالضرورة. أي فيما اشتهر كونه مع الدين. بحيث يعلمه من غير افتقار إلى نظر واستدلال كوحدة الصانع ووجوب الصلاة، وحرمة الخمر، ونحو ذلك . ويكفي الإجمال فيما يلاحظ إجمالاً. ويشترط التفصيل فيما يلاحظ تفصيلا، حتى لو لم يصدق بوجوب الصلاة عند السؤال عنه. وبحرمة الخمر عند السؤال عنه كان كافراً . وهذا هو المشهور. وعليه الجمهور. وقد يجعل اسماً للمعرفة. أعني معرفة ما ذكرناه، ويتناول معرفة الله تعالى بوحدانيته وسائر ما يليق به وتنزهه عما لا يليق به وهو مذهب الشيعة (٢) ، وجهم بن صفوان وأبي الحسين الصالحي من القدرية. وقد يميل إليه الأشعري. وستعرف فرقاً بين المعرفة والتصديق. ومن الناس من يكاد يقول بأنه اسم لمعنى آخر غير المعرفة والتصديق هو التسليم. إلا أنه يعود بالآخرة إلى التصديق على ما يراه أهل التحقيق . (۲) الشيعة : هم الذين شايعو علياً - رضي الله عنه على الخصوص، وقالوا بإمامته وخلافته نصاً ووصيه إما جلياً وإما خفياً، واعتقدوا أن الإمامة لا تخرج من أولاده وإن خرجت فبظلم يكون من غيره أو بتقية من عنده، وقالوا ليست الإمامة بقضية مصلحة بل قضية أصولية. راجع الملل والنحل ١: ١٤٦
As for the first approach, it is sometimes made a name for tasdiq (assent), meaning assent to the Prophet regarding what is known necessarily that he brought, i.e., regarding what is widely known as part of the religion, such that it is known without need for reflection and inference—like the oneness of the Creator, the obligation of prayer, the prohibition of wine, and the like. Sufficiency of general belief suffices for what is regarded generally, and detail is required for what is regarded in detail, such that if one does not assent to the obligation of prayer when asked about it, or to the prohibition of wine when asked about it, he becomes a disbeliever. And this is the well-known view, and upon it is the majority. And it is sometimes made a name for ma'rifa (knowledge), meaning knowledge of what we mentioned, and it encompasses knowledge of God Most High with His oneness and all that befits Him and His transcendence above what does not befit Him. This is the madhhab of the Shi'a (2), Jahm bin Safwan, and Abu al-Husayn al-Salihi from the Qadariyya. And al-Ash'ari inclines to it. And you will learn a difference between ma'rifa and tasdiq. And among people there is one who almost says that it is a name for another meaning besides ma'rifa and tasdiq, which is taslim (submission). However, it ultimately returns to tasdiq according to what the scholars of verification hold. (2) The Shi'a: they are those who supported Ali specifically, and said his imamate and caliphate are by explicit designation and testament, either clear or hidden, and believed that imamate does not leave his descendants, and if it leaves then by injustice from others or by taqiyya from him, and they said that imamate is not a matter of benefit but a matter of principle. See al-Milal wa al-Nihal 1:146.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage discusses the first approach to defining iman in the Sharia. The author explains two main opinions within this approach. First, the majority hold that iman is tasdiq (assent) of the heart regarding matters known necessarily from the Prophet—i.e., core teachings like the oneness of God, obligation of prayer, prohibition of wine. This tasdiq must be detailed for matters known in detail, and general for matters known in general. Failure to assent to a specific obligation when asked constitutes disbelief. Second, some (e.g., the Shi'a, Jahm, al-Salihi, and Ash'ari in inclination) define iman as ma'rifa (knowledge) of God's oneness and attributes, which is broader than tasdiq. The author notes there is a difference between ma'rifa and tasdiq, and a third opinion—taslim (submission)—ultimately reduces to tasdiq. The footnote defines the Shi'a as those who hold to the designation of 'Ali.

Iman as Verbal Confession: Three Sub-Views
Al-Taftazani presents three sub-views on iman as verbal confession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the view that iman is verbal confession and the three different conditions attached to it by al-Raqqashi, al-Qattan, and the Karramiyya.

Core Idea
Iman as confession is not just a word; its meaning changes with what is required alongside it.

In this passage, al-Taftazani presents the second major approach to defining iman: that it is an act of the tongue, specifically verbal confession (iqrar). However, scholars differed on whether confession alone suffices or must be accompanied by inner knowledge or assent. By examining three sub-views, we see how these nuances affect the definition and practical rulings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then compare with the translation to understand the overall argument.
Notice the three distinct positions introduced by 'wa qad yushtaratū' phrases.
2
Identify the three conditions
List the three positions: (1) with knowledge (ma'rifa) but knowledge is involuntary, (2) with tasdiq, (3) with no internal condition.
Each position is attributed to a specific scholar or group.
3
Understand al-Raqqashi's reasoning
He claims knowledge is necessary and occurs inevitably, so it is not part of the acquired act of iman. Confession alone is iman.
This is a subtle point: knowledge is required but not included in the definition.
4
Understand al-Qattan's view
He requires tasdiq; confession alone is not iman, but when both are present, iman is the confession.
Here, iman is identified with the confession, not the combined act.
5
Understand the Karramiyya view
They require only confession for worldly rulings. Inward disbelief leads to punishment; hidden faith without confession does not earn paradise.
This view separates worldly judgment from ultimate destiny.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى الثاني، وهو أن يجعل اسماً لفعل اللسان. أعني الإقرار بحقية ما جاء به النبي عليه السلام). وقد يشترط معه معرفة القلب، حتى لا يكون الإقرار بدونها إيماناً. وإليه ذهب الرقاشي زاعماً أن المعرفة ضرورية يوجد لا محالة. فلا يجعل من الإيمان لكونه اسماً لفعل مكتسب، لا ضروري. وقد يشترط التصديق وإليه ذهب القطان ، (۱) وصرح بأن الإقرار الخالي عن المعرفة والتصديق لا يكون إيماناً. وعند اقترانه بهما يكون الإيمان هو الإقرار فقط . وقد لا يشترط شيء منهما . وإليه ذهب الكرامية، حتى إن من أضمر الكفر، وأظهر الإيمان، يكون مؤمناً، إلا أنه يستحق الخلود في النار. ومن أضمر الإيمان وأظهر الكفر، لا يكون مؤمناً. ومن أضمر الإيمان، ولم يتفق منه الإظهار والإقرار لم يستحق الجنة. وإذا تحققت فليس لهؤلاء الفرق الثلاث كثير خلاف في المعنى وفيما يرجع إلى الأحكام . (۱) هو يحيى بن سعيد بن فروخ القطان التميمي بو سعيد من حفاظ الحديث الثقة حجة من أقران مالك وشعبة من أهل البصرة كان يفتي بقول أبي حنيفة وأورد له البلخي سقطات ولم يعرف له تأليف إلا ما في كشف الظنون من أن له كتاب المغازي. قال أحمد بن حنبل : ما رأيت بعيني مثل يحيى القطان توفي عام ۱۹۸ هـ راجع تذكرة الحفاظ ١ : ٢٧٤ وتهذيب التهذيب ٢١٦:١١ وتاريخ بغداد ١٤ : ١٣٥ وكشف الظنون ١٤٦ والعبر للذهبي ۱ : ۳۲۷.
And as for the second approach, it is to make it a name for an act of the tongue, namely iqrar (confession) of the truth of what the Prophet (peace be upon him) brought. And it is sometimes conditioned together with it knowledge (ma'rifa) of the heart, so that confession without it is not iman. This was the view of al-Raqqashi, claiming that knowledge is necessary and occurs inevitably, so it is not made part of iman because iman is a name for an acquired act, not a necessary one. And sometimes tasdiq (assent) is conditioned with it; this was the view of al-Qattan (1), who explicitly stated that confession devoid of knowledge and assent is not iman, but when they accompany it, iman is only the confession. And sometimes nothing from them is conditioned; this was the view of the Karramiyya, such that if one conceals disbelief and manifests faith, he is a believer, but he deserves eternal punishment in the Fire. And if one conceals faith and manifests disbelief, he is not a believer. And if one conceals faith and does not give expression and confession, he does not deserve Paradise. And if you investigate, there is not much difference among these three groups in meaning and in what pertains to rulings. (1) He is Yahya bin Sa'id bin Farukh al-Qattan al-Tamimi, Abu Sa'id, a narrator of Hadith, trustworthy, a proof, of the peers of Malik and Shu'ba from Basra; he gave fatwa according to Abu Hanifa's opinion; al-Balkhi recorded slips from him; he is not known for any work except what is in Kashf al-Zunun that he has a book on Maghazi. Ahmad bin Hanbal said: 'I have not seen with my eye anyone like Yahya al-Qattan.' He died in 198 AH. See Tadhkirat al-Huffaz 1:274, Tahdhib al-Tahdhib 11:216, Tarikh Baghdad 14:135, Kashf al-Zunun 146, and al-'Ibar for al-Dhahabi 1:327.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents the second approach to defining iman: that it is primarily an act of the tongue, i.e., verbal confession (iqrar). Within this approach, there are three sub-views. The first, held by al-Raqqashi, requires that confession be accompanied by ma'rifa (knowledge) of the heart, but he considers knowledge as necessary and involuntary, hence not part of iman proper. The second, held by al-Qattan, requires tasdiq (assent) along with confession; confession alone is not iman, but when both are present, iman is identified with confession. The third, held by the Karramiyya, requires no internal state; external confession suffices for iman in this world, but the person may be punished in the afterlife if they conceal disbelief. The passage notes that despite these differences, the three groups agree on many practical rulings. The footnote provides a biography of Yahya al-Qattan.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Karramiyya view implies that hypocrisy is acceptable and a hypocrite is fully a believer in every sense.
Correction: The Karramiyya do consider the outward hypocrite a believer in worldly terms but affirm he deserves eternal punishment for hidden disbelief.

Key Takeaway
The view of iman as confession is not uniform; the presence or absence of required inner states significantly affects its meaning and rulings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Al-Raqqashi distinguished necessary knowledge from acquired confession. Is your faith a matter of mere intellectual knowledge or a conscious act you choose to affirm? Pinpoint one area where your confession is not backed by deliberate choice.
?
Al-Qattan insisted that confession without tasdiq is not iman. Do you live as if your verbal declarations are accompanied by genuine assent? Think of a moment today where your tongue said one thing but your heart wavered—what does that reveal about the reality of your iman?

The Third View: Iman as Heart and Tongue
Al-Taftazani presents the third view of iman and its soteriological consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third view of iman, which requires both inner assent and verbal confession, and its soteriological implications.

Core Idea
Iman requires both heart and tongue; confession is essential for salvation.

After presenting two earlier views, al-Taftazani now discusses the third and most comprehensive view: that iman is both an act of the heart (tasdiq) and an act of the tongue (iqrar). This view, attributed to Abu Hanifa and held by many investigators, carries significant consequences for who is considered a believer in God's sight. In this passage, we explore the definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage contrasts two views.
2
Identify the third view
The passage defines iman as 'tasdiq together with iqrar'. Find this definition and note who holds this view (many muhaqqiqun and Abu Hanifa).
Also note the interchangeable terms like ma'rifa, 'ilm, i'tiqad.
3
Understand the consequence
The passage states that without iqrar, one is not a believer and deserves eternal punishment. This is the key soteriological consequence.
Contrast this with the first view where iqrar is only for worldly rulings.
4
Compare with the first view
The passage explicitly contrasts the third view with the first view (iman as tasdiq only). Notice the different roles of iqrar in each.
The first view makes iqrar a condition for worldly rulings, not for faith itself.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why verbal confession might be considered essential for faith. This view emphasises the communal and testified nature of iman.
Think about the difference between inner belief and outward expression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى الثالث، وهو أن يكون اسماً لفعل القلب واللسان، فهو اسم للتصديق المذكور مع الاقرار وعليه كثير من المحققين، وهو المحكي عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى). وكثيراً ما يقع في عبارات النحارير من مكان التصديق تارة المعرفة وتارة العلم وتارة الاعتقاد فعلى هذا من صدق بقلبه، ولم يتفق له الإقرار باللسان في عمره مرة، لا يكون مؤمناً عند الله تعالى، ولا يستحق دخول الجنة ولا النجاة من الخلود في النار. بخلاف ما إذا جعل اسماً للتصديق فقط. فإن الإقرار حينئذ شرط لإجراء الأحكام في الدنيا من الصلاة عليه وخلفه والدفن في مقابر المسلمين، والمطالبة بالعشور والزكاة ونحو ذلك . ولا يخفى أن الإقرار بهذا الغرض لا
And as for the third approach, it is to be a name for an act of the heart and the tongue: it is a name for the aforementioned tasdiq together with iqrar. And upon this view are many of the investigators (muhaqqiqun), and it is reported from Abu Hanifa (may God have mercy on him). And often in the expressions of the eminent scholars (al-nahārīr), in place of tasdiq, sometimes ma'rifa, sometimes 'ilm, and sometimes i'tiqad appear. So on this basis, one who assents with his heart but does not happen to confess with his tongue even once in his life is not a believer in the sight of God Most High, nor does he deserve entry into Paradise nor salvation from eternal punishment. Unlike the case if iman is made a name for tasdiq only. For then iqrar is a condition for executing rulings in this world, such as praying over him, inheritance, burial in Muslim cemeteries, and the requirement of tithes and zakat, and the like. And it is not hidden that iqrar for this purpose is not...
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage explains the third view: that iman is both an act of the heart and an act of the tongue—i.e., tasdiq (assent) combined with iqrar (confession). Many investigators (muhaqqiqun) hold this, and it is attributed to Abu Hanifa. The author notes that in the works of eminent scholars, terms like ma'rifa, 'ilm, and i'tiqad are sometimes used interchangeably with tasdiq. The key consequence is that if iman requires both, then someone who has heart assent but never verbally confesses is not a believer in God's sight and deserves eternal punishment. By contrast, if iman were only tasdiq (the first view), then iqrar would be merely a condition for worldly rulings (e.g., prayer, inheritance, burial) but not essential for faith itself. The passage ends abruptly but clearly distinguishes the soteriological implications of the two definitions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that verbal confession is only for worldly rulings; the third view makes it essential for true iman.
Correction: According to the third view, iqrar is not just a worldly condition but an essential component of iman itself. Without it, one is not a believer in God's sight.

Checkpoint
According to the third view, what is the status of a person who believes in heart but never confesses with his tongue?
A) He is a believer and will enter Paradise
B) He is not a believer and deserves eternal punishment
C) He is a believer but must be treated as a non-Muslim in this world
D) He is a hypocrite

Key Takeaway
Iman, according to the third view, consists of both heart assent and tongue confession; without both, one is not a believer in God's sight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever treated your own recitation of the shahada as a mere formality, uttered without conscious engagement? If the third view is correct, your eternal salvation hinges on that confession being a genuine act of both heart and tongue—not just a ritual. What needs to change in how you say it?
?
If you truly believed that without a single lifetime confession you would not be saved, would you ever hesitate to declare your faith, even in a difficult situation? Is there any part of you that holds back, thinking heart belief alone is enough? What does that hesitation reveal about the state of your iman?
?
Reflect on your own practice: have you ever, even for a moment, thought that the words 'I believe' from your heart might suffice without ever speaking them? According to this passage, what is at stake for you personally, and what commitment will you make to ensure your tongue confirms what your heart holds?

Iman as Taslim (Submission) and Its Return to Tasdiq
The author defines iman as submission and clarifies its equivalence to assent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first definition of iman as submission (taslim) and why the verifiers ultimately consider it to be assent (tasdiq).

Core Idea
Iman is submission, but submission ultimately means assent of the heart.

The author begins by presenting the first of several definitions of iman. Some scholars define iman as 'submission' (taslim), a meaning distinct from knowledge and assent. However, the verifiers (ahl al-tahqiq) argue that submission ultimately reduces to assent. This lesson unpacks that subtle but important point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation. Notice the two parts: the definition and the clarification that it returns to tasdiq.
2
Understand the term 'taslim'
What does submission mean in this context? It implies surrendering one's will to Allah's commands.
This is a spiritual attitude.
3
Grasp the return to tasdiq
Why do the verifiers say taslim ultimately returns to tasdiq? Think about how genuine submission requires inner conviction.
4
Connect to the broader debate
This definition is not the final word; it sets up the discussion of iqrar in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بأنه اسم لمعنى آخر غير المعرفة والتصديق هو التسليم. إلا أنه يعود بالآخرة إلى التصديق على ما يراه أهل التحقيق . وعلى الثاني، وهو أن يجعل اسماً لفعل اللسان. أعني الإقرار بحقية ما جاء به النبي عليه السلام). وقد يشترط معه معرفة القلب، حتى لا يكون الإقرار بدونها إيماناً. وإليه ذهب الرقاشي زاعماً أن المعرفة ضرورية يوجد لا محالة. فلا يجعل من الإيمان لكونه اسماً لفعل مكتسب، لا ضروري. وقد يشترط التصديق وإليه ذهب القطان وصرح بأن الإقرار الخالي عن المعرفة والتصديق لا يكون إيماناً. وعند اقترانه بهما يكون الإيمان هو الإقرار فقط . وقد لا يشترط شيء منهما . وإليه ذهب الكرامية، حتى إن من أضمر الكفر، وأظهر الإيمان، يكون مؤمناً، إلا أنه يستحق الخلود في النار. ومن أضمر الإيمان وأظهر الكفر، لا يكون مؤمناً. ومن أضمر الإيمان، ولم يتفق منه الإظهار والإقرار لم يستحق الجنة. وإذا تحققت فليس لهؤلاء الفرق الثلاث كثير خلاف في المعنى وفيما يرجع إلى الأحكام .
That it is a name for a meaning other than knowledge (ma'rifa) and assent (tasdiq), namely submission (taslim). However, it ultimately returns to assent according to what the verifiers (ahl al-tahqiq) hold. And the second [view]: that it is made a name for the action of the tongue, i.e., verbal confession (iqrar) of the truth of what the Prophet (peace be upon him) brought. And sometimes with it is stipulated knowledge of the heart, so that confession without it is not faith (iman). This is the position of al-Raqqashi, claiming that knowledge is necessary and occurs inevitably; so it is not made part of faith because it is a name for an acquired action, not a necessary one. And sometimes assent is stipulated, and this is the position of al-Qattan, who declared that confession devoid of knowledge and assent is not faith. And when they accompany it, faith is only the confession. And sometimes neither of them is stipulated. This is the position of the Karamiyya, so that one who conceals disbelief and manifests faith is a believer, except that he deserves eternal punishment in the Fire. And one who conceals faith and manifests disbelief is not a believer. And one who conceals faith but does not manifest or confess it does not deserve Paradise. And if you reflect, there is not much disagreement among these three sects regarding the meaning and regarding the rulings.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents two major definitions of iman and three sub-views. The author begins by stating the first definition: iman is submission (taslim), but clarifies that according to the verifiers, submission ultimately reduces to assent (tasdiq). Then he moves to the second definition: iman is a name for the action of the tongue, i.e., verbal confession (iqrar). Within this second definition, there are three sub-views: (1) Al-Raqqashi stipulates that confession must be accompanied by knowledge of the heart, because knowledge is necessary and unacquired, so it cannot be part of the acquired act of iman. (2) Al-Qattan stipulates that confession must be accompanied by assent, so that confession without knowledge or assent is not iman; but when accompanied by them, iman is only the confession itself. (3) The Karamiyya stipulate neither, so that even one who inwardly disbelieves but outwardly professes is a believer, albeit deserving eternal punishment; one who inwardly believes but outwardly disbelieves is not a believer; and one who inwardly believes but never outwardly confesses does not deserve Paradise. The author concludes that these three sects do not differ much in the essential meaning and the resulting legal rulings.

Key Terms
الإيمان
التسليم
التصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think submission (taslim) is mere outward obedience without inner conviction.
Correction: The verifiers clarify that true submission is rooted in inner assent (tasdiq), so iman cannot be purely external.

Checkpoint
According to the verifiers (ahl al-tahqiq), what does submission (taslim) ultimately return to?
A) Knowledge
B) Assent
C) Confession
D) Ignorance

Key Takeaway
The first definition of iman as submission is not complete; it must be understood as ultimately being assent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may outwardly submit to Islamic rulings, but does your heart truly assent? How do you know the difference between mere compliance and genuine inner conviction? This lesson teaches that submission ultimately returns to assent. Examine your own iman: Is your submission merely outward, or does it spring from a heart that assents? What one thing will you change today to align your outward submission with inner tasdiq?
?
The author notes that according to the verifiers, even submission is ultimately assent. This means your actions are worthless if your heart is not convinced. In what areas of your life do you find a gap between your actions and your inner beliefs? How can you bridge that gap starting now?
?
Consider a situation where you outwardly obeyed Allah but inwardly felt doubt or reluctance. What does this lesson say about the reality of such an act? How can you transform your submission into genuine tasdiq? What step will you take this week to strengthen the connection between your limbs and your heart?

Iman as Iqrar (Verbal Confession) with Three Conditions
The author enumerates the second definition of iman and its three sub-views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second definition of iman as verbal confession and the three positions on what must accompany it.

Core Idea
Iman is confession by the tongue, but scholars differ on whether inner knowledge or assent is required.

After presenting the first definition, the author turns to a second major view: that iman is the action of the tongue, i.e., verbal confession (iqrar). Within this camp, three sub-views emerge regarding what else is required: some stipulate knowledge of the heart, some stipulate assent, and some stipulate neither. This lesson unpacks each position and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the full iqrar passage
Read the Arabic passage about iman as iqrar and its three conditions. Notice how the author presents each view with its proponent.
2
Understand al-Raqqashi's position
Al-Raqqashi stipulates knowledge (ma'rifa) alongside confession, but argues knowledge is not part of iman because it is necessary and unacquired.
He separates necessary inner states from acquired actions.
3
Understand al-Qattan's position
Al-Qattan stipulates assent (tasdiq) alongside confession. Without both, confession is not iman. But when both are present, iman is only the confession.
Interesting nuance: the inner state is not iman itself.
4
Understand the Karamiyya position
The Karamiyya stipulate neither. Their view leads to unusual rulings: a hidden disbeliever who professes faith is a believer (but deserves Hell), and a hidden believer who does not confess is not a believer.
This highlights the extreme focus on outward profession.
5
Reflect on the final note
The author concludes that the three groups do not differ much in meaning and rulings. Consider why that might be.
