Iman as Heart and Tongue: Abu Hanifa's View
The author presents the third definition of iman as assent and confession, attributed to Abu Hanifa, with its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third definition of iman as inner assent and verbal confession, its implications for one's status before Allah and in the community, and the reasoning behind judgments on cases like Abu Talib.

Core Idea
Iman requires both inner assent and verbal confession for salvation.

In this passage, the author presents the third of the definitions of iman, which combines the action of the heart and the tongue. This view, famously associated with Abu Hanifa, holds that verbal confession is an integral part of iman itself, not merely a condition for worldly rulings. The author explains the consequences of this position for the believer's status before Allah and in the community, and illustrates it with the controversial case of Abu Talib.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the overall argument. Notice the central definition and the flow of reasoning.
Pay attention to how the author contrasts this view with the one where confession is only for worldly rulings.
2
Identify the definition and its proponents
Find the definition of iman as 'action of the heart and tongue' (assent and confession). Note that it is attributed to Abu Hanifa and many verifiers. Also notice the alternative terms used (knowledge, science, belief).
These alternatives indicate the flexibility in terminology among scholars.
3
Understand the two roles of confession
The passage distinguishes between two purposes of confession: for completion of iman (which affects salvation) and for worldly rulings (which affect community life). Recognize that for worldly rulings, confession must be public, while for iman, mere utterance suffices.
This distinction is crucial for understanding later cases.
4
Analyze the special cases and the example of Abu Talib
Examine the rulings on the incapable (mute) and the obstinate refuser. Then see how the doctrine is applied to Abu Talib: despite his love for the Prophet, his lack of confession leads to the judgment of disbelief. Reflect on the author's critique of those who disagree.
This shows the practical application of the definition and its sectarian implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى الثالث، وهو أن يكون اسماً لفعل القلب واللسان، فهو اسم للتصديق المذكور مع الاقرار وعليه كثير من المحققين، وهو المحكي عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى. وكثيراً ما يقع في عبارات النحارير من مكان التصديق تارة المعرفة وتارة العلم وتارة الاعتقاد فعلى هذا من صدق بقلبه، ولم يتفق له الإقرار باللسان في عمره مرة، لا يكون مؤمناً عند الله تعالى، ولا يستحق دخول الجنة ولا النجاة من الخلود في النار. بخلاف ما إذا جعل اسماً للتصديق فقط. فإن الإقرار حينئذ شرط لإجراء الأحكام في الدنيا من الصلاة عليه وخلفه والدفن في مقابر المسلمين، والمطالبة بالعشور والزكاة ونحو ذلك . ولا يخفى أن الإقرار بهذا الغرض لا بد أن يكون على وجه الإعلان والإظهار على الإمام وغيره من أهل الإسلام. بخلاف ما إذا كان لإتمام الإيمان، فإنه يكفي مجرد التكلم، وإن لم يظهر على غيره. ثم الخلاف فيما إذا كان قادراً ، وترك التكلم، لا على وجه الإباء. إذ العاجز كالأخرس مؤمن وفاقاً. والمصر على عدم الإقرار مع المطالبة به كافر وفاقاً لكون ذلك من أمارات عدم التصديق. ولهذا أطبقوا على كفر أبي طالب وإن كابرت الروافض غير متأملين في أنه كان أشهر أعمام النبي اهتماماً عليه السلام، وأكثرهم به، وأوفرهم حرصاً من النبي عليه السلام على إيمانه فكيف اشتهر إيمان حمزة والعباس رضي الله عنهما، وشاع على رؤوس المنابر فيما بين الناس، وورد في إيمانهما الأحاديث المشهورة، وكثر منهما في الإسلام المساعي المشكورة دون أبي طالب.
And the third [view]: that it is a name for the action of the heart and the tongue, so it is a name for the aforementioned assent along with confession. This is the position of many of the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) and is reported from Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him). Frequently in the expressions of the eminent scholars, in place of 'assent' one finds sometimes 'knowledge', sometimes 'science', and sometimes 'belief'. Accordingly, whoever assents with his heart but does not happen to confess with his tongue even once in his lifetime is not a believer in the sight of Allah, and does not deserve entry into Paradise nor salvation from eternal punishment in the Fire. Contrary to the case where [iman] is made a name for assent only. For then confession is a condition for the application of worldly rulings, such as praying over him, inheriting from him, burying him in Muslim cemeteries, and demanding tithes and alms and the like. And it is not hidden that confession for this purpose must be in a public and manifest manner before the imam and other Muslims. Contrary to the case where it is for the completion of iman, for then mere utterance suffices, even if not manifested to others. Then there is disagreement regarding one who is able but refrains from speaking, not out of obstinacy. For the incapable, like the mute, is a believer by consensus. And the one who persists in refusing to confess despite being called to do so is a disbeliever by consensus, because that is an indication of lack of assent. For this reason, they unanimously held Abu Talib to be a disbeliever, even though the Rawafid (Rafidis) stubbornly [deny this], not reflecting that he was the most attentive of the Prophet's uncles, the most concerned for him, and the most eager for the Prophet's faith. How then did the faith of Hamza and al-Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them) become famous, spread from the pulpits among the people, and well-known hadiths about their faith exist, and their praiseworthy efforts in Islam became numerous, unlike Abu Talib?
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This lesson presents the third definition of iman: that it is a name for both inner assent (tasdiq) and outward confession (iqrar). This is the position of many verifiers and is attributed to Abu Hanifa. The author notes that in the expressions of the eminent scholars, 'assent' is sometimes replaced by 'knowledge', 'science', or 'belief'. He then deduces the ruling: one who assents in heart but never confesses with his tongue is not a believer before Allah and will not enter Paradise or be saved from eternal punishment. This is opposed to the view that iman is only assent, where confession is only a condition for worldly rulings such as funeral prayers, inheritance, burial, and payment of zakat. The author distinguishes between two purposes of confession: for worldly rulings, it must be public and manifest to the ruler; for completion of iman, mere utterance suffices even if not known to others. Then he discusses cases: the incapable (mute) is a believer by consensus; the one who obstinately refuses to confess despite ability is a disbeliever by consensus because it indicates lack of assent. As an example, he cites the unanimous judgment that Abu Talib, despite his care for the Prophet, was a disbeliever, because he never confessed. He criticizes the Rawafid who deny this, noting that the faith of the Prophet's uncles Hamza and al-Abbas is well-known and supported by hadith, unlike Abu Talib.

أبو حنيفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can be a true believer in the sight of Allah without ever verbally confessing iman.
Correction: According to this view, verbal confession is an integral part of iman; lack of it prevents salvation and indicates lack of true faith.

Checkpoint

A) He is a believer in this world and the next
B) He is a believer in this world but not in the next
C) He is not a believer in the sight of Allah and deserves eternal punishment
D) He is a hypocrite

Key Takeaway
According to Abu Hanifa, iman requires both inner assent and verbal confession; confession is necessary for salvation, not just for worldly rulings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Abu Talib, who loved and protected the Prophet, is still a disbeliever because he never uttered the words of faith, what does that mean for your own shahada? Do you say it regularly with full consciousness that it is your only ticket to salvation, or has it become a mere habit?
?
The lesson says that for salvation, a private utterance of confession suffices. But ask yourself: if you were alone and about to die, would you confess iman? Is there any hidden doubt or pride that might hold your tongue?
?
You have the ability to speak clearly, unlike the mute who is excused. Are you truly grateful for this ability? Or do you waste your tongue on gossip and argument, neglecting the one utterance that secures your eternity?

Iman as Action of Heart, Tongue, and Limbs: Three Positions
The author presents the fourth definition of iman and the three positions on the status of one who abandons action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three positions on the status of one who abandons action under the definition of iman that includes actions of the heart, tongue, and limbs.

Core Idea
Iman includes action, but scholars disagree on whether abandoning action takes one out of faith.

This lesson explores the fourth definition of iman, which incorporates outward actions alongside inward belief. Different theological schools have drawn different conclusions about the status of a person who fails to perform the required actions. We will examine the positions of the Khawarij, Mu'tazila, and the majority of early Muslims, along with an apparent difficulty in the majority view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the three positions and the accompanying discussion.
Pay attention to the sequence: definition, Khawarij, Mu'tazila, majority, and the ishkal.
2
Understand the fourth definition of iman
Note that iman is defined as confession by the tongue, assent by the heart, and action by the limbs. This sets the stage for the debate.
This definition includes all three components.
3
Analyze the Khawarij position
The Khawarij hold that abandoning action removes one from iman and makes one a disbeliever. This is the extreme view.
4
Analyze the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila hold an intermediate status, neither believer nor disbeliever. They differ on which actions are required: Abu Ali and Abu Hashim say obligatory acts, while Abu al-Hudhayl and Abd al-Jabbar include supererogatory acts. The author criticizes the latter as irrational.
Notice the internal disagreement within the Mu'tazila.
5
Analyze the majority position and the ishkal
The majority (Salaf, hadith imams, many mutakallimun, Malik, Shafi'i, Awza'i) hold that the one who abandons action is still a believer and will enter Paradise. However, the author points out an apparent difficulty: if action is part of the definition, how can its absence not negate faith?
This iskhal is left unresolved in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما على الرابع، وهو أن يكون الإيمان اسماً لفعل القلب واللسان والجوارح على ما يقال إنه إقرار باللسان وتصديق بالجنان، وعمل بالأركان . فقد يجعل تارك العمل خارجاً عن الإيمان، داخلاً في الكفر، وإليه ذهب الخوارج، أو غير داخل فيه وهو القول بالمنزلة بين المنزلتين، وإليه ذهب المعتزلة ، إلا أنهم اختلفوا في الأعمال، فعند أبي علي وأبي هاشم ، فعل الواجبات وترك المحظورات وعند أبي الهذيل وعبد الجبار، فعل الطاعات واجبة كانت أو مندوبة، إلا أن الخروج عن الإيمان، وحرمان دخول الجنة بترك المندوب مما لا ينبغي أن يكون مذهباً لعاقل. وقد لا يجعل تارك العمل خارجاً عن الإيمان، بل يقطع بدخول الجنة، وعدم خلوده في النار، وهو مذهب أكثر السلف ، وجميع أئمة الحديث، وكثير من المتكلمين، والمحكى عن مالك والشافعي والأوزاعي، وعليه إشكال ظاهر وهو أنه كيف لا ينتفي الشيء؟
And as for the fourth [view], that iman is a name for the action of the heart, tongue, and limbs, as it is said: it is confession (iqrar) by the tongue, assent (tasdiq) by the heart, and action ('amal) by the limbs. Then [according to this view] one who abandons action may be considered outside of iman and entering into kufr, and this is the position of the Khawarij; or [he is] not entering into it, and this is the saying of the intermediate status (al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn), held by the Mu'tazila, except that they differed regarding the [required] actions: according to Abu Ali and Abu Hashim, [it is] performing obligatory acts and avoiding prohibited acts; according to Abu al-Hudhayl and Abd al-Jabbar, [it is] performing acts of obedience, whether obligatory or supererogatory; except that leaving the fold of iman and being deprived of entering Paradise due to abandoning a supererogatory act is not a position a rational person should hold. And [some] do not consider the one who abandons action as outside of iman, but rather decisively affirm his entry into Paradise and his not being eternally in the Fire. This is the position of most of the Salaf, all of the imams of hadith, and many of the mutakallimun, and it is reported from Malik, al-Shafi'i, and al-Awza'i. However, there is an apparent difficulty: how is it that the thing is not negated?
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth definition of iman, which includes action of the heart, tongue, and limbs. The author then explains three positions taken by different sects regarding the status of one who abandons action. First, the Khawarij hold that such a person leaves iman and enters kufr. Second, the Mu'tazila hold the intermediate position (al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn), neither believer nor disbeliever, but they differ on which actions are required: Abu Ali and Abu Hashim say obligatory acts and avoidance of prohibitions; Abu al-Hudhayl and Abd al-Jabbar include all acts of obedience, even supererogatory. The author comments that expelling from iman and denying Paradise due to abandoning a supererogatory act is irrational. Third, the majority of the Salaf, all hadith imams, and many mutakallimun hold that the one who abandons action does not leave iman; rather, he is definitely a believer who will enter Paradise and not be eternally punished. This is reported from Malik, al-Shafi'i, and al-Awza'i. The author notes an apparent difficulty (ishkal) in this third view: if action is part of the definition, how can the absence of a part not negate the whole? The passage ends with this question, leaving it for further discussion.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Mu'tazila and Khawarij hold the same view on the status of a sinner.
Correction: The Mu'tazila hold an intermediate status (al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn) — neither believer nor disbeliever — while the Khawarij consider the sinner a disbeliever. They are distinct.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, what is the status of a Muslim who abandons obligatory actions?
A) He is a disbeliever
B) He is in an intermediate status between belief and disbelief
C) He remains a believer but will be punished in Hell eternally
D) He is a hypocrite

Key Takeaway
Under the definition of iman that includes action, the Khawarij, Mu'tazila, and majority offer three distinct positions on the status of one who abandons action, with the majority view facing an apparent logical difficulty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a religious duty you have been neglecting. When you commit that neglect, do you feel your faith is weakened? Which of the three positions in this lesson most accurately reflects your own view—and does that comfort you or disturb you? Will you make a concrete plan to act on that duty starting today?
?
Have you ever looked at someone who sins openly and thought, 'That person is not a true believer'? How does the majority view—that the abandoner of action is still a believer who will enter Paradise—challenge your willingness to judge others' faith? Commit today to leaving judgment to Allah and instead focus on your own actions.
?
The author calls the idea that abandoning supererogatory acts can deprive one of Paradise 'irrational.' Yet, are there small, recommended acts you consistently ignore (like sunnah prayers or charity) while assuming you will be saved? How does this lesson move you to take even the extra acts seriously, without falling into despair or presumption? What one supererogatory act will you begin to perform regularly from now on?

Conditions of Oral Confession
Taftazani enumerates conditions of oral confession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions under which oral confession of faith is required, including public declaration, private completion, and the rulings for the incapable and the stubborn.

Core Idea
Oral confession must be public when required, but private for one's own faith.

In this passage, Taftazani lays out the jurisprudential conditions for oral confession as part of faith. He distinguishes between different contexts and abilities, showing that the requirement varies. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly assessing a person's faith status.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the three scenarios
Find the three cases: public declaration, private completion, and the able refuser vs. incapable. Notice how each is ruled differently.
The passage also mentions the incapable mute by consensus.
3
Understand the ruling for refusal
Focus on the last sentence: insisting on not confessing when demanded makes one a disbeliever. This is a sign of lack of inner affirmation (tasdiq).
This connects to the case of Abu Talib later.
4
Reflect on the principle
Ask yourself: why does intention and ability matter? The passage shows that faith is not merely external but tied to inner conviction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بد أن يكون على وجه الإعلان والإظهار على الإمام وغيره من أهل الإسلام. بخلاف ما إذا كان لإتمام الإيمان، فإنه يكفي مجرد التكلم، وإن لم يظهر على غيره. ثم الخلاف فيما إذا كان قادراً ، وترك التكلم، لا على وجه الإباء. إذ العاجز كالأخرس مؤمن وفاقاً. والمصر على عدم الإقرار مع المطالبة بـه كافر وفاقاً لكون ذلك من أمارات عدم التصديق. ولهذا أطبقوا على كفر أبي طالب وإن كابرت الروافض غير متأملين في أنه كان أشهر أعمام النبي اهتماماً (عليه السلام)، وأكثرهم به، وأوفرهم حرصاً من النبي (عليه السلام) على إيمانه فكيف اشتهر إيمان حمزة والعباس (رضي الله عنهما)، وشاع على رؤوس المنابر فيما بين الناس، وورد في إيمانهما الأحاديث المشهورة، وكثر منهما في الإسلام المساعي المشكورة دون أبي طالب.
It must be in the manner of declaration and manifestation before the Imam and other Muslims. Contrary to the case when it is for completion of faith, then mere speaking suffices, even if not manifested to others. Then the disagreement is regarding one who is able and leaves speaking, not out of refusal. For the incapable, like the mute, is a believer by consensus. And the one who insists on not confessing while being demanded is a disbeliever by consensus, because that is a sign of lack of affirmation. For this reason, they unanimously agreed on the disbelief of Abu Talib, even though the Rafidah dispute it, not contemplating that he was the most famous of the Prophet's uncles in care, and the most abundant of them in [care] for him, and the most eager of them for the Prophet's faith. So how was the faith of Hamzah and al-'Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them) well-known and spread from the pulpits among the people, and famous hadiths came about their faith, and their commendable efforts in Islam were abundant, unlike Abu Talib.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions of oral confession (iqrar) in matters of faith. It begins by stating that declaration must be public before the Imam and other Muslims, unless it is for the completion of a person's own faith, in which case mere speaking suffices even if not publicly manifested. Then it addresses the case of a person who is able to speak but refuses, not out of stubbornness; the incapable (like a mute) is still a believer by consensus. However, insisting on not confessing when demanded is a sign of lack of inner affirmation (tasdiq), making one a disbeliever. The passage then applies this principle to Abu Talib, the Prophet's uncle, noting that despite his care for the Prophet, his refusal to confess indicates his disbelief, contrary to the Rafidah claim. The popularity of Hamzah and al-Abbas's faith is contrasted.

Key Terms
إقرار
إظهار
تصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any silent person is a disbeliever.
Correction: The text shows that inability or private completion makes one a believer; only persistent refusal with ability indicates disbelief.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to a person who is able to speak but refuses to confess when demanded?
A) He is considered a believer if his heart is certain.
B) He is considered a disbeliever because refusal indicates lack of affirmation.
C) He is excused due to his stubbornness.
D) He is treated as a mute.

Key Takeaway
Oral confession is not absolute; its requirement varies with ability and context, and persistent refusal indicates lack of faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been in a situation where speaking your faith aloud could cost you something? Knowing that the mute is excused, but the one who refuses is not—where do you truly stand in your heart when silence is safer than confession?
?
If the Imam or a Muslim authority demanded you to declare your faith publicly, would you hesitate? What would your hesitation reveal about the state of your tasdiq?
?
The passage teaches that mere utterance suffices for your own faith—but do you treat your private declaration as enough, or do you shy away even from that? Today, before you sleep, will you whisper the shahada with full presence of heart?

The Case of Abu Talib: Consensus on Disbelief
Taftazani applies the rule on persistent refusal to Abu Talib and refutes the Rafidah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni consensus on Abu Talib's disbelief and the argument from comparison with Hamzah and Abbas.

Core Idea
Abu Talib's refusal to confess despite his love for the Prophet proves his disbelief.

Taftazani applies the principle of refusal to confess to the case of Abu Talib, establishing consensus on his disbelief. He then refutes the Rafidah claim by contrasting Abu Talib's lack of public faith with the well-known faith of Hamzah and Abbas, who are universally accepted as believers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Abu Talib
Read the Arabic passage and translation, noting the logical connection to the previous principle.
The opening 'ولهذا' links back to the earlier rule about persistent refusal.
2
Identify the argument
Find the two parts: the consensus on disbelief and the refutation of the Rafidah using the comparison with Hamzah and Abbas.
3
Understand the comparison
Notice that Hamzah and Abbas are known believers due to public confession, hadiths, and deeds. The absence of such for Abu Talib is decisive.
The Rafidah claim that Abu Talib's care implies faith is insufficient without oral testimony.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: why does the lack of public knowledge about his faith outweigh his personal care? This shows the importance of oral confession as a public sign.

The Fourth Definition of Iman and the Three Schools on Works
Taftazani defines iman as heart, tongue, limbs, and lists three schools on abandoning works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth definition of iman as encompassing heart, tongue, and limbs, and the three main schools' positions on whether abandoning works removes one from faith.

Core Idea
Iman is not just inner belief; it includes words and deeds, but schools differ on the consequence of missing some.

This passage introduces a comprehensive definition of faith that includes confession, affirmation, and action. It then outlines three major theological schools—the Khawarij, Mu'tazila, and Salaf—and their stances on whether abandoning works excludes one from faith. Understanding these positions is key to appreciating the spectrum of Islamic thought on iman.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of iman
Start by reading the fourth definition: iman is a name for the action of the heart, tongue, and limbs—confession, affirmation, and works.
Notice that this definition is broader than mere inner belief.
2
Identify the Khawarij position
The Khawarij hold that abandoning works removes one from faith entirely, making him a disbeliever.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila place the abandoner in a middle station (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn), neither believer nor disbeliever. Note their internal disagreement on which works matter.
Abu Ali and Abu Hashim require wajibat; Abu al-Hudhayl and Abd al-Jabbar include mandubat.
4
Contrast with the Salaf position
The Salaf, most hadith scholars, and many mutakallimun hold that the abandoner of works is still a believer and will eventually enter paradise.
This position is attributed to Malik, al-Shafi'i, and al-Awza'i.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما على الرابع، وهو أن يكون الإيمان اسماً لفعل القلب واللسان والجوارح على ما يقال إنه إقرار باللسان وتصديق بالجنان، وعمل بالأركان . فقد يجعل تارك العمل خارجاً عن الإيمان، داخلاً في الكفر، وإليه ذهب الخوارج، أو غير داخل فيه وهو القول بالمنزلة بين المنزلتين، وإليه ذهب المعتزلة ، إلا أنهم اختلفوا في الأعمال، فعند أبي علي وأبي هاشم ، فعل الواجبات وترك المحظورات وعند أبي الهذيل وعبد الجبار، فعل الطاعات واجبة كانت أو مندوبة، إلا أن الخروج عن الإيمان، وحرمان دخول الجنة بترك المندوب مما لا ينبغي أن يكون مذهباً لعاقل. وقد لا يجعل تارك العمل خارجاً عن الإيمان، بل يقطع بدخول الجنة، وعدم خلوده في النار، وهو مذهب أكثر السلف ، وجميع أئمة الحديث، وكثير من المتكلمين، والمحكى عن مالك والشافعي والأوزاعي، وعليه إشكال ظاهر وهو أنه كيف لا ينتفي الشيء؟ انتفاء ركنه، أعني الأعمال وكيف يدخل الجنة من لم يتصف بما جعل اسماً للإيمان؟ وجوابه أن الإيمان يطلق على ما هو الأصل والأساس في دخول الجنة، وهو التصديق وحده، أو مع الإقرار. وعلى ما هو الكامل المنجي بلا خلاف، وهو التصديق مع الإقرار والعمل على ما أشير إليه بقوله تعالى : إنما المؤمنون الذين إذا ذكر الله وجلت قلوبهم . . . (۱) إلى قوله : أولئك هم المؤمنون حقاً . وموضع الخلاف أن مطلق الاسم للأول أم الثاني؟ وذكر الإمام في وجه الضبط أن الإيمان إما أن يكون اسماً لعمل القلب فقط، وهو المعرفة عنـد الإمامية، وجهم، والتصديق عندنا وإما لعمل الجوارح. فإن كان هو القول، فمذهب الكرامية أو سائر الأعمال، فمذهب المعتزلة. وإما مجموع عمل القلب والجوارح ، وهو مذهب السلف وفيه اختلال من جهة ترك عمل القلب في مذهب الاعتزال، وعدم التعرض لمذهب التصديق والإقرار.
As for the fourth [definition], that faith is a name for the action of the heart, tongue, and limbs, according to what is said that it is confession by the tongue, affirmation by the heart, and action by the limbs. So the abandoner of action may be considered outside of faith, entering into disbelief – and to this went the Khawarij – or not entering into it, and that is the position of the station between the two stations, and to this went the Mu'tazila, except they differed regarding actions: according to Abu 'Ali and Abu Hashim, it is performing obligations and avoiding prohibitions; according to Abu al-Hudhayl and 'Abd al-Jabbar, it is performing acts of obedience, whether obligatory or supererogatory, except that exiting from faith and being deprived of entering paradise due to abandoning supererogatory [acts] is not a position befitting a rational person. And sometimes the abandoner of action is not considered outside of faith, rather it is decisively concluded that he enters paradise and does not remain eternally in the fire, and this is the school of most of the Salaf, all the Imams of Hadith, and many of the mutakallimun, and it is reported from Malik, al-Shafi'i, and al-Awza'i. And there is an apparent problem: how does something not become nonexistent due to the nonexistence of its pillar, i.e., works? And how does one enter paradise who does not possess that which is made a name for faith? The answer is that 'faith' is used for that which is the origin and foundation for entering paradise, which is affirmation alone or with confession; and for that which is the complete [faith] that saves without dispute, which is affirmation with confession and action, as indicated by His saying: 'Indeed, the believers are those who, when Allah is mentioned, their hearts tremble...' up to His saying: 'Those are the believers truly.' And the point of disagreement is whether the absolute name is for the first or the second? And the Imam mentioned in a manner of control that faith is either a name for the action of the heart only – which is knowledge according to the Imamiyyah and Jahm, and affirmation according to us – or for the action of the limbs. If it is speech, then the school of the Karamiyyah; or for all actions, then the school of the Mu'tazila. Or it is a combination of the action of the heart and limbs, and that is the school of the Salaf, and in it is a defect from the aspect of neglecting the action of the heart in the Mu'tazila school, and not addressing the school of affirmation and confession.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth view of iman as encompassing heart, tongue, and limbs (confession, affirmation, and works). It then presents three schools: 1) Khawarij: abandoner of works leaves faith entirely. 2) Mu'tazila: the 'station between the two stations' – the abandoner is neither believer nor disbeliever; they differ on which works matter. 3) Salaf and others: the abandoner still enters paradise eventually, based on tasdiq alone. An objection follows: if works are part of the definition, how can one lacking works be saved? The answer distinguishes between the essential core (tasdiq, with or without iqrar) which is the foundation, and the complete name (tasdiq+iqrar+amal). The dispute is which is the absolute meaning. The Imam then summarizes: iman is either heart-action only (knowledge or tasdiq), limb-action only (speech or all actions), or both. The Salaf school combines both but the Mu'tazila neglects heart-action.

Key Terms
إيمان
عمل القلب
عمل الجوارح
خوارج
معتزلة
سلف
منزلة بين المنزلتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Salaf believe works are unnecessary for faith.
Correction: The Salaf hold that works are not part of the essential definition of iman for salvation, but they are required for complete faith and are rewarded.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which group holds that the abandoner of works is in a middle position between faith and disbelief?
A) Khawarij
B) Mu'tazila
C) Salaf
D) Karamiyya

Key Takeaway
The fourth definition of iman includes confession, affirmation, and action; the Khawarij, Mu'tazila, and Salaf differ on whether abandoning works removes one from faith, with the Salaf affirming eventual salvation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a Muslim who neglects obligatory prayers, which of the three positions (Khawarij, Mu'tazila, Salaf) do you instinctively lean toward? How might that be affecting your relationship with that person and your own humility before Allah? Take a moment to ask Allah to purify your heart from judging others.
?
The passage reports that the Salaf view leads to the assurance that the abandoner of works will still enter paradise. Are there any good works you have abandoned out of laziness or fear? How does this teaching move you to both hope in Allah's mercy and strive to act, without despairing of His grace?
?
The author says that considering leaving supererogatory acts as cause for exiting faith is 'not a position befitting a rational person.' Reflect: Have you ever burdened yourself or others with unnecessary guilt over non-obligatory matters? What would change if you adopted the balanced perspective of the Salaf?

Objection and Answer: Can One Without Works Be Saved?
Taftazani raises and answers an objection to the Salaf position on abandoning works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against the Salaf position that the abandoner of works is saved, and the answer that distinguishes between the essential core of iman (tasdiq) and its complete form (tasdiq + iqrar + amal).

Core Idea
Faith's core is affirmation; works complete it, but the saved rely on the core.

After presenting the Salaf view that the abandoner of works still enters paradise, an immediate objection arises: if works are part of the definition of iman, how can one lacking works be saved? The answer resolves this by distinguishing two uses of the word 'iman'—the essential foundation and the complete reality. This distinction is crucial for understanding the apparent contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection states: if works are a pillar of iman, how can iman remain when its pillar is missing? And how can one who does not possess what is called iman enter paradise?
2
Identify the answer's first part
The answer says 'iman' is used for the origin and foundation of entering paradise: affirmation alone or with confession.
This is the minimal definition.
3
Understand the second part of the answer
Iman is also used for the complete, saving faith: affirmation with confession and action, as indicated by the verse.
The verse mentions trembling hearts, etc.
4
Grasp the resolution
The disagreement is about which usage is the absolute name. The Salaf prioritize the foundational meaning for salvation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما على الرابع، وهو أن يكون الإيمان اسماً لفعل القلب واللسان والجوارح على ما يقال إنه إقرار باللسان وتصديق بالجنان، وعمل بالأركان . فقد يجعل تارك العمل خارجاً عن الإيمان، داخلاً في الكفر، وإليه ذهب الخوارج، أو غير داخل فيه وهو القول بالمنزلة بين المنزلتين، وإليه ذهب المعتزلة ، إلا أنهم اختلفوا في الأعمال، فعند أبي علي وأبي هاشم ، فعل الواجبات وترك المحظورات وعند أبي الهذيل وعبد الجبار، فعل الطاعات واجبة كانت أو مندوبة، إلا أن الخروج عن الإيمان، وحرمان دخول الجنة بترك المندوب مما لا ينبغي أن يكون مذهباً لعاقل. وقد لا يجعل تارك العمل خارجاً عن الإيمان، بل يقطع بدخول الجنة، وعدم خلوده في النار، وهو مذهب أكثر السلف ، وجميع أئمة الحديث، وكثير من المتكلمين، والمحكى عن مالك والشافعي والأوزاعي، وعليه إشكال ظاهر وهو أنه كيف لا ينتفي الشيء؟ انتفاء ركنه، أعني الأعمال وكيف يدخل الجنة من لم يتصف بما جعل اسماً للإيمان؟ وجوابه أن الإيمان يطلق على ما هو الأصل والأساس في دخول الجنة، وهو التصديق وحده، أو مع الإقرار. وعلى ما هو الكامل المنجي بلا خلاف، وهو التصديق مع الإقرار والعمل على ما أشير إليه بقوله تعالى : إنما المؤمنون الذين إذا ذكر الله وجلت قلوبهم . . . (۱) إلى قوله : أولئك هم المؤمنون حقاً . وموضع الخلاف أن مطلق الاسم للأول أم الثاني؟ وذكر الإمام في وجه الضبط أن الإيمان إما أن يكون اسماً لعمل القلب فقط، وهو المعرفة عنـد الإمامية، وجهم، والتصديق عندنا وإما لعمل الجوارح. فإن كان هو القول، فمذهب الكرامية أو سائر الأعمال، فمذهب المعتزلة. وإما مجموع عمل القلب والجوارح ، وهو مذهب السلف وفيه اختلال من جهة ترك عمل القلب في مذهب الاعتزال، وعدم التعرض لمذهب التصديق والإقرار.
As for the fourth [definition], that faith is a name for the action of the heart, tongue, and limbs, according to what is said that it is confession by the tongue, affirmation by the heart, and action by the limbs. So the abandoner of action may be considered outside of faith, entering into disbelief – and to this went the Khawarij – or not entering into it, and that is the position of the station between the two stations, and to this went the Mu'tazila, except they differed regarding actions: according to Abu 'Ali and Abu Hashim, it is performing obligations and avoiding prohibitions; according to Abu al-Hudhayl and 'Abd al-Jabbar, it is performing acts of obedience, whether obligatory or supererogatory, except that exiting from faith and being deprived of entering paradise due to abandoning supererogatory [acts] is not a position befitting a rational person. And sometimes the abandoner of action is not considered outside of faith, rather it is decisively concluded that he enters paradise and does not remain eternally in the fire, and this is the school of most of the Salaf, all the Imams of Hadith, and many of the mutakallimun, and it is reported from Malik, al-Shafi'i, and al-Awza'i. And there is an apparent problem: how does something not become nonexistent due to the nonexistence of its pillar, i.e., works? And how does one enter paradise who does not possess that which is made a name for faith? The answer is that 'faith' is used for that which is the origin and foundation for entering paradise, which is affirmation alone or with confession; and for that which is the complete [faith] that saves without dispute, which is affirmation with confession and action, as indicated by His saying: 'Indeed, the believers are those who, when Allah is mentioned, their hearts tremble...' up to His saying: 'Those are the believers truly.' And the point of disagreement is whether the absolute name is for the first or the second? And the Imam mentioned in a manner of control that faith is either a name for the action of the heart only – which is knowledge according to the Imamiyyah and Jahm, and affirmation according to us – or for the action of the limbs. If it is speech, then the school of the Karamiyyah; or for all actions, then the school of the Mu'tazila. Or it is a combination of the action of the heart and limbs, and that is the school of the Salaf, and in it is a defect from the aspect of neglecting the action of the heart in the Mu'tazila school, and not addressing the school of affirmation and confession.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth view of iman as encompassing heart, tongue, and limbs (confession, affirmation, and works). It then presents three schools: 1) Khawarij: abandoner of works leaves faith entirely. 2) Mu'tazila: the 'station between the two stations' – the abandoner is neither believer nor disbeliever; they differ on which works matter. 3) Salaf and others: the abandoner still enters paradise eventually, based on tasdiq alone. An objection follows: if works are part of the definition, how can one lacking works be saved? The answer distinguishes between the essential core (tasdiq, with or without iqrar) which is the foundation, and the complete name (tasdiq+iqrar+amal). The dispute is which is the absolute meaning. The Imam then summarizes: iman is either heart-action only (knowledge or tasdiq), limb-action only (speech or all actions), or both. The Salaf school combines both but the Mu'tazila neglects heart-action.

تصديق
إقرار
عمل

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the 'origin and foundation' (al-asl wa al-asas) for entering paradise?
A) Affirmation (tasdiq) alone or with confession (iqrar)
B) Affirmation, confession, and works
C) Works alone
D) Confession alone

Key Takeaway
The objection that works are a pillar of iman is resolved by distinguishing between iman as the foundation (tasdiq) and iman as complete (with works); the saved are those who have the foundation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the foundation of your salvation is only the affirmation in your heart, and not your deeds, then what truly motivates you to perform good works? Is it love, gratitude, or fear? Examine your heart today: are your works a response to the gift of iman, or are you relying on them to earn paradise?
?
This lesson teaches that the term 'iman' has two senses. Do you often confuse the minimal foundation required for salvation with the complete, transformative faith that includes works? How might that confusion affect your spiritual growth and your view of fellow Muslims who struggle with deeds?
?
The objection raised in this passage is a real intellectual challenge. Have you ever doubted Allah's mercy because you felt your works were insufficient? How does the distinction between foundational and complete iman address that doubt? What concrete step will you take today to strengthen your tasdiq – your heart's affirmation of Allah?

The Imam's Classification: Faith as Heart, Limbs, or Both
The Imam classifies definitions of iman into three categories and evaluates schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Imam's systematic classification of definitions of iman into three types: heart-action only, limb-action only, and both, along with the schools that hold each view, and the critique of the Mu'tazila position.

Core Idea
Iman definitions are reduced to three: heart alone, limbs alone, or both; each has its proponents and problems.

After resolving the objection, the Imam (likely al-Ghazali or Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) offers a systematic classification of all possible definitions of iman. He reduces them to three categories: iman as action of the heart only, iman as action of the limbs only, and iman as combination of both. This classification helps map the various schools and their shortcomings, particularly pointing out the Mu'tazila's neglect of heart-action.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification framework
The Imam states that the point of disagreement is whether the absolute name 'iman' refers to the first (core) or second (complete) meaning. He then classifies iman definitions into three types.
2
Identify the first type: heart-action only
This is knowledge (ma'rifa) for the Imamiyya and Jahm, or affirmation (tasdiq) for the Ash'aris.
3
Identify the second type: limb-action only
If it is speech, it is the Karamiyya school; if all actions, it is the Mu'tazila school.
4
Understand the third type: combination of heart and limbs
This is the Salaf school, but the Imam notes a defect: it neglects heart-action in the Mu'tazila classification and does not address the affirmation+confession school.

Objection on Multiplicity of Schools and the Answer
Taftazani answers the objection that multiplicity of schools contradicts the known command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani resolves the apparent contradiction between the multiplicity of schools on iman and the known command of the Prophet.

Core Idea
Disagreement on iman's exact criterion does not undermine the clarity of the original command.

Taftazani anticipates an objection: if the Prophet commanded a clear, known matter, how can there be about ten different schools on the definition of iman? This lesson examines his answer, which distinguishes between worldly rulings and otherworldly salvation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the objection: the interlocutor argues that the multiplicity of schools contradicts the known command.
Notice the phrase 'فإن قيل' which introduces a typical dialectical objection.
2
Identify key terms
Note the terms 'تصديق' (tasdiq), 'إقرار' (iqrar), 'أحكام دنيوية' (worldly rulings), and 'أحكام أخروية' (otherworldly rulings).
3
Understand the answer's structure
Taftazani first affirms common ground: no dispute on the core command. Then identifies the real area of disagreement: the criterion for otherworldly rulings.
The answer begins with 'قلنا'.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Note that Taftazani says 'وهذا لا بأس به' — this disagreement is not objectionable. Think about why he considers it permissible.
This shows that ijtihad in theological details does not contradict the certainty of the original command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : قد ذكرت من المذاهب ما يبلغ عشرة ونحن قاطعون بأن النبي (عليه السلام) ومن بعده كانوا يأمرون بأمر معلوم يمتثل من غير افتقار إلى بيان، ولا استفسار إلا بحسب المتعلق . أعني ما يجب الإيمان به، فكيف ذلك؟ قلنا : لا خفاء ولا خلاف في أنهم كانوا يأمرون بالتصديق وقبول الأحكام، ويكتفون في حق الأحكام الدنيوية بما يدل على ذلك، وهو الإقرار. إلا أنه وقع اختلاف واجتهاد في أن مناط الأحكام الأخروية مجرد هذا المعنى أم مع الإقرار، أم كلاهما مع الأعمال. وفي أن ذلك مجرد معرفة واعتقاد أم أمر زائد على ذلك. وهذا لا بأس به.
If it is said: 'You have mentioned from the schools about ten, while we are certain that the Prophet (peace be upon him) and those after him used to command a known matter which was complied with without needing explanation, and no inquiry except according to the particular subject – I mean what must be believed in – so how is that?' We say: 'There is no concealment and no disagreement that they used to command affirmation and acceptance of the rulings, and they were satisfied in matters of worldly rulings with what indicates that, which is confession. However, disagreement and ijtihad occurred regarding whether the criterion for otherworldly rulings is merely this meaning, or with confession, or both with actions; and whether that is mere knowledge and belief or something additional to that. And this is not objectionable.'
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents an objection: the author has listed about ten schools on the definition of iman, yet the Prophet and early Muslims commanded a clear, known matter without needing clarification, so how can there be such disagreement? The answer distinguishes between worldly rulings and otherworldly (akhrawi) rulings. For worldly matters, they commanded tasdiq and acceptance of rulings, and were satisfied with iqrar as evidence. For otherworldly salvation, however, ijtihad and difference of opinion occurred regarding the criterion: is it tasdiq alone, tasdiq with iqrar, or both with actions? And is it mere knowledge/ belief or something more? The author concludes that this difference is not problematic ('لا بأس به').

Key Terms
مذاهب
تصديق
إقرار
أحكام أخروية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The multiplicity of schools on iman means the core command is unknown or contradictory.
Correction: The core command (tasdiq and acceptance) is clear and undisputed; the disagreement concerns the precise criterion for otherworldly salvation, which is a matter of ijtihad.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what was the area of disagreement among the schools?
A) Whether the Prophet commanded tasdiq or not.
B) The criterion for otherworldly rulings.
C) Whether iqrar is required for worldly rulings.
D) The number of pillars of iman.

Key Takeaway
The multiplicity of schools on iman does not indicate confusion; it reflects ijtihad on the precise criterion for otherworldly salvation, while the core command remains clear.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter differing opinions among scholars on matters of faith, do you feel confusion or confidence in the clarity of the core message? What prevents you from trusting that the core command is known even when details are debated?
?
The early Muslims were satisfied with confession for worldly rulings. In your daily life, do you prioritize outward conformity over inner conviction? How does this lesson challenge you to examine your own criterion for what truly matters to Allah?
?
Taftazani says disagreement on the criterion for otherworldly rulings is not objectionable. Are there areas where you have held onto a particular scholarly opinion as the only truth, forgetting that ijtihad is permissible? Will you humble yourself to accept valid differences?

The Two Senses of Iman: Basis and Complete Faith
Defines iman in two senses and identifies the point of dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term iman is used in two senses: as the minimal foundation for salvation (tasdiq with or without iqrar) and as the complete faith that includes actions.

Core Idea
Iman has a minimal meaning and a complete meaning—both are used in the Quran.

This lesson introduces a crucial distinction in Islamic theology: the term iman has two meanings. The first is the absolute minimum required to be considered a believer and enter Paradise; the second is the perfect faith that guarantees salvation without dispute. This distinction underlies many later disagreements among schools.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition passage
Read the Arabic passage where the author defines the two senses of iman.
2
Identify the first sense
Find the description of the minimal meaning: tasdiq alone or with iqrar.
This is the 'origin and foundation for entering Paradise.'
3
Identify the second sense
Find the complete meaning: tasdiq, iqrar, and actions.
This is the 'complete saving faith.'
4
Note the Quranic evidence
Observe the citation of Quran 8:2-4 to support the second sense.
5
Recognize the dispute
Understand that the point of debate is which sense is the primary meaning of the absolute term 'iman.'

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انتفاء ركنه، أعني الأعمال وكيف يدخل الجنة من لم · أعني الإيمان. مع يتصف بما جعل اسماً للإيمان؟ وجوابه أن الإيمان يطلق على ما هو الأصل والأساس في دخول الجنة، وهو التصديق وحده، أو مع الإقرار. وعلى ما هو الكامل المنجي بلا خلاف، وهو التصديق مع الإقرار والعمل على ما أشير إليه بقوله تعالى : إنما المؤمنون الذين إذا ذكر الله وجلت قلوبهم . . . (۱) إلى قوله : أولئك هم المؤمنون حقاً . وموضع الخلاف أن مطلق الاسم للأول أم الثاني؟ وذكر الإمام في وجه الضبط أن الإيمان إما أن يكون اسماً لعمل القلب فقط، وهو المعرفة عنـد الإمامية، وجهم، والتصديق عندنا وإما لعمل الجوارح. فإن كان هو القول، فمذهب الكرامية أو سائر الأعمال، فمذهب المعتزلة. وإما مجموع عمل القلب والجوارح ، وهو مذهب السلف وفيه اختلال من جهة ترك عمل القلب في مذهب الاعتزال، وعدم التعرض لمذهب التصديق والإقرار . • فإن قيل : قد ذكرت من المذاهب ما يبلغ عشرة ونحن قاطعون بأن النبي (عليه السلام) ومن بعده كانوا يأمرون بأمر معلوم يمتثل من غير افتقار إلى بيان، ولا استفسار إلا بحسب المتعلق . أعني ما يجب الإيمان به، فكيف ذلك؟ قلنا : لا خفاء ولا خلاف في أنهم كانوا يأمرون بالتصديق وقبول الأحكام، ويكتفون في حق الأحكام الدنيوية بما يدل على ذلك، وهو الإقرار. إلا أنه وقع اختلاف واجتهاد في أن مناط الأحكام الأخروية مجرد هذا المعنى أم مع الإقرار، أم كلاهما مع الأعمال. وفي أن ذلك مجرد معرفة واعتقاد أم أمر زائد على ذلك. وهذا لا بأس به.
The absence of its pillar, meaning actions, and how can one enter Paradise who did not... I mean iman. Who is described with what is made a name for iman? And its answer: that iman is applied to what is the origin and foundation for entering Paradise, which is tasdiq (affirmation) alone, or with iqrar (verbal confession). And to what is the complete, saving (al-kamil al-munji) without dispute, which is tasdiq with iqrar and action, as indicated by His saying: 'Indeed the believers are those who, when Allah is mentioned, their hearts tremble...' to His saying: 'Those are the believers truly.' And the place of dispute is whether the absolute name is for the first or the second? And the Imam mentioned in a manner of precision that iman is either a name for the act of the heart only, which is ma'rifa (knowledge) according to the Imamiyyah and Jahm, and tasdiq according to us, or for the act of the limbs. If it is speech, then the school of the Karamiyyah, or all actions, then the school of the Mu'tazilah. Or as the totality of the act of the heart and the limbs, which is the school of the Salaf, and in it there is disorder from the aspect of abandoning the act of the heart in the Mu'tazilite school, and not addressing the school of tasdiq and iqrar. • Then it was said: You have mentioned from among the schools up to ten, and we are certain that the Prophet (peace be upon him) and those after him used to command a known matter that was complied with without need for explanation, and no inquiry except regarding the object—I mean what must be believed in. How is that? We said: There is no obscurity nor dispute that they used to command tasdiq and acceptance of rulings, and they were content concerning worldly rulings with what indicates that, which is iqrar. However, difference and ijtihad occurred as to whether the criterion for otherworldly rulings is merely this meaning, or with iqrar, or both with actions. And as to whether that is mere ma'rifa (knowledge) and i'tiqad (belief) or something additional to that. And there is no harm in this.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage discusses the definition of iman (faith) and the disputes among theological schools. The author begins by clarifying that iman is a term used in two senses: the minimal basis for entering Paradise, which is tasdiq (affirmation of the heart) alone or with iqrar (verbal confession), and the complete saving iman which includes actions as well, citing Quran 8:2-4. The point of contention is which sense is the primary meaning of the term. The author then lists several schools: the Imamiyyah and Jahm consider iman as knowledge (ma'rifa) of the heart; the author's own school (Ash'ari) considers it tasdiq; the Karamiyyah consider it speech (qawl); the Mu'tazilah consider it all righteous actions; and the Salaf consider it the combination of heart's act and bodily actions, though the author notes that the Mu'tazilite view omits the heart's act and the Salaf do not adequately address the distinction between tasdiq and iqrar. Next, an objection is raised: the Prophet and his companions commanded a clear matter (iman) without needing further explanation, so how can there be ten different views? The answer is that they commanded tasdiq and acceptance of rulings, and were content with iqrar for worldly rulings. However, for otherworldly rulings, scholars differed over whether the criterion is mere inner affirmation, or includes verbal confession, or also actions, and whether it is mere knowledge and belief or something more. The author concludes this is a valid area of ijtihad.

Key Terms
الإيمان
التصديق
الإقرار
العمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think iman is only about inner belief without any expression.
Correction: The passage shows that even the minimal sense includes iqrar (verbal confession) as an option, and the complete sense includes actions.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the minimal basis for entering Paradise?
A) Tasdiq alone
B) Tasdiq with iqrar
C) Tasdiq with iqrar and actions
D) Both A and B are mentioned as options

Key Takeaway
Iman has two Quranic meanings: a minimal foundation and a complete saving faith, and scholars disagree on which is primary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If iman in its minimal sense is only affirmation and confession, do you consider that sufficient for your salvation? Or does your life show the complete iman that includes actions? Which sense of iman—minimal or complete—truly describes you as you are right now? And if it is the minimal, what must you do to move toward the complete?
?
The scholars dispute which meaning of iman is primary. But ignoring that dispute, ask yourself: In your daily life, do you treat iman as a mere label you carry, or as a reality that transforms your actions? Which definition does your life actually affirm?
?
You know that iman can mean just tasdiq and iqrar. Is that enough for you to feel safe? Or does your heart demand the complete iman that changes how you live? If you are content with the minimal, what does that say about your love for Allah and your concern for pleasing Him?

Schools on the Definition of Iman
Enumerates five schools on the definition of iman and their positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the positions of the Imamiyyah, Jahm, Ash'aris, Karamiyyah, Mu'tazilah, and Salaf on what constitutes iman.

Core Idea
Different theological schools define iman as either heart belief, speech, actions, or a combination.

After establishing the two possible meanings of iman, the author presents a more detailed classification of schools. Each school emphasizes a different component: the heart, the tongue, the limbs, or all of them. This classification helps clarify the landscape of the debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration passage
Read the Arabic passage listing the schools and their views.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage divides views into act of heart, act of limbs, or combination.
3
Match schools to categories
Note which schools fall into each category: Imamiyyah/Jahm, Ash'ari, Karamiyyah, Mu'tazila, Salaf.
4
Observe the criticism
The author criticizes the Mu'tazila for ignoring the heart and the Salaf for not addressing tasdiq/iqrar distinction.

?

?

?

Objection and Answer on the Clarity of Iman
Answers an objection about multiple schools despite clear prophetic command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars reconciled the multiple definitions of iman with the fact that the Prophet commanded a clear, known matter without needing explanation.

Core Idea
Scholarly disagreements on iman are about the criteria for otherworldly salvation, not about the basic command.

An objection arises: if the Prophet commanded something clear (iman) without needing further explanation, why are there ten different schools? The answer is that the Prophet commanded the basic tasdiq and acceptance, which sufficed for worldly rulings. The disagreements concern the details of what is required for salvation in the afterlife, which is a legitimate area of ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the passage that presents the objection and the author's response.
2
Understand the objection
The objector points out that the Prophet's command was clear and needed no explanation, so why are there ten schools?
3
Distinguish worldly vs otherworldly
The author says the Prophet's command was sufficient for worldly rulings (tasdiq and iqrar).
4
Identify the source of difference
Scholars differed on the criteria for otherworldly rulings: mere inner meaning, or with iqrar, or also actions.
5
Appreciate ijtihad
The author concludes that this difference is legitimate ijtihad and not problematic.

Source Text

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter disagreement among scholars on a matter of faith, do you feel unsettled or do you recognize it as permissible ijtihad? What does your reaction reveal about your reliance on God's guidance versus your desire for certainty?
?
Are you content to settle for the bare minimum of iman that suffices for worldly acceptance (confession), or are you striving for the deeper criteria that may be required for the afterlife? How does this passage urge you to examine your own practice?
?
If the Prophet's command was clear, why do you still find yourself seeking easy answers instead of engaging with the legitimate complexities of faith that scholars have wrestled with? Will you commit to respecting scholarly difference without dismissing it as confusion?

Iman as an Act of the Heart
Presents the first position and its defense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first position establishes iman as an act of the heart, supported by Quranic and hadith evidence, and how the objection about verbal confession is resolved.

Core Idea
Iman resides in the heart before the tongue.

This lesson focuses on the first of three positions regarding the nature of iman. The passage argues that iman is fundamentally an act of the heart (fi‘l al-qalb), citing multiple Quranic verses and hadith. It also addresses a common objection: if the shahada suffices in worldly matters, does that mean speech defines iman? We'll see how the author clarifies that worldly sufficiency does not affect the essential reality of iman.

Guided Path
1
Read the first position and its evidence
Start by reading the Arabic passage for the first position. Notice the Quranic verses and hadith cited to support that iman is an act of the heart.
2
Understand the objection
The passage presents an objection: if the shahada is sufficient in worldly matters, does that mean speech defines iman? Identify this objection.
The objection is based on divine reward and worldly practice.
3
Follow the response
The author responds by distinguishing the worldly ruling from the spiritual reality. The sufficiency is only for worldly protection, not the essence of iman.
This is a key point: iman in the heart is what matters for the afterlife.

قال . لنا مقامات الأول - أنه فعل القلب لقوله تعالى: أولئك كتب في قلوبهم الإيمان (۱) وقلبه مطمئن بالإيمان (۲) ولم تؤمن قلوبهم (۳) ولما يدخل الإيمان في قلوبكم (٤) وفي الحديث: «اللهم ثبت قلبي على دينك» (۹) ، ومن كان في قلبه مثقال ذرة من حبة من خردل من الإيمان (٦) . قالوا : فأثابهم الله بما قالوا (۷) وأيضاً شاع الاكتفاء بالكلمتين . قلنا : الثواب على المقول، وهو المعنى، أو على القول بدلالته عليه والاكتفاء إنما كان في حكم الدنيا، وبه عصمة الدم والمال. ولذا قال: «أمرت أن أقاتل الناس . . . » (۸) الحديث) أن الإيمان فعل القلب دون مجرد فعل اللسان . الثاني - أنه التصديق دون المعرفة والاعتقاد. والثالث - أن الأعمال ليست داخلة فيه بحيث ينتفي هو بانتفائها .
He said: We have stations. The first: That it [iman] is an act of the heart, by His saying: 'Those – He has written faith in their hearts' (Quran 58:22), and 'his heart is at rest with faith' (Quran 16:106), and 'their hearts did not believe' (Quran 5:41), and 'faith has not entered into your hearts' (Quran 49:14). And in the hadith: 'O Allah, establish my heart upon Your religion,' and 'Whoever has in his heart the weight of a mustard seed of faith.' They said: 'So Allah rewarded them for what they said' (Quran 5:85), and also there is widespread sufficiency with the two words [i.e., declaration of faith]. We said: The reward is for the spoken content, which is the meaning, or for the speech due to its indication of it, and the sufficiency was only in the worldly ruling, and by it blood and property are protected. Hence he said: 'I have been commanded to fight the people...' hadith. That iman is an act of the heart, not merely an act of the tongue. The second: That it is tasdiq (affirmation) without ma'rifa (knowledge) and i'tiqad (belief). The third: That actions are not included in it such that it is negated by their negation.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents three positions (maqamat) regarding the nature of iman (faith). The first position is that iman is an act of the heart (fi‘l al-qalb), not merely an act of the tongue. Evidence includes Quranic verses that link iman to the heart, such as 'He has written iman in their hearts' (58:22) and 'his heart is at rest with iman' (16:106), and hadiths like 'O Allah, establish my heart upon Your religion' and the mention of a mustard seed of faith in the heart. The author then addresses a potential objection: the verse 'So Allah rewarded them for what they said' (5:85) and the widespread practice of accepting the two words (the shahada) seem to indicate that speech suffices. The response (قلنا) clarifies that the reward is for the meaning signified by the speech, and the sufficiency of verbal confession applies only to worldly rulings (like protection of life and property), not to the essence of iman. This is supported by the hadith 'I have been commanded to fight the people until they say...' which shows that outward confession is sufficient for worldly purposes. The second position is that iman is tasdiq (affirmation of the heart) specifically, not ma'rifa (knowledge) or i'tiqad (mere belief) – this distinguishes the Ash'ari view from others. The third position is that actions (a‘mal) are not part of iman, meaning that iman is not negated by the absence of actions; a person who lacks righteous deeds but has tasdiq still has iman, though it may be deficient.

Key Terms
فعل القلب
الإيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that verbal confession alone (shahada) is sufficient for iman in the afterlife.
Correction: The passage clarifies that worldly rulings differ from essential iman; the shahada suffices only for worldly protection, not for the essence of faith.

Checkpoint
According to the response in the passage, why does the shahada suffice in worldly rulings?
A) Because it proves true iman in the heart
B) Because it protects life and property in this life
C) Because actions are not required
D) Because the heart follows the tongue

Key Takeaway
Iman is essentially an act of the heart; verbal confession serves only for worldly protection, not as the reality of faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the shahada, does your heart experience the ‘tranquility with faith’ described in Quran 16:106? If not, what would it take for you to sincerely ask Allah tonight to ‘establish your heart upon His religion’?
?
This lesson teaches that worldly protection comes with the tongue, but real faith is in the heart. Are you more concerned with being seen as a Muslim by others than with the state of your heart before Allah? What is one concrete change you can make this week to prioritize inner conviction over outward appearance?
?
The Prophet himself prayed, ‘O Allah, establish my heart upon Your religion.’ If even he felt the need for this, where is your heart today? Write down one distraction that weakens your iman and commit to a specific action this week to remove or reduce it.

Iman as Tasdiq and Excluding Actions
States the second and third positions on iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari positions that iman is specifically tasdiq (affirmation) and that actions are not part of iman's definition.

Core Idea
Iman is inner affirmation, not outer deeds.

Having established that iman is an act of the heart, the passage now specifies what that act is: it is tasdiq (affirmation), distinct from mere knowledge or belief. Furthermore, actions are not part of iman, meaning a believer's sins do not negate faith. This lesson unpacks these two concise but crucial positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second position
The second position is that iman is tasdiq, not ma'rifa or i'tiqad. Understand the distinction.
Tasdiq implies a firm, heart-felt affirmation.
2
Understand the third position
The third position clearly states that actions are not part of iman. Reflect on the implications.
This means a person with tasdiq in the heart has iman even if deeds are lacking.

Faith as an Act of the Heart
Author states the first position and provides scriptural evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first position on iman: that it is an act of the heart, supported by Quranic verses and prophetic hadith.

Core Idea
True faith is planted in the heart, not just uttered by the tongue.

The author begins by stating there are three positions on iman. The first and most fundamental is that iman is an act of the heart. This lesson examines the evidence he provides from the Quran and Sunna to establish this position.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Notice that the author declares 'We have positions: First - that it is the act of the heart.' This sets the stage for the evidence.
2
Examine the Quranic evidence
Four verses are cited: 58:22, 16:106, 5:41, 49:14. Each verse connects faith directly to the heart.
Read the verses in context to see how they emphasize the heart's role.
3
Consider the hadith evidence
Two hadiths are quoted: one asking for firmness of heart on religion, another stating that an atom's weight of faith in the heart suffices.
These hadiths reinforce that faith is an internal state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال . لنا مقامات الأول - أنه فعل القلب لقوله تعالى: أولئك كتب في قلوبهم الإيمان (١) وقلبه مطمئن بالإيمان (٢) ولم تؤمن قلوبهم (٣) ولما يدخل الإيمان في قلوبكم (٤) وفي الحديث: «اللهم ثبت قلبي على دينك» (٩) ، ومن كان في قلبه مثقال ذرة من حبة من خردل من الإيمان (٦) . قالوا : فأثابهم الله بما قالوا (٧) وأيضاً شاع الاكتفاء بالكلمتين . قلنا : الثواب على المقول، وهو المعنى، أو على القول بدلالته عليه والاكتفاء إنما كان في حكم الدنيا، وبه عصمة الدم والمال. ولذا قال: «أمرت أن أقاتل الناس . . . » (٨) الحديث) أن الإيمان فعل القلب دون مجرد فعل اللسان . الثاني - أنه التصديق دون المعرفة والاعتقاد. والثالث - أن الأعمال ليست داخلة فيه بحيث ينتفي هو بانتفائها .
He said: We have positions: First - that it is the act of the heart, due to His saying, Most High: 'Those wrote faith in their hearts' (58:22), 'and his heart is at rest with faith' (16:106), 'and their hearts did not believe' (5:41), 'and faith has not entered into your hearts' (49:14), and in the hadith: 'O Allah, make my heart firm upon Your religion' (9), and 'Whoever has in his heart an atom's weight of a grain of mustard of faith' (6). They said: 'So their Lord rewarded them for what they said' (7) and also the sufficiency with the two words has become widespread. We said: The reward is upon what is said, which is the meaning, or upon the speech due to its indication of it, and the sufficiency was only in the ruling of this world, and by it is the inviolability of blood and wealth. Therefore he said: 'I have been commanded to fight the people...' (8) the hadith) that faith is the act of the heart, not merely the act of the tongue. Second - that it is assent (tasdiq), not knowledge (ma'rifa) and belief (i'tiqad). Third - that actions are not included in it such that it is negated by their negation.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage enumerates three doctrinal positions (maqamat) regarding the nature of faith (iman). The author begins by stating that there are three positions. The first position is that iman is an act of the heart, not merely the tongue. He supports this by quoting several Quranic verses: 'Those wrote faith in their hearts' (58:22), 'and his heart is at rest with faith' (16:106), 'and their hearts did not believe' (5:41), 'and faith has not entered into your hearts' (49:14), and a hadith: 'O Allah, make my heart firm upon Your religion' and 'Whoever has in his heart an atom's weight of a grain of mustard of faith.' He then reports an objection from opponents who cite the verse 'So their Lord rewarded them for what they said' (5:85) and the widespread practice of accepting the two testimonies as sufficient proof of faith. The author responds: the reward is for the meaning expressed by the speech, or for the speech as indicating that meaning; the sufficiency in worldly rulings is only for the protection of blood and property. Thus he reaffirms that iman is an act of the heart. The second position is that iman is assent (tasdiq), not mere knowledge (ma'rifa) or belief (i'tiqad) in the sense of mental conviction without assent. The third position is that actions (a'mal) are not included in the definition of iman, so that the absence of actions does not negate iman itself.

Key Terms
iman
fi'l al-qalb

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think iman is merely verbal confession without any inner reality.
Correction: The evidence clearly shows iman is primarily an inward state of the heart.

Checkpoint
According to the first position in this passage, what is faith (iman) primarily?
A) A verbal confession
B) An act of the heart
C) The performance of good deeds
D) Knowledge of God

Key Takeaway
The first position defines iman as an internal state of the heart, supported by clear scriptural proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the shahada, do you ever check whether its meaning has truly settled in your heart? What one change in your daily attitude would prove that faith is written there, not just on your tongue?
?

?
The verses show that even the hypocrites had outward actions but no faith in their hearts. What outward religious deed do you rely on most, and could you be substituting it for the inner reality that Allah looks at? Ask yourself honestly: is your heart at rest with faith?

Responding to the Objection: The Meaning Behind Words
Author addresses an objection and provides a counterargument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that verbal confession alone suffices for faith, and the author's response distinguishing between worldly and spiritual acceptance.

Core Idea
Words without heart are hollow; reward is for the meaning, not the sound.

After presenting the heart-based position, the author anticipates an objection: don't the Quran and practice suggest that simply saying the words is enough? In this lesson, we explore his nuanced response, which separates worldly rulings from true faith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The opponents cite a verse where people are rewarded for what they said, and the common practice of accepting the two testimonies as proof of faith.
The objection relies on apparent textual and practical evidence.
2
Analyze the author's response
The author argues that reward is for the meaning expressed by speech, or for the speech as indicating that meaning; worldly sufficiency is only for protection of blood and wealth.
He differentiates between spiritual worth and legal rulings.
3
Reaffirm the first position
The response concludes that iman remains an act of the heart, not mere tongue.
The objection does not undermine the original definition.

The Second and Third Positions on Faith
Author states the second and third positions concisely.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two alternative views: that faith is assent (tasdiq) without knowledge, and that actions are not part of the definition of faith.

Core Idea
Faith is not just knowledge or belief, but active assent; actions are its fruit, not its essence.

Having defended the first position, the author briefly mentions two other views. These clarify what faith is not: it is not mere knowledge (ma'rifa) or belief (i'tiqad) without assent, and it is not negated by the absence of actions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second position
This view defines iman as tasdiq (assent), distinguishing it from ma'rifa (knowledge) and i'tiqad (belief). Reflect on the difference between knowing something and actively assenting to it.
Tasdiq implies an element of will and commitment.
2
Understand the third position
This view holds that actions (a'mal) are not part of the definition of iman, so their absence does not negate iman itself. Consider how this relates to the first position.
This distinguishes between faith and its manifestations.
3
Compare all three positions
The first emphasizes the heart's act, the second emphasizes assent over mere knowledge, and the third separates actions from faith's essence. Each sheds light on a different aspect.
All agree iman is inward, but differ on its exact nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال . لنا مقامات الأول - أنه فعل القلب لقوله تعالى: أولئك كتب في قلوبهم الإيمان (١) وقلبه مطمئن بالإيمان (٢) ولم تؤمن قلوبهم (٣) ولما يدخل الإيمان في قلوبكم (٤) وفي الحديث: «اللهم ثبت قلبي على دينك» (٩) ، ومن كان في قلبه مثقال ذرة من حبة من خردل من الإيمان (٦) . قالوا : فأثابهم الله بما قالوا (٧) وأيضاً شاع الاكتفاء بالكلمتين . قلنا : الثواب على المقول، وهو المعنى، أو على القول بدلالته عليه والاكتفاء إنما كان في حكم الدنيا، وبه عصمة الدم والمال. ولذا قال: «أمرت أن أقاتل الناس . . . » (٨) الحديث) أن الإيمان فعل القلب دون مجرد فعل اللسان . الثاني - أنه التصديق دون المعرفة والاعتقاد. والثالث - أن الأعمال ليست داخلة فيه بحيث ينتفي هو بانتفائها .
He said: We have positions: First - that it is the act of the heart, due to His saying, Most High: 'Those wrote faith in their hearts' (58:22), 'and his heart is at rest with faith' (16:106), 'and their hearts did not believe' (5:41), 'and faith has not entered into your hearts' (49:14), and in the hadith: 'O Allah, make my heart firm upon Your religion' (9), and 'Whoever has in his heart an atom's weight of a grain of mustard of faith' (6). They said: 'So their Lord rewarded them for what they said' (7) and also the sufficiency with the two words has become widespread. We said: The reward is upon what is said, which is the meaning, or upon the speech due to its indication of it, and the sufficiency was only in the ruling of this world, and by it is the inviolability of blood and wealth. Therefore he said: 'I have been commanded to fight the people...' (8) the hadith) that faith is the act of the heart, not merely the act of the tongue. Second - that it is assent (tasdiq), not knowledge (ma'rifa) and belief (i'tiqad). Third - that actions are not included in it such that it is negated by their negation.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage enumerates three doctrinal positions (maqamat) regarding the nature of faith (iman). The author begins by stating that there are three positions. The first position is that iman is an act of the heart, not merely the tongue. He supports this by quoting several Quranic verses: 'Those wrote faith in their hearts' (58:22), 'and his heart is at rest with faith' (16:106), 'and their hearts did not believe' (5:41), 'and faith has not entered into your hearts' (49:14), and a hadith: 'O Allah, make my heart firm upon Your religion' and 'Whoever has in his heart an atom's weight of a grain of mustard of faith.' He then reports an objection from opponents who cite the verse 'So their Lord rewarded them for what they said' (5:85) and the widespread practice of accepting the two testimonies as sufficient proof of faith. The author responds: the reward is for the meaning expressed by the speech, or for the speech as indicating that meaning; the sufficiency in worldly rulings is only for the protection of blood and property. Thus he reaffirms that iman is an act of the heart. The second position is that iman is assent (tasdiq), not mere knowledge (ma'rifa) or belief (i'tiqad) in the sense of mental conviction without assent. The third position is that actions (a'mal) are not included in the definition of iman, so that the absence of actions does not negate iman itself.

Key Terms
tasdiq
ma'rifa
i'tiqad
fi'l

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Faith is simply knowing about God (ma'rifa) or holding a belief (i'tiqad) without active assent.
Correction: The second position argues that faith requires active assent (tasdiq), which goes beyond mere knowledge or belief.

Checkpoint
According to the second position, what is faith (iman) specifically?
A) Knowledge (ma'rifa)
B) Belief (i'tiqad)
C) Assent (tasdiq)
D) Action (fi'l)

Key Takeaway
The second position emphasizes assent over mere knowledge, and the third holds that actions do not define faith's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you find yourself equating your knowledge of Islam with genuine faith? The second position says iman is not just knowing but actively assenting. Is there a gap between what you know and what you truly affirm in your heart? Identify one belief you hold intellectually but have not fully submitted to, and ask yourself: what would it mean to move from i'tiqad to tasdiq today?
?
The third position says actions are not the essence of iman. Does that comfort you because you struggle with deeds, or does it challenge you to examine whether your faith is real even when deeds are lacking? Honestly: are you relying on your deeds for spiritual security, or on tasdiq? Resolve to strengthen your inner assent as the root, and let actions grow from it naturally.
