Faith and Disbelief
beginner
5 min
Textual Proofs that Faith is an Act of the Heart
Lists Quranic verses and prophetic hadith proving iman is an act of the heart.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic and prophetic evidence that faith (iman) is an act of the heart, not mere verbal acknowledgment.

Core Idea
Faith is rooted in the heart, not the tongue.

The author begins by marshaling clear textual evidence from the Quran and hadith to establish that iman is fundamentally a matter of the heart. These texts show that faith is written in hearts, remains in hearts despite coercion, and is distinct from mere verbal claims. Before examining rational arguments, it is crucial to see how central the heart is in the primary sources.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verses
Read each verse carefully. Notice how they mention the heart in connection with faith (iman).
Pay attention to the context of each verse.
2
Read the prophetic hadith
Read the hadith about the heart being made firm and the grain of mustard of faith.
The hadith explicitly mentions the heart.
3
Reflect on the overall message
Consider how all these texts collectively refute the idea that faith is merely verbal.
The author says denying these texts is like denying the Quran.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المقام الأول فبيانه بنصوص تدل على ذلك حتى إن القول بكون الإيمان مجرد الإقرار يكاد يجري مجرى إنكار النصوص . قال الله تعالى : أولئك كتب في قلوبهم الإيمان (١) إلا من أكره وقلبه مطمئن بالإيمان (٢) الذين قالوا آمنا بأفواههم ولم تؤمن قلوبهم (٣) قالت الأعراب آمنا قل لم تؤمنوا ولكن قولوا أسلمنا ولما يدخل الإيمان في قلوبكم (٤) إذا جاءكم المؤمنات مهاجرات فامتحنوهن الله أعلم بإيمانهن (٥) . وقال النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم : «اللهم ثبت قلبي على دينك، ومن كان في قلبه مثقال حبة من خردل من الإيمان (٦) الحديث . وقد يستدل بوجهين : أحدهما — أنه لو كان الإيمان هو القول لما كان المكلف مؤمناً حقيقة إلا حال التلفظ لانقضاء القول بعده بخلاف التصديق، فإنه باق في القلب حتى حال النوم والغفلة إلى طر آن ضده، الذي هو الكفر. وأجيب بعد تسليم كون اسم الفاعل حقيقة في الحال، دون الماضي بأن المؤمن من بحسب الشرع اسم لمن تكلم بما يدل التصديق إلى أن يطرأ ضده. وثانيهما أنا لو فرضنا عدم وضع لفظ التصديق لمعنى، أو وضعه لمعنى آخر، لم يكن المتلفظ به مؤمناً قطعاً. وأجيب بأنهم لا يعنون أن الإيمان هو التلفظ بهذه الحروف كيف ما كانت، بل التلفظ بالكلام الدال على تصديق القلب أية ألفاظ كانت، وأية حروف، من غير أن يجعل التصديق جزءاً منه . والحاصل أنه اسم للمقيد، دون المجموع .
As for the first position, its explanation is by texts indicating that, so much so that saying faith is mere acknowledgment almost becomes a mode of denying the texts. Allah Most High said: 'Those wrote faith in their hearts' (58:22), 'except him who is forced while his heart is at rest with faith' (16:106), 'those who said we believe with their mouths but their hearts did not believe' (5:41), 'The Bedouins said we believe. Say you do not believe but rather say we submit, and faith has not entered into your hearts' (49:14), 'When the believing women come to you as emigrants, examine them; Allah knows best their faith' (60:10). And the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said: 'O Allah, make my heart firm upon Your religion, and whoever has in his heart a grain of mustard of faith' (6) the hadith. And it may be argued by two ways: The first — that if faith were speech, the responsible person (mukallaf) would not be a believer in reality except at the moment of utterance, because speech ends afterwards, unlike assent (tasdiq), for it remains in the heart even during sleep and heedlessness until the occurrence of its opposite, which is disbelief. And it was answered, after conceding that the active participle is a reality in the present time, not the past, that the believer in terms of Islamic law is a name for one who speaks what indicates tasdiq until its opposite occurs. And the second — that if we suppose that the word tasdiq had no meaning, or had another meaning, then the utterer of it would certainly not be a believer. And it was answered that they do not mean that faith is the utterance of these letters however they may be, but rather the utterance of speech that indicates the heart's tasdiq, whatever words and letters, without making tasdiq a part of it. And the conclusion is that it is a name for the qualified (al-muqayyad), not the composite (al-majmu').
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage provides the detailed proof for the first position that faith (iman) is an act of the heart. The author begins by stating that the textual evidence is so clear that claiming faith is mere verbal acknowledgment almost amounts to denying the texts. He then cites several Quranic verses: 58:22 (faith written in hearts), 16:106 (heart at rest with faith despite coercion), 5:41 (those who said we believe with mouths but hearts did not believe), 49:14 (Bedouins: we believe; but faith has not entered hearts), 60:10 (examining emigrant women; Allah knows their faith). He also cites the hadith: 'O Allah, make my heart firm upon Your religion' and 'whoever has in his heart a grain of mustard of faith.' The author then presents two rational arguments. The first argument: if faith were speech, a person would be a believer only at the moment of speaking, because speech ceases afterwards; but assent (tasdiq) remains in the heart even during sleep and heedlessness until its opposite, disbelief, occurs. The response to this argument acknowledges that the active participle (ism al-fa'il) is a reality for the present state, not the past, but in Islamic law the term 'believer' applies to one who speaks what indicates inner assent, and this status continues until its opposite appears. The second argument: if we suppose the word tasdiq had no meaning or a different meaning, then the utterer would not be a believer at all. The response: they do not mean that faith is the mere utterance of specific letters; rather, it is the utterance of speech that indicates the heart's assent, without including that assent as a part. The conclusion: faith is a name for the qualified (al-muqayyad, i.e., speech indicating assent) and not for the composite (al-majmu', i.e., speech plus assent).

Key Terms
iman
qalb

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Faith is only a verbal declaration.
Correction: The Quran and hadith consistently associate faith with the heart, showing it is an internal reality.

Checkpoint
Which of the following verses is NOT cited in the passage?
A) 58:22
B) 16:106
C) 2:285
D) 60:10

Key Takeaway
The primary sources of Islam establish that iman is fundamentally an act of the heart, not mere speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever recited the Shahada with your tongue while your heart was distant or distracted? What would it take for your heart to catch up with your words?
?
If you were threatened with loss of life or property to deny your faith, do you think your heart would remain at rest with iman? What does your honest answer reveal about the current state of your faith?
?
The Bedouins said 'we believe' but faith had not entered their hearts. In what areas of your life might you be saying 'I believe' while your heart remains untouched? What is one concrete step you can take today to let faith penetrate deeper into your heart?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
6 min
The First Rational Argument: Speech is Temporary
First rational argument that faith cannot be speech because it would be temporary, with its response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational argument that faith cannot be speech because speech is temporary, and the legal response that the believer is defined by persistent speech indicating inner assent.

Core Idea
Speech ends, but faith lasts; therefore faith is not speech.

After presenting textual evidence, the author introduces two rational arguments. The first argument draws on the nature of speech: if faith were identical to the act of speaking, it would vanish the moment speech ends. But we know that believers remain believers even when silent. The response clarifies that in Islamic law, the term 'believer' applies to one who has spoken the testimony of faith with the intention that it indicates inner assent, and this status persists until its opposite appears.

Guided Path
1
Understand the argument
Read the argument: if faith is qawl (speech), a person is only a believer at the moment of utterance. Speech ends, so faith would end.
This is based on the linguistic reality of active participle and the temporality of speech acts.
2
Read the response
The response concedes that the active participle applies to the present state, but explains that in Shari'a, the believer is someone who speaks speech that indicates tasdiq, and this status continues until the opposite occurs.
The key is that the Shari'a definition gives enduring effect to that initial speech.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this addresses the objection: faith is not the speech act itself, but the speech act qualifies one as a believer as long as no contradictory state appears.
This shows that faith is not reducible to speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المقام الأول فبيانه بنصوص تدل على ذلك حتى إن القول بكون الإيمان مجرد الإقرار يكاد يجري مجرى إنكار النصوص . قال الله تعالى : أولئك كتب في قلوبهم الإيمان (١) إلا من أكره وقلبه مطمئن بالإيمان (٢) الذين قالوا آمنا بأفواههم ولم تؤمن قلوبهم (٣) قالت الأعراب آمنا قل لم تؤمنوا ولكن قولوا أسلمنا ولما يدخل الإيمان في قلوبكم (٤) إذا جاءكم المؤمنات مهاجرات فامتحنوهن الله أعلم بإيمانهن (٥) . وقال النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم : «اللهم ثبت قلبي على دينك، ومن كان في قلبه مثقال حبة من خردل من الإيمان (٦) الحديث . وقد يستدل بوجهين : أحدهما — أنه لو كان الإيمان هو القول لما كان المكلف مؤمناً حقيقة إلا حال التلفظ لانقضاء القول بعده بخلاف التصديق، فإنه باق في القلب حتى حال النوم والغفلة إلى طر آن ضده، الذي هو الكفر. وأجيب بعد تسليم كون اسم الفاعل حقيقة في الحال، دون الماضي بأن المؤمن من بحسب الشرع اسم لمن تكلم بما يدل التصديق إلى أن يطرأ ضده. وثانيهما أنا لو فرضنا عدم وضع لفظ التصديق لمعنى، أو وضعه لمعنى آخر، لم يكن المتلفظ به مؤمناً قطعاً. وأجيب بأنهم لا يعنون أن الإيمان هو التلفظ بهذه الحروف كيف ما كانت، بل التلفظ بالكلام الدال على تصديق القلب أية ألفاظ كانت، وأية حروف، من غير أن يجعل التصديق جزءاً منه . والحاصل أنه اسم للمقيد، دون المجموع .
As for the first position, its explanation is by texts indicating that, so much so that saying faith is mere acknowledgment almost becomes a mode of denying the texts. Say you do not believe but rather say we submit, and faith has not entered into your hearts' (49:14), 'When the believing women come to you as emigrants, examine them; Allah knows best their faith' (60:10). And the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said: 'O Allah, make my heart firm upon Your religion, and whoever has in his heart a grain of mustard of faith' (6) the hadith. And it may be argued by two ways: The first — that if faith were speech, the responsible person (mukallaf) would not be a believer in reality except at the moment of utterance, because speech ends afterwards, unlike assent (tasdiq), for it remains in the heart even during sleep and heedlessness until the occurrence of its opposite, which is disbelief. And it was answered, after conceding that the active participle is a reality in the present time, not the past, that the believer in terms of Islamic law is a name for one who speaks what indicates tasdiq until its opposite occurs. And the second — that if we suppose that the word tasdiq had no meaning, or had another meaning, then the utterer of it would certainly not be a believer. And it was answered that they do not mean that faith is the utterance of these letters however they may be, but rather the utterance of speech that indicates the heart's tasdiq, whatever words and letters, without making tasdiq a part of it. And the conclusion is that it is a name for the qualified (al-muqayyad), not the composite (al-majmu').

Commentary
This passage provides the detailed proof for the first position that faith (iman) is an act of the heart. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Faith is only the act of speaking the Shahada; if you don't speak, you have no faith.
Correction: Faith is based on inner assent, and the Shari'a considers the spoken declaration as an indicator of that inner state, but it is not the faith itself.

Checkpoint
According to the response, what keeps a person a believer after they have spoken the declaration of faith?
A) The words themselves continue to exist.
B) The Shari'a rule that a believer is one who has spoken words indicating tasdiq until its opposite appears.
C) The fact that tasdiq is impossible.
D) The person must continuously repeat the words.

Key Takeaway
The first rational argument shows that if faith were speech, it would be temporary, but the Shari'a definition of believer gives enduring effect to the initial speech that indicates inner assent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your faith were only as real as your last spoken word, what would that say about your current state? But this lesson shows that faith is rooted in inner assent that remains even when you are not speaking. How often do you ignore the state of your inner assent? What would it take for you to truly examine whether your tasdiq is alive in your heart right now? Commit to one action today that will demonstrate your inner faith.
?
The response in this lesson shows that the Shari'a definition gives enduring effect to the initial speech. Yet many Muslims rely solely on a past declaration without nurturing inner conviction. Which aspect of your faith—outer speech or inner assent—needs more attention today? Be specific.
?
The argument says that if faith were only speech, you would cease to be a believer when you fall silent. But in reality, your faith is supposed to be a constant state. When you go through your day, how often are you consciously aware of your inner tasdiq? What distracts you from it? Make a plan to reconnect with your inner faith during your daily activities.

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate

The Second Rational Argument and Conclusion
Second rational argument and conclusion that iman is speech indicating tasdiq, not a composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second rational argument that if the word 'tasdiq' were meaningless, utterance alone would not make one a believer, and the conclusion that faith is a name for qualified speech, not a composite.

Core Idea
Speech without inner meaning cannot be faith; faith is defined as speech that indicates heart's assent.

The second rational argument considers a hypothetical: if the word 'tasdiq' had no meaning or a different meaning, then uttering it would not make one a believer. The response clarifies that faith is not the mere uttering of sounds, but the uttering of speech that indicates the heart's assent. The passage concludes with a precise definition: faith is a name for the qualified speech (speech that indicates assent), not for the composite of speech and assent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second argument
Read the argument: if the word 'tasdiq' had no meaning, uttering it would not confer faith.
This shows that the mere phonetics are irrelevant.
2
Read the response
The response distinguishes between utterance of letters and utterance of meaningful speech that indicates tasdiq.
The key is that the speech must be meaningful and intentionally indicative of inner assent.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Read the final sentence: faith is a name for the qualified (al-muqayyad), not the composite (al-majmu').
This means faith is the speech qualified by indicating tasdiq, not the combination of speech plus tasdiq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المقام الأول فبيانه بنصوص تدل على ذلك حتى إن القول بكون الإيمان مجرد الإقرار يكاد يجري مجرى إنكار النصوص . قال الله تعالى : أولئك كتب في قلوبهم الإيمان (١) إلا من أكره وقلبه مطمئن بالإيمان (٢) الذين قالوا آمنا بأفواههم ولم تؤمن قلوبهم (٣) قالت الأعراب آمنا قل لم تؤمنوا ولكن قولوا أسلمنا ولما يدخل الإيمان في قلوبكم (٤) إذا جاءكم المؤمنات مهاجرات فامتحنوهن الله أعلم بإيمانهن (٥) . وقال النبي صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم : «اللهم ثبت قلبي على دينك، ومن كان في قلبه مثقال حبة من خردل من الإيمان (٦) الحديث . وقد يستدل بوجهين : أحدهما — أنه لو كان الإيمان هو القول لما كان المكلف مؤمناً حقيقة إلا حال التلفظ لانقضاء القول بعده بخلاف التصديق، فإنه باق في القلب حتى حال النوم والغفلة إلى طر آن ضده، الذي هو الكفر. وأجيب بعد تسليم كون اسم الفاعل حقيقة في الحال، دون الماضي بأن المؤمن من بحسب الشرع اسم لمن تكلم بما يدل التصديق إلى أن يطرأ ضده. وثانيهما أنا لو فرضنا عدم وضع لفظ التصديق لمعنى، أو وضعه لمعنى آخر، لم يكن المتلفظ به مؤمناً قطعاً. وأجيب بأنهم لا يعنون أن الإيمان هو التلفظ بهذه الحروف كيف ما كانت، بل التلفظ بالكلام الدال على تصديق القلب أية ألفاظ كانت، وأية حروف، من غير أن يجعل التصديق جزءاً منه . والحاصل أنه اسم للمقيد، دون المجموع .
As for the first position, its explanation is by texts indicating that, so much so that saying faith is mere acknowledgment almost becomes a mode of denying the texts. Allah Most High said: 'Those wrote faith in their hearts' (58:22), 'except him who is forced while his heart is at rest with faith' (16:106), 'those who said we believe with their mouths but their hearts did not believe' (5:41), 'The Bedouins said we believe. Say you do not believe but rather say we submit, and faith has not entered into your hearts' (49:14), 'When the believing women come to you as emigrants, examine them; Allah knows best their faith' (60:10). And the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said: 'O Allah, make my heart firm upon Your religion, and whoever has in his heart a grain of mustard of faith' (6) the hadith. And it may be argued by two ways: The first — that if faith were speech, the responsible person (mukallaf) would not be a believer in reality except at the moment of utterance, because speech ends afterwards, unlike assent (tasdiq), for it remains in the heart even during sleep and heedlessness until the occurrence of its opposite, which is disbelief. And it was answered, after conceding that the active participle is a reality in the present time, not the past, that the believer in terms of Islamic law is a name for one who speaks what indicates tasdiq until its opposite occurs. And the second — that if we suppose that the word tasdiq had no meaning, or had another meaning, then the utterer of it would certainly not be a believer. And it was answered that they do not mean that faith is the utterance of these letters however they may be, but rather the utterance of speech that indicates the heart's tasdiq, whatever words and letters, without making tasdiq a part of it. And the conclusion is that it is a name for the qualified (al-muqayyad), not the composite (al-majmu').
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage provides the detailed proof for the first position that faith (iman) is an act of the heart. The author begins by stating that the textual evidence is so clear that claiming faith is mere verbal acknowledgment almost amounts to denying the texts. He then cites several Quranic verses: 58:22 (faith written in hearts), 16:106 (heart at rest with faith despite coercion), 5:41 (those who said we believe with mouths but hearts did not believe), 49:14 (Bedouins: we believe; but faith has not entered hearts), 60:10 (examining emigrant women; Allah knows their faith). He also cites the hadith: 'O Allah, make my heart firm upon Your religion' and 'whoever has in his heart a grain of mustard of faith.' The author then presents two rational arguments. The first argument: if faith were speech, a person would be a believer only at the moment of speaking, because speech ceases afterwards; but assent (tasdiq) remains in the heart even during sleep and heedlessness until its opposite, disbelief, occurs. The response to this argument acknowledges that the active participle (ism al-fa'il) is a reality for the present state, not the past, but in Islamic law the term 'believer' applies to one who speaks what indicates inner assent, and this status continues until its opposite appears. The second argument: if we suppose the word tasdiq had no meaning or a different meaning, then the utterer would not be a believer at all. The response: they do not mean that faith is the mere utterance of specific letters; rather, it is the utterance of speech that indicates the heart's assent, without including that assent as a part. The conclusion: faith is a name for the qualified (al-muqayyad, i.e., speech indicating assent) and not for the composite (al-majmu', i.e., speech plus assent).

Key Terms
tasdiq
muqayyad
majmu'
lafz

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Faith consists of uttering specific Arabic words.
Correction: Faith is uttering any words that meaningfully indicate the heart's assent; the specific linguistic form is not the essence.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the passage regarding the definition of faith?
A) Faith is the utterance of specific letters.
B) Faith is the composite of speech and inner assent.
C) Faith is a name for the qualified speech that indicates tasdiq.
D) Faith is only inner assent without speech.

Key Takeaway
Faith is not the mere sound of words, but meaningful speech that indicates the heart's inner assent; it is defined as the qualified speech, not a composite of speech and assent.

First Proof: Faith vs. Mere Speech
Author gives first rational proof that iman cannot be mere utterance, with its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational argument against equating faith with mere verbal confession and how the legal definition of 'believer' resolves it.

Core Idea
Faith cannot be mere speech because speech is temporary, but heart-attestation is enduring.

This lesson examines a key objection raised against defining faith as mere utterance. The argument highlights the transitory nature of speech versus the persistent nature of inner conviction. We will see both the proof and its rebuttal based on legal terminology.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
Read the passage carefully. The proof argues that if faith were speech, it would only exist at the moment of utterance, but we know faith persists beyond that moment.
Notice how the proof contrasts speech with attestation (tasdiq).
2
Understand the contrast
Identify the key difference: speech ends after utterance, while attestation remains in the heart continuously, even during sleep.
This contrast is the core of the proof.
3
Examine the answer
The answer concedes the linguistic point but introduces the legal perspective: in Sacred Law, 'believer' refers to one who speaks what indicates attestation, and that status continues until its opposite occurs.
The answer uses the legal definition to neutralize the objection.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this discussion shows the importance of distinguishing between literal and legal meanings in theological arguments.
This lesson is a good example of how kalam arguments are refined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يستدل بوجهين : أحدهما ـ أنه لو كان الإيمان هو القول لما كان المكلف مؤمناً حقيقة إلا حال التلفظ لانقضاء القول بعده بخلاف التصديق، فإنه باق في القلب حتى حال النوم والغفلة إلى طر آن ضده، الذي هو الكفر. وأجيب بعد تسليم كون اسم الفاعل حقيقة في الحال، دون الماضي بأن المؤمن من بحسب الشرع اسم لمن تكلم بما يدل التصديق إلى أن يطرأ ضده.
And he may argue by two proofs: The first — that if faith were speech, then the responsible person would only be a believer in reality at the time of utterance because speech ends after it, unlike attestation (tasdiq), for it remains in the heart even during sleep and heedlessness until the occurrence of its opposite, which is disbelief. And it was answered, after conceding that the active participle is truly applied to the present state, not the past, that the believer (al-mu'min) in the Sacred Law is a name for one who speaks what indicates attestation (tasdiq) until its opposite occurs.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents a rational argument against defining faith as mere verbal confession. The author provides a proof (istidlal) and its rebuttal. The proof argues: if iman were qawl (speech), a person would only be a believer at the exact moment of utterance, because speech is transient. In contrast, tasdiq (heart attestation) persists continuously, even during sleep or forgetfulness, until its opposite (kufr) appears. Therefore, iman must be identified with the enduring tasdiq, not the fleeting qawl. The answer (jawab) concedes that the term 'believer' (mu'min) applies to the present state, not the past. However, according to Shar' (Sacred Law), the name 'believer' is given to one who utters what indicates tasdiq, and that status continues until the opposite arises. So the objection is neutralized: the legal definition extends the believer's status over time based on the utterance's indicative value, not on the act of utterance itself.

القول
التصديق
المؤمن
شرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If faith were speech, a person would only be a believer at the exact moment of utterance.
Correction: The legal definition extends the status of believer based on the utterance that indicates attestation, so the status continues until its opposite appears.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The first proof against equating faith with mere speech is neutralized by the legal definition that calls 'believer' one who speaks what indicates attestation, thus extending the status beyond the moment of utterance.

Second Proof: Assuming No Word for Tasdiq
Second rational proof and opponent's clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second rational proof that iman requires tasdiq and how the opponent's clarification resolves a misunderstanding.

Core Idea
Faith is not mere utterance; it is utterance that signifies heart attestation.

In this passage, the author presents a second rational argument to show that iman must be defined as tasdiq (heart attestation). The argument uses a hypothetical scenario to expose a flaw in the opposing view. However, the opponent responds with an important clarification that narrows the disagreement. Let's see how this exchange advances the discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Understand the hypothetical assumption and its conclusion: if the word 'tasdiq' had no meaning or a different meaning, the utterer would not be a believer.
2
Examine the opponent's answer
Notice how they qualify their position: they do not mean mere letters, but speech that indicates heart attestation.
This clarification shows the opponent's position is more nuanced.
3
Grasp the final distinction
Understand the terms 'muqayyad' (qualified) and 'majmu' (composite). Iman is a name for the qualified, not the composite.
The opponent agrees that tasdiq is essential, but sees utterance as a necessary qualification.

Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage gives the second rational proof for the definition of iman as tasdiq. The proof is a hypothetical: if the term 'tasdiq' had no meaning or a different meaning, then uttering it would not make one a believer. This shows that the essence of iman is in the meaning, not the utterance. The answer clarifies that proponents of the 'utterance alone' view do not mean iman is the mere articulation of letters. Rather, they mean utterance that signifies heart attestation, regardless of language. Therefore, iman is the name for the restricted concept (al-muqayyad)—i.e., speech qualified by indicating tasdiq—not for the composite of utterance and tasdiq as separate parts. So the proof was based on a misunderstanding of their position.

Responding to the Proof from 'Qawl' in the Quran
Author rebuts opponent's Quranic proof with linguistic analysis and supportive verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the inner belief requirement by reinterpreting the Quranic verse that seems to reward mere speech.

Core Idea
The word 'qawl' in the Quran is flexible, not always mere utterance.

The opponent's first proof is a verse that appears to make Paradise conditional on speech. But is that really what it means? In this lesson, we'll see the author's careful linguistic analysis that shows the verse actually points to inner meaning, not outer utterance. We'll also see how two other Quranic verses confirm that faith requires heart attestation.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's proof
Read the verse (5:85) and the opponent's claim that reward is for speech. Then read the author's first response.
Notice the word 'qawl' appears.
2
Analyze the linguistic options
Work through the two possibilities for 'ma' (relative pronoun vs. verbal noun) and the two subdivisions.
Each interpretation points to inner meaning or tasdiq.
3
Examine the supporting verses
Read the two additional Quranic verses (4:145, 2:8) and see how they support the author's position.
Both verses show that mere speech without heart belief is insufficient or leads to punishment.
4
Answer the checkpoint question
Test your understanding with a multiple-choice question about the interpretation when 'ma' is a relative pronoun.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمسك المخالف بوجهين : أحدهما ـ قوله تعالى : فأثابهم الله بما قالوا (١). حيث رتب ثواب الجنة على القول . قلنا: إن كانت «ما» موصولة فالقول بالتحقيق هو المعنى . وإن كانت مصدرية، فالقول إن حمل على اللفظ فالثواب عليه لدلالته على وجود المعنى في النفس . وإن حمل على النفس فهو نفس التصديق. ويدل على ما ذكرنا قوله تعالى : (إن المنافقين في الدرك الأسفل من النار (١) حيث رتب على القول الخالي عن تصديق القلب العقاب بالنار والمخالف أيضاً لا يخالف في ذلك. وقوله تعالى : ومن الناس من يقول آمنا بالله وباليوم الآخر وما هم بمؤمنين (٢) حيث نفى الإيمان عمن أقر باللسان دون القلب.
The opponent holds by two proofs: the first - His saying, Most High: 'So Allah rewarded them for what they said' (5:85) where He made reward of Paradise contingent upon speech. We say: if 'what' (ma) is a relative pronoun, then the speech (al-qawl) in reality is the meaning (al-ma'na). And if it is a verbal noun (masdariyya), then if speech is interpreted as utterance, the reward is upon it because it indicates the presence of the meaning in the soul; and if it is interpreted as the soul itself, then it is attestation itself. And indicates what we have mentioned His saying, Most High: 'Indeed the hypocrites are in the lowest depths of the Fire' (4:145) where He made punishment by Fire contingent upon speech devoid of heart attestation, and the opponent does not disagree with that. And His saying, Most High: 'And among people are those who say, "We believe in Allah and the Last Day," but they are not believers' (2:8) where He negated faith from those who confess with the tongue without the heart.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents the opponent's first proof from the Quran: the verse 'فأثابهم الله بما قالوا' (So Allah rewarded them for what they said) apparently makes reward conditional on mere speech. The author responds with a detailed linguistic analysis. If 'ما' (ma) is a relative pronoun, then 'what they said' refers to the meaning (al-ma'na), i.e., the belief. If 'ما' is a verbal noun (masdariyya), then 'speech' could be interpreted as utterance, and the reward is for it because it indicates inner belief; or it could be interpreted as the inner speech (the soul's content), which is exactly tasdiq. To support, the author cites two other verses: one about hypocrites being punished for speech without heart belief (showing that mere speech without inner faith is insufficient), and another negating iman from those who say with their tongue but lack heart belief. This demonstrates that the Quran connects iman to inner conviction, not just external utterance.

التصديق
المنافقون

Correction: The response shows 'qawl' can refer to meaning or indicative speech, and other verses confirm inner belief is required.

Second Proof: Verbal Confession and the Response
Opponent cites verbal confession; author distinguishes worldly vs. afterlife judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Prophet's acceptance of verbal confession only applies to worldly rulings, not to the reality of faith in the afterlife.

Core Idea
Outward confession protects in this world; only inner faith matters for the next.

The opponent argues that the Prophet accepted mere verbal confession as sufficient for faith, citing the hadith of Usama and the command to fight until people say the shahada. The author responds by drawing a crucial distinction between worldly and afterlife rulings, turning the same hadith into evidence for the necessity of inner conviction.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's proof
Read the first part of the passage where the opponent cites the Prophet's practice of accepting verbal confession. Note the specific examples mentioned.
The opponent uses two hadiths: the Usama incident and the command to fight until shahada.
2
Examine the author's response
Read the author's response starting with 'قلنا'. Identify the key distinction he makes between 'ahkam al-dunya' and 'ahkam al-akhira'.
This distinction is central to the argument.
3
Reflect on the Usama hadith
Consider how the author claims the Usama hadith actually supports his position. The Prophet rebuked Usama for judging based on outward confession, implying inner faith is what truly matters.
This turns the opponent's evidence against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيهما أن النبي عليه السلام ومن بعده كانوا يكتفون من كل أحد بمجرد الإقرار والتلفظ بكلمتي الشهادة حتى أن أسامة حين قتل من قال: «لا إله إلا الله» ذهاباً إلى أنه لم يكن مصدقاً بالقلب أنكر عليه النبي (عليه السلام) وقال : هلا شققت قلبه (٣)؟ وقال عليه السلام أمرت أن أقاتل الناس حتى يقولوا : لا إله إلا الله . فإذا قالوا ذلك، عصموا مني دماءهم وأموالهم» (٤) . قلنا : هذا في حق أحكام الدنيا، وإنما النزاع في أحكام الآخرة. وإذا تأملت فحديث أسامة لنا لا علينا .

And if you reflect, the hadith of Usama is in our favor, not against us.

Commentary
This passage presents the opponent's second proof: the prophetic practice of accepting mere verbal confession, as shown in the hadith of Usama and the command to fight until people say the shahada. The author's response makes a crucial distinction: these rulings apply only to worldly judgments (ahkam al-dunya), such as protection of life and property, not to the reality of faith in the afterlife (ahkam al-akhira). The real dispute is about the essence of iman that leads to salvation in the hereafter. Moreover, the author notes that the Usama hadith actually supports their position, because the Prophet rebuked Usama for judging the man's inner state based on outward confession, implying that inner faith is what matters. Thus, the opponent's proof is turned around.

Iman in Sharia: Tasdiq Specified, Not Transferred
Author argues that iman in sharia is not transferred from its linguistic meaning; it is tasdiq specified.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that the sharia term 'iman' is not a transferred meaning but a specification of its original linguistic meaning of tasdiq.

Core Idea
'Iman' in sharia is tasdiq specified, not a new coinage.

In this lesson, we explore the second maqam from the text, where the author defends the view that 'iman' in Islamic law retains its linguistic meaning of attestation (tasdiq). Rather than being a transferred term, it is simply tasdiq directed at specific objects of belief. This argument relies on two key points: the principle that words retain their original meaning unless proven otherwise, and the fact that the Arabs understood the term without needing clarification. The famous hadith of Jibril is cited as evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's concise argument.
Focus on the terms: naql (transfer), tasdiq (attestation), asl (origin).
2
Understand the principle against transfer
The author says transferring a word from its original meaning is contrary to the default principle (khilaf al-asl). Why is that a strong argument?
In kalam methodology, the default is that words retain their meanings unless there is clear evidence of change.
3
Examine the evidence from Arab usage
The author notes that Arabs used 'iman' without inquiry or pause, except regarding what must be believed. This suggests they understood it as tasdiq.
The hadith of Jibril is cited as proof that iman means believing in Allah, etc.
4
Recognize the specification nuance
The author clarifies that iman is not a new meaning but tasdiq specified to particular matters. The Persian gloss 'kroīdan and rāstgūy dāshtan' means believing and speaking truthfully.
This shows that both heart and tongue are involved in tasdiq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : المقام الثاني أن الإيمان (في الشرع لم ينقل إلى غير معنى التصديق لأنه خلاف الأصل، ولأن العرب كانوا يتمثلون من غير استفسار ولا توقف إلا فيما يجب الإيمان به. وقد بين بقوله : «الإيمان أن تؤمن بالله . . . » (١) الحديث. غاية الأمر أنه خص بالتصديق بأمور مخصوصة. ومعناه ما يعبر عنه بكرويدن و راستگوی داشتن
He said: The second station (al-maqam al-thani) is that faith (al-iman) in the Sacred Law was not transferred to other than the meaning of attestation (tasdiq) because that is contrary to the principle (khilaf al-asl), and because the Arabs used to act upon it without inquiry or pause except regarding what must be believed. And he explained by his saying: 'Faith is that you believe in Allah...' the hadith. The utmost is that it was specified to attestation in particular matters. And its meaning is what is expressed as 'kroīdan and rāstgūy dāshtan' (believing and speaking truth).
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage introduces the second maqam, which argues that the sharia term 'iman' was not transferred (manqul) from its linguistic meaning of tasdiq to something entirely new. Such transfer would be contrary to the principle (al-asl) that words retain their original meaning unless proven otherwise. Moreover, the Arabs understood the term without needing explanation, except for the specific objects of belief. The author references the hadith of Jibril where the Prophet defined iman as 'an tu'mina billah...' (that you believe in Allah, etc.), which shows it is tasdiq in specific matters. Therefore, iman in sharia is simply the linguistic meaning of tasdiq, but specified (mukhassas) to certain beliefs, not a new coining. The passage ends with a Persian gloss explaining that iman means believing and speaking truthfully, indicating that tasdiq involves both heart and tongue.

Correction: The author shows that 'iman' in sharia is simply the linguistic meaning of tasdiq, but specified to a particular set of beliefs. It is not a transferred term.

Iman as Tasdiq vs. Mere Knowledge
The author defends iman as tasdiq against mere knowledge using Qur'anic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why iman (faith) is defined as tasdiq (heartfelt assent) and not merely knowledge (ma'rifa), and how this distinction is supported by Qur'anic evidence.

Core Idea
Knowing the truth is not enough; faith requires heartfelt submission.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid defends the Ash'ari position that faith is more than intellectual knowledge. It argues that many disbelievers knew the truth but refused to accept it out of arrogance. The author distinguishes between 'knowing' (ma'rifa) and 'assenting' (tasdiq), which is the core of iman.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without stopping. Then read the English translation. Notice the author's tone and the flow of his argument.
The passage contains Persian terms — they show how the concept of tasdiq is expressed in another language.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the words 'إيمان', 'تصديق', 'معرفة', 'تكذيب', and 'تسليم' in the passage. Understand their relationships.
Tasdiq is the opposite of takdhib, and both differ from knowledge (ma'rifa).
3
Examine the Qur'anic evidence
The author cites four verses that show people who knew the truth but did not affirm it. List these verses and note how each supports the distinction.
Verses 2:146, 2:144, 27:14, and 17:102 are quoted.
4
Understand the need for the distinction
The author explains why it is necessary to separate knowledge from tasdiq: so that obstinate disbelievers can have one without the other.
This distinction rescues the definition of iman from being mere cognition.
5
Reflect on al-Ghazali's contribution
Note the link between tasdiq and taslim (submission). Why does al-Ghazali connect these two?
Taslim cannot coexist with denial and arrogance, unlike mere knowledge.

فذكر لفظ تؤمن بالله تعويلاً على ظهور معناه عندهم. ثم قال: هذا جبرائيل أتاكم يعلمكم دينكم. ولو كان الإيمان غير التصديق، لما كان هذا تعليماً وإرشاداً، بل تلبساً وإضلالاً. نعم لو قيل إنه في اللغة لمطلق التصديق، وقد نقل في الشرع إلى التصديق بأمور مخصوصة، فلا نزاع. وإنما المقصود أنه تصديق بالأمور المخصوصة بالمعنى اللغوي، وهو ما يعبر عنه بالفارسية بكرويدن وراستكوي داشتن ويخالفه التكذيب، وينافيه التوقف والتردد . ولهذا اختار العلماء في ألفاظ الإيمان كرويدم باور داشتم راستكوي داشتم ،بدل، وأنه معنى واضح عند العقل لا يشتبه على العوام، فضلا عن الخواص والمذهب أنه غير العلم والمعرفة، لأن من الكفار من كان يعرف الحق ولا يصدق به عناداً واستكباراً . قال الله تعالى : الذين آتيناهم الكتاب يعرفونه كما يعرفون أبناءهم وإن فريقاً منهم ليكتمون الحق وهم يعلمون) (۱). • وقال: وإن الذين أوتوا الكتاب ليعلمون أنه الحق من ربهم وما الله بغافل (۲) عما يعملون) (٢) . وقال : وجحدوا بها واستيقنتها أنفسهم ظلماً وعلوا (۳) وقال حكاية عن موسى (عليه السلام) لفرعون ولقد علمت ما أنزل هؤلاء إلا رب السموات والأرض بصائر ) (٤) فاحتيج إلى الفرق بين العلم بما جاء به النبي عليه السلام) وهو معرفته، وبين التصديق ليصح كون الأول حاصلاً للمعاندين، دون الثاني، وكون الثاني إيماناً دون الأول. فاقتصر بعضهم على أن ضد التصديق هو الإنكار والتكذيب، وضد المعرفة النكارة والجهالة. وإليه أشار الإمام الغزالي (٥) (رحمه الله) حيث فسر التصديق بالتسليم فإنه لا يكون مع الإنكار والاستكبار، بخلاف العلم والمعرفة.
He mentioned the word 'tuminu billah' relying on the clarity of its meaning among them. Then he said: 'This is Jibril who has come to teach you your religion.' If iman were other than tasdiq, this would not be teaching and guidance, but rather confusion and misguidance. Yes, if it is said that in language it is for absolute tasdiq, and it was transferred in sharia to tasdiq of particular matters, then there is no dispute. The point is that it is tasdiq of particular matters in the linguistic sense, which is expressed in Persian as 'krawdidan' and 'rastkwi dashtan', and it is opposed by takdhib and incompatible with hesitation and doubt. For this reason, scholars chose as words for iman: 'krawdidan', 'bawar dashtan', 'rastkwi dashtan' as substitutes, and that it is a meaning clear to the intellect, not confusing for the common people, let alone the elite. The madhhab is that it is other than knowledge and gnosis, because among the disbelievers were those who knew the truth but did not affirm it out of stubbornness and arrogance. Allah Almighty said: 'Those to whom We gave the Scripture know it as they know their own sons, and indeed a party of them conceal the truth while they know' (2:146). And He said: 'And indeed those who were given the Scripture know that it is the truth from their Lord, and Allah is not unaware of what they do' (2:144). And He said: 'And they denied them, while their souls were certain of them, out of injustice and arrogance' (27:14). And He said, relating from Moses (peace be upon him) to Pharaoh: 'You have known that none sent these down except the Lord of the heavens and the earth as insights' (17:102). So there was a need for a distinction between knowledge of what the Prophet (peace be upon him) brought – which is his recognition – and between tasdiq, so that it may be correct that the first is attained by the obstinate without the second, and the second is iman without the first. So some restricted themselves to saying that the opposite of tasdiq is denial and rejection, and the opposite of knowledge is ignorance and lack of recognition. And Imam al-Ghazali (may Allah have mercy on him) pointed to this when he interpreted tasdiq as taslim, for it does not occur with denial and arrogance, unlike knowledge and gnosis.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage defends the Ash'ari view that iman (faith) is tasdiq (assent) of the heart, distinguishing it from mere knowledge (ma'rifa). The author begins by referencing the hadith of Jibril where the Prophet said 'this is Jibril come to teach you your religion,' arguing that if iman were not tasdiq, the teaching would be misleading. Then he concedes that if someone says iman in language is absolute tasdiq and in sharia is tasdiq of specific matters, there is no dispute. The core point is that iman is tasdiq in the linguistic sense, which is expressed in Persian as 'krawdidan' etc. This kind of tasdiq is opposed to takdhib (denial) and incompatible with hesitation. The author then states the madhhab that iman is other than knowledge and gnosis, because many disbelievers knew the truth but did not affirm it out of stubbornness. He cites Qur'anic verses: 'Those to whom We gave the Scripture know it as they know their own sons' (2:146), 'And indeed those who were given the Scripture know that it is the truth from their Lord' (2:144), 'And they denied them, while their souls were certain of them' (27:14), and the story of Moses with Pharaoh: 'You have known that none sent down these except the Lord of the heavens and earth' (17:102). Thus, a distinction is needed between knowledge of what the Prophet brought (ma'rifa) and tasdiq, so that the first can be true of the obstinate without the second, and the second is iman without the first. Some scholars simplified: the opposite of tasdiq is denial and rejection, while the opposite of ma'rifa is ignorance. Imam al-Ghazali pointed to this by interpreting tasdiq as taslim (submission), which cannot coexist with denial and arrogance, unlike knowledge and ma'rifa.

The Voluntary and Acquired Nature of Tasdiq in Iman
Defines tasdiq as acquired and voluntary, distinguishing it from knowledge and logical tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that tasdiq in iman is an acquired, voluntary act of the heart, commanded and rewarded, and distinct from involuntary knowledge.

Core Idea
Tasdiq in iman is a voluntary tying of the heart, not just any knowledge.

The author begins by presenting a detailed definition of tasdiq (affirmation) as it pertains to iman. He clarifies that it is not merely any cognitive awareness but an acquired action of the heart undertaken by choice. This distinction is crucial because it explains why iman is commanded and rewarded—it is a voluntary act. In contrast, mere knowledge (ma'rifa) can occur without volition, as when one automatically recognizes an object. This passage also introduces the key difference between tasdiq in iman and logical tasdiq.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Notice the definitions and the example.
2
Identify the two key contrasts
Notice how the passage contrasts tasdiq with ma'rifa (knowledge) and with logical tasdiq. Identify the element that makes tasdiq in iman unique.
The passage uses the term 'ikhtiyari' (voluntary) as the defining feature.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this voluntary nature makes iman a praiseworthy act. Think about what would happen if iman were merely involuntary knowledge.
This connects to the idea of reward and responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفصل بعضهم زيادة تفصيل وقال : التصديق عبارة عن ربط القلب بما علم من إخبار المخبر، وهو أمر كسبي يثبت باخبار المصدق ولهذا يؤمر ويثاب عليه بل يجعل رأس العبادات بخلاف المعرفة فإنها ربما يحصل بلا كسب كمن وقع بصره على جسم فحصل له معرفة أنه جدار أو حجر، وحققه بعض المتأخرين زيادة تحقيق، فقال : المعتبر في الإيمـان هـو الاختياري ومعناه نسبة الصدق إلى المتكلم اختياراً. وبهذا القيد يمتاز عن التصديق المنطقي المقابل للتصور. فإنه قد يخلو عن الاختيار. كما إذا ادعى النبي النبوة، وأظهر المعجزة، فوقع في القلب صدقه ضرورة من غير أن ينسب إليه اختياراً، فانه لا يقال في اللغة إنه صدقه فلا يكون إيماناً شرعياً. كيف، والتصديق مأمور به فيكون فعلاً اختيارياً زائداً على العلم لكونه كيفية نفسانية أو انفعالاً وهو حصول المعنى في القلب والفعل القلبي ليس كذلك. بل هو إيقاع النسبة اختياراً الذي هو كلام النفس ويسمى عقد القلب. فالسوفسطائي عالم بوجود النهار ، وكذا بعض الكفار بنبوة النبي عليه السلام لكنهم ليسوا مصدقين لغة، لأنهم لا يحكمون اختياراً، بل ينكرون . وكلام هذا المحقق متردد يميل تارة إلى أن التصديق المعتبر في الإيمان نوع من التصديق المنطقي الذي هو أحد قسمي العلم لكونه مقيداً بالاختيار وكون التصديق العلمي أعم لا فرق بينهما إلا بلزوم الاختيار ،وعدمه وتارة إلى أنه ليس من جنس العلم أصلاً لكونه فعلاً اختيارياً ، وكون العلم كيفية أو انفعالاً. وعلى هذا الأخير أصـر بعض المعتنين بتحقيق معنى الإيمان، وجزم بأن التسليم الذي فسر به الإمام الغزالي التصديق ليس من جنس العلم، بل أمر وراء معناه کردن دادن و کرویدن و حق داشتن مرانداکه حق دانسته باشى ويؤيده ما ذكر إمام الحرمين (١) أن التصديق على التحقيق كلام النفس، لكن لا يثبت كلام النفس إلا مع العلم .
And some of them gave a more detailed elaboration and said: Tasdiq is an expression of tying the heart to what one knows from the report of the informant, and it is an acquired thing that is established by the report of the one being affirmed, and for this reason it is commanded and rewarded, indeed it is made the head of acts of worship, unlike knowledge which may be obtained without acquisition, like one whose eyes fall upon a body and he gains knowledge that it is a wall or a stone. And some later scholars investigated further and said: What is considered in iman is the voluntary element, and its meaning is attributing truthfulness to the speaker by choice. By this restriction it is distinguished from logical tasdiq, which is the counterpart of tasawwur, for it may lack choice. For example, if a prophet claims prophethood and shows a miracle, his truthfulness occurs in the heart necessarily without being attributed to him by choice, then it is not said in language that he affirmed him, so it is not shar'i iman. How so? And tasdiq is commanded, so it must be a voluntary act additional to knowledge, because it is a psychological quality or an emotion, which is the occurrence of the meaning in the heart, and the act of the heart is not like that. Rather, it is the voluntary positing of the relation, which is speech of the soul and is called the heart's knot. For the sophist knows the existence of daytime, and likewise some disbelievers know the prophethood of the Prophet (peace be upon him), but they are not 'musaddiqun' in language, because they do not judge voluntarily; rather, they deny. And the speech of this investigator wavers: sometimes he tends to the view that the tasdiq considered in iman is a type of logical tasdiq which is one of the two divisions of knowledge, because it is qualified by choice, and that scientific tasdiq is more general, there being no difference between them except the necessity of choice and its absence; and sometimes he tends to the view that it is not of the genus of knowledge at all, because it is a voluntary act, and knowledge is a quality or an emotion. And on this latter view, some of those concerned with investigating the meaning of iman insisted, and they asserted that taslim, with which Imam al-Ghazali interpreted tasdiq, is not of the genus of knowledge, but rather something beyond its meaning: 'kardan dadan wa krawdidan wa haqq dashtan mar an ra kih haqq danistah bashi' (giving, affirming, and holding true what you know to be true). And this is supported by what Imam al-Haramayn mentioned: that tasdiq in reality is speech of the soul, but speech of the soul is not established except with knowledge.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage provides further detail on the nature of tasdiq in iman, emphasizing that it is a voluntary act of the heart. The author begins by citing some scholars who define tasdiq as 'tying the heart to what one knows from the informant's report' — it is an acquired thing that is commanded and rewarded, even the head of acts of worship, unlike ma'rifa which can be involuntary. Then a later scholar (al-muhaqqiq) gives a more precise analysis: what counts in iman is the voluntary element, meaning attributing truthfulness to the speaker by choice. By this condition, tasdiq in iman is distinguished from logical tasdiq (the counterpart of tasawwur), which may lack choice — e.g., if a prophet claims prophethood and shows a miracle, the heart necessarily affirms his truthfulness without voluntary attribution; in language, this is not called 'sadaqahu' (he affirmed him), so it is not shar'i iman. Moreover, tasdiq is commanded, so it must be a voluntary act additional to knowledge, either as a psychological quality (kayfiyya nafsāniyya) or an emotion, but the act of the heart is different — it is the voluntary positing of relation (īqāʿ al-nisba ikhtiyāran), which is 'speech of the soul' (kalam al-nafs) and called 'the heart's knot' (ʿaqd al-qalb). The author then gives an example: a sophist knows the existence of daytime, but he does not affirm it voluntarily; likewise, some disbelievers know the Prophet's prophethood but are not 'musaddiqūn' in language because they do not judge voluntarily; they deny. The author notes that this scholar's view wavers: sometimes he considers the tasdiq in iman a type of logical tasdiq (one of the two divisions of knowledge) because it is qualified by choice, with no difference between them except the presence or absence of choice; other times he considers it not part of knowledge at all because it is a voluntary act while knowledge is a quality or emotion. The author then states that some concerned with investigating the meaning of iman have insisted on the latter view, asserting that taslim (submission), which al-Ghazali used to interpret tasdiq, is not a kind of knowledge but something beyond its meaning — 'kardan dadan wa krawdidan wa haqq dashtan mar an ra kih haqq danistah bashi' (giving, affirming, and holding true what you know to be true). This is supported by what Imam al-Haramayn mentioned: that tasdiq in reality is kalam al-nafs, but kalam al-nafs is not established except with knowledge.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Iman requires voluntary tasdiq (choice-based affirmation), not just involuntary certainty.

Checkpoint

Tasdiq as a Voluntary Act Beyond Knowledge
Explains the voluntary nature of tasdiq, discusses its relation to knowledge, and presents the debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that tasdiq in iman is a voluntary act of the heart (kalam al-nafs) that goes beyond mere knowledge, and you will explore the debate whether it is a type of logical tasdiq or something entirely different.

Core Idea
Tasdiq is a voluntary act of the heart, not a type of knowledge.

The author continues by explaining that tasdiq, being commanded, must be a voluntary act additional to knowledge. He then defines it as the voluntary positing of a relation, which is 'speech of the soul' (kalam al-nafs). The passage provides a vivid example: a sophist knows it is daytime but does not affirm it voluntarily; similarly, some disbelievers know the Prophet's truth but deny it. The discussion then turns to a scholarly debate: is the tasdiq in iman a subset of logical tasdiq or is it a separate voluntary act? The passage concludes with the view that taslim (submission) is not knowledge but something beyond it, supported by Imam al-Haramayn.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Focus on the definition of tasdiq as 'īqāʿ al-nisba ikhtiyāran' and its identification with 'kalam al-nafs'. Note the example of the sophist.
2
Examine the two views
Identify the two tendencies of the investigator: one that sees imani tasdiq as a type of logical tasdiq with a condition of choice, and another that sees it as entirely separate from knowledge.
This is a nuanced point; note the difference.
3
Understand the conclusion
Read about the emphasis on taslim as beyond knowledge, and the support from Imam al-Haramayn. Reflect on how this solidifies the voluntary nature.
The Persian phrase illustrates the meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفصل بعضهم زيادة تفصيل وقال : التصديق عبارة عن ربط القلب بما علم من إخبار المخبر، وهو أمر كسبي يثبت باخبار المصدق ولهذا يؤمر ويثاب عليه بل يجعل رأس العبادات بخلاف المعرفة فإنها ربما يحصل بلا كسب كمن وقع بصره على جسم فحصل له معرفة أنه جدار أو حجر، وحققه بعض المتأخرين زيادة تحقيق، فقال : المعتبر في الإيمـان هـو الاختياري ومعناه نسبة الصدق إلى المتكلم اختياراً. وبهذا القيد يمتاز عن التصديق المنطقي المقابل للتصور. فإنه قد يخلو عن الاختيار. كما إذا ادعى النبي النبوة، وأظهر المعجزة، فوقع في القلب صدقه ضرورة من غير أن ينسب إليه اختياراً، فانه لا يقال في اللغة إنه صدقه فلا يكون إيماناً شرعياً. كيف، والتصديق مأمور به فيكون فعلاً اختيارياً زائداً على العلم لكونه كيفية نفسانية أو انفعالاً وهو حصول المعنى في القلب والفعل القلبي ليس كذلك. بل هو إيقاع النسبة اختياراً الذي هو كلام النفس ويسمى عقد القلب. فالسوفسطائي عالم بوجود النهار ، وكذا بعض الكفار بنبوة النبي عليه السلام لكنهم ليسوا مصدقين لغة، لأنهم لا يحكمون اختياراً، بل ينكرون . وكلام هذا المحقق متردد يميل تارة إلى أن التصديق المعتبر في الإيمان نوع من التصديق المنطقي الذي هو أحد قسمي العلم لكونه مقيداً بالاختيار وكون التصديق العلمي أعم لا فرق بينهما إلا بلزوم الاختيار ،وعدمه وتارة إلى أنه ليس من جنس العلم أصلاً لكونه فعلاً اختيارياً ، وكون العلم كيفية أو انفعالاً. وعلى هذا الأخير أصـر بعض المعتنين بتحقيق معنى الإيمان، وجزم بأن التسليم الذي فسر به الإمام الغزالي التصديق ليس من جنس العلم، بل أمر وراء معناه کردن دادن و کرویدن و حق داشتن مرانداکه حق دانسته باشى ويؤيده ما ذكر إمام الحرمين (١) أن التصديق على التحقيق كلام النفس، لكن لا يثبت كلام النفس إلا مع العلم .
And some of them gave a more detailed elaboration and said: Tasdiq is an expression of tying the heart to what one knows from the report of the informant, and it is an acquired thing that is established by the report of the one being affirmed, and for this reason it is commanded and rewarded, indeed it is made the head of acts of worship, unlike knowledge which may be obtained without acquisition, like one whose eyes fall upon a body and he gains knowledge that it is a wall or a stone. And some later scholars investigated further and said: What is considered in iman is the voluntary element, and its meaning is attributing truthfulness to the speaker by choice. By this restriction it is distinguished from logical tasdiq, which is the counterpart of tasawwur, for it may lack choice. For example, if a prophet claims prophethood and shows a miracle, his truthfulness occurs in the heart necessarily without being attributed to him by choice, then it is not said in language that he affirmed him, so it is not shar'i iman. How so? And tasdiq is commanded, so it must be a voluntary act additional to knowledge, because it is a psychological quality or an emotion, which is the occurrence of the meaning in the heart, and the act of the heart is not like that. Rather, it is the voluntary positing of the relation, which is speech of the soul and is called the heart's knot. For the sophist knows the existence of daytime, and likewise some disbelievers know the prophethood of the Prophet (peace be upon him), but they are not 'musaddiqun' in language, because they do not judge voluntarily; rather, they deny. And the speech of this investigator wavers: sometimes he tends to the view that the tasdiq considered in iman is a type of logical tasdiq which is one of the two divisions of knowledge, because it is qualified by choice, and that scientific tasdiq is more general, there being no difference between them except the necessity of choice and its absence; and sometimes he tends to the view that it is not of the genus of knowledge at all, because it is a voluntary act, and knowledge is a quality or an emotion. And on this latter view, some of those concerned with investigating the meaning of iman insisted, and they asserted that taslim, with which Imam al-Ghazali interpreted tasdiq, is not of the genus of knowledge, but rather something beyond its meaning: 'kardan dadan wa krawdidan wa haqq dashtan mar an ra kih haqq danistah bashi' (giving, affirming, and holding true what you know to be true). And this is supported by what Imam al-Haramayn mentioned: that tasdiq in reality is speech of the soul, but speech of the soul is not established except with knowledge.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage provides further detail on the nature of tasdiq in iman, emphasizing that it is a voluntary act of the heart. The author begins by citing some scholars who define tasdiq as 'tying the heart to what one knows from the informant's report' — it is an acquired thing that is commanded and rewarded, even the head of acts of worship, unlike ma'rifa which can be involuntary. Then a later scholar (al-muhaqqiq) gives a more precise analysis: what counts in iman is the voluntary element, meaning attributing truthfulness to the speaker by choice. By this condition, tasdiq in iman is distinguished from logical tasdiq (the counterpart of tasawwur), which may lack choice — e.g., if a prophet claims prophethood and shows a miracle, the heart necessarily affirms his truthfulness without voluntary attribution; in language, this is not called 'sadaqahu' (he affirmed him), so it is not shar'i iman. Moreover, tasdiq is commanded, so it must be a voluntary act additional to knowledge, either as a psychological quality (kayfiyya nafsāniyya) or an emotion, but the act of the heart is different — it is the voluntary positing of relation (īqāʿ al-nisba ikhtiyāran), which is 'speech of the soul' (kalam al-nafs) and called 'the heart's knot' (ʿaqd al-qalb). The author then gives an example: a sophist knows the existence of daytime, but he does not affirm it voluntarily; likewise, some disbelievers know the Prophet's prophethood but are not 'musaddiqūn' in language because they do not judge voluntarily; they deny. The author notes that this scholar's view wavers: sometimes he considers the tasdiq in iman a type of logical tasdiq (one of the two divisions of knowledge) because it is qualified by choice, with no difference between them except the presence or absence of choice; other times he considers it not part of knowledge at all because it is a voluntary act while knowledge is a quality or emotion. The author then states that some concerned with investigating the meaning of iman have insisted on the latter view, asserting that taslim (submission), which al-Ghazali used to interpret tasdiq, is not a kind of knowledge but something beyond its meaning — 'kardan dadan wa krawdidan wa haqq dashtan mar an ra kih haqq danistah bashi' (giving, affirming, and holding true what you know to be true). This is supported by what Imam al-Haramayn mentioned: that tasdiq in reality is kalam al-nafs, but kalam al-nafs is not established except with knowledge.

Correction: Iman requires voluntary tasdiq, which is an act of the heart beyond mere knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Tasdiq in iman is a voluntary act of the heart (kalam al-nafs) that goes beyond knowledge; it is the heart's active affirmation by choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever have undeniable knowledge of a truth from Allah—like His existence, His commands, or the prophethood of Muhammad ﷺ—yet fail to voluntarily affirm it in your heart? Where in your life have you let denial creep in, even while knowing the truth? Today, commit to making one inward act of affirmation (tasdiq) for a truth you have been resisting.
?
The sophist knows it is daytime but does not affirm it voluntarily. Are there truths in your life about your own weaknesses or sins that you know intellectually but refuse to affirm voluntarily in your heart, choosing instead to live in denial? What would it cost you to finally 'knot your heart' to that truth and act on it?
?

Right now, is your faith merely intellectual agreement, or have you actively tied your heart to what you know? What is one belief you hold as knowledge but have not yet fully submitted to (taslim)? Will you make that taslim today, right now?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate

The Voluntary Nature of Assent in Faith: Two Arguments
The author presents two arguments supporting the voluntary nature of tasdiq in iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the view that faith's assent (tasdiq) is a voluntary, commanded psychic quality, supported by Ibn Sina's definition of tasdiq.

Core Idea
Inner states like faith are commanded and voluntary because they are within our power and choice.

The author addresses a key objection: how can inner faith be commanded if it is not an outward action? He presents two arguments. The first clarifies that commanded acts can be psychic qualities acquired by choice. The second strengthens his case by citing Ibn Sina, showing that tasdiq itself—whether logical or linguistic—is an act of affirmation, not mere knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening and first point
Start by reading the first few lines where the author defines the kind of tasdiq in iman and then the first point. Notice the Persian glosses and how the author sets up the issue.
The first point begins with 'الأول ـ أنه ليس معنى كون المأمور به مقدوراً...'
2
Analyze the first point's logic
Focus on the claim that 'being commanded does not require being an external action.' The author gives examples of postures, qualities, passions, and abstentions. Ask: how does this support that faith is voluntary?
Notice the phrase 'لا ينافي كونه كيفية نفسانية' – this is the key conclusion.
3
Read the second point on Ibn Sina
The author then quotes Ibn Sina's Dānishnāma-yi ʿAlāʾī to show that logical tasdiq equals linguistic affirmation. Pay attention to the Persian term 'بکرویدن' (bikrawīdan) and the translation.
This point reinforces that tasdiq is an act of affirmation, not passive cognition.
4
Trace the overall conclusion
Both points together aim to show that faith's assent is within human power, commanded, and rewarded. Summarize in one sentence how the author supports his view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحن نقول : لا شك أن التصديق المعتبر في الإيمان هو يعبر عنه في الفارسية بكرويدن و باور کردن وراستكوي داشتن إذا أضيف إلى الحاكم، وراست داشتن وحق داشتن إذا أضيف إلى الحكم ولا يكفي مجرد العلم والمعرفة الخالي عن هذا المعني . لكن ههنا مواضع نظر ومطارح فكر لا بد من التنبيه عليها، ولا غنى من الإشارة إليها :

(۲)
الأول ـ أنه ليس معنى كون المأمور به مقدوراً . واختيارياً أنه يلزم أن يكون البتة من مقولة الفعل التي ربما ينازع في كونها من الأعيان الخارجية دون الاعتبارات العقلية، بل أن يصح تعلق قدرته به وحصوله بكسبه واختياره، سواء نفسه كان في من الأوضاع والهيئات كالقيام والقعود، أو الكيفيات كالعلم والنظر. فاعلم أنه لا إله إلا هو (۱) قل انظروا ماذا في السموات والأرض (٢) . أو الانفعالات كالتسخين والتبرد والحركات والسكنات، وغير ذلك، كالصلاة أو التروك كالصوم، إلى غير ذلك. ومع هذا فالواجب المقدور المثاب عليه بحكم الشرع يكون نفس تلك الأمور، لا مجرد إيقاعها . فكون الإيمان مأموراً به اختيارياً مقدوراً مثاباً ،عليه، لا ينافي كونه كيفية نفسانية يكتسبها المكلف بقدرته واختياره بتوفيق الله تعالى وهدايته على أنه لو لزم كون المأمور به هو الفعل بمعنى التأثير، جاز أن يكون معنى الأمر بالإيمان الأمر بإيقاعه واكتسابه وتحصيله كما في سائر الواجبات .

الثاني - أن ابن سينا، وهو القدوة في فن المنطق والثقة في تفسير ألفاظه وشرح معانيه صرح بأن التصديق المنطقي الذي قسم العلم إليه وإلى التصور هو بعينه اللغوي المعبر عنه في الفارسية «بكرويدون» المقابل للتكذيب، قال في كتابه المسمی بدانش نامه علائی (دانش دو کونه است یکی در یافتن و درر سيدن وآنر ابتازی قصور خوانند و دوم کرویدن و آنر ابتازی تصدیق خوانند». بأن ثاني قسمي العلم هو المعنى الذي وضع بإزائه لفظ التصديق صریح في لغة العرب «وكر ويدن» في لغة الفرس، ونفى لما عسى يذهب إليه معاند

وهذا
And we say: There is no doubt that the assent (al-tasdiq) considered in faith (al-iman) is expressed in Persian as 'bikrawīdan' and 'bāwar kardan' and 'rāst-gūy dāshtan' when attributed to the ruler, and 'rāst dāshtan' and 'ḥaq dāshtan' when attributed to the judgment, and mere knowledge (al-ʿilm) and cognition (al-maʿrifa) devoid of this meaning are not sufficient. But here there are points of consideration and fields of thought that must be pointed out, and it is indispensable to indicate them:

(2)
First — That the meaning of something commanded being within one's power (maqdūr) and voluntary (ikhtiyārī) is not that it must necessarily be from the category of action (fiʿl) about which there might be dispute as to whether it is among external entities or intellectual considerations; rather, [it means] that his power can attach to it and it can occur by his acquisition (kasb) and choice, whether it itself is among the postures and states like standing and sitting, or the qualities like knowledge and reflection — so know that there is no god but He (Surah Muhammad, verse 19), say: 'Behold what is in the heavens and the earth' (Surah Yunus, verse 101) — or the passions like heating and cooling and movements and stillness, and other things like prayer or abstentions like fasting, and so on. And despite this, the obligatory, within one's power, rewarded by the ruling of the Law is those very things themselves, not merely their performance. So faith being commanded, voluntary, within one's power, rewarded, does not contradict its being a psychic quality (kayfiyya nafsāniyya) that the legally responsible person acquires by his power and choice with the success and guidance of Allah the Exalted. And if it were necessary that the commanded thing is the action in the sense of effecting, then it would be permissible that the meaning of the command for faith is the command to perform it, acquire it, and obtain it as in other obligations.

Second — That Ibn Sina, who is the authority in the art of logic and the trusted one in interpreting its terms and explaining its meanings, declared that the logical assent (al-tasdiq al-manṭiqī) to which knowledge is divided along with conception is exactly the linguistic one expressed in Persian as 'bikrawīdan' opposite of denial (al-takdhīb). He said in his book called Dānishnāma-yi ʿAlāʾī: 'Knowledge is two kinds: one is grasping and finding, and they call it conception (taṣawwur); the second is affirming (krawīdan), and they call it assent (taṣdīq).' That the second division of knowledge is the meaning for which the word 'tasdiq' is placed explicitly in the language of the Arabs, and 'bikrawīdan' in the language of the Persians, and he refuted what an obstinate person might go to.
And this.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents the author's own two points (al-awwal and al-thani) in defense of the view that tasdiq in iman is voluntary. The author begins by stating that the tasdiq considered in iman is expressed in Persian as 'bikrawīdan' (to believe), 'bāwar kardan' (to trust), etc., and mere knowledge devoid of this meaning is insufficient. He then flags two points for attention.

First point: The meaning of something being commanded (ma'mūr bih) and within one's power (maqdūr) and voluntary does not require it to be an external action in the category of physical motion; rather, it is sufficient that the person's power can attach to it and it can be acquired by his choice. The commanded thing can be a state like standing, or a quality like knowledge, or a passion like heating, or an abstention like fasting. The obligatory thing itself is these very things, not just their performance. Therefore, faith being a psychic quality (kayfiyya nafsāniyya) that the believer acquires by his power and choice does not contradict its being commanded and rewarded. If one insisted that the commanded thing must be an action in the sense of effecting (ta'thīr), then the command for faith could be understood as the command to acquire and obtain it, as with other obligations.

Second point: Ibn Sina, the authority in logic, explicitly states that logical tasdiq is identical to linguistic tasdiq (affirmation) as used in Persian 'bikrawīdan', opposite of denial. In his Dānishnāma-yi ʿAlāʾī, he divides knowledge into conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq), where the second is the meaning of tasdiq in Arabic and 'bikrawīdan' in Persian. This supports the author's view that tasdiq is voluntary affirmation, not mere cognition.

اختيار
قدرة
كسب
كيفية نفسانية
بکرویدن
تصديق منطقي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a psychic state cannot be commanded because it is not an outward action.
Correction: The author demonstrates that commanded acts can be inner states like faith, as long as they are within a person's power and acquired by choice.

Checkpoint
According to the author's first point, what is necessary for something to be considered commanded and voluntary?
A) It must be an external physical action.
B) It must be a quality like knowledge.
C) It must be within the person's power and acquired by choice.
D) It must be an act of worship like prayer.

Key Takeaway
The author defends the voluntary nature of faith's assent by showing that commanded acts include inner states and that Ibn Sina confirms tasdiq is an act of affirmation, not mere knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever treated your faith as something that simply 'happens' to you, rather than something you actively choose and work to acquire? Based on this lesson, what would it mean for you to consciously 'acquire' tasdiq today?
?
If Ibn Sina says that tasdiq is an act of affirmation (bikrawidan), does your own belief feel like an active affirmation or a passive acceptance? What specific step can you take to make your faith more volitional?
?
When you struggle with doubt or weak faith, do you realize that tasdiq is something you can command yourself to do? What would it look like for you to obey the command to believe, even when your heart feels reluctant?

Fourth Argument: Tasdiq Equated with Knowledge and Belief
Author cites classical authorities equating tasdiq with ma'rifa and i'tiqad, and quotes Ali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that classical scholars used tasdiq interchangeably with ma'rifa and i'tiqad, supporting its classification as a type of knowledge, and that faith adds specific conditions.

Core Idea
Tasdiq is not distinct from knowledge; it is a kind of belief.

In this fourth argument, the author draws on the actual usage of great religious scholars to show that tasdiq is not a separate faculty but is synonymous with knowledge (ma'rifa) and belief (i'tiqad). This linguistic evidence strengthens the case that tasdiq belongs to the genus of sciences. The argument culminates in a famous saying attributed to Ali, linking faith, knowledge, submission, and assent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation to grasp the author's claim: that classical authorities used tasdiq, ma'rifa, and i'tiqad interchangeably.
Notice the term 'Kruwidan' is a Latinized form referring to logical assent.
2
Identify the interchangeable terms
Pick out the terms tasdiq, ma'rifa, and i'tiqad. Understand that the author is arguing they are synonyms in classical usage.
This is the core evidence for the argument.
3
Examine the conditions for faith
Notice that while tasdiq is a type of knowledge or belief, when applied to iman (faith) it has extra conditions: acquisition, choice, and avoidance of denial and arrogance.
This shows that not every tasdiq counts as faith.
4
Analyze the quote from Ali
Read the quote: 'Faith is ma'rifa, ma'rifa is taslim, and taslim is tasdiq.' See how this chain demonstrates the equivalence in classical thought.
The quote provides authoritative support for the argument.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this argument matters: it grounds the definition of tasdiq in actual usage, not just theoretical reasoning.
This bridges logical analysis and religious tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع - أنه وقع في كلام كثير من عظماء الملة، وعلماء الأمة مكان لفظ التصديق لفظ المعرفة والاعتقاد فينبغي أن يحمل على العلم التصديق المعبر عنه بكرويدن، ويقطع بأن التصديق من جنس العلوم والاعتقادات، لكنه في الإيمان مشروط بقيود و خصوصيات كالتحصيل والاختيار، وترك الجحود والاستكبار. ويدل على ذلك ما ذكره أمير المؤمنين (علي كرم الله وجهه) أن الإيمان معرفة، والمعرفة تسليم، والتسليم تصديق
The fourth [point]: That in the speech of many of the great ones of the religion and scholars of the community, the word tasdiq has been replaced by the word ma'rifa (knowledge) and i'tiqad (belief). So it should be understood as the knowledge of tasdiq expressed by Kruwidan, and it is certain that tasdiq is of the genus of sciences and beliefs, but in faith it is conditioned by restrictions and particularities such as acquisition, choice, and abandoning denial and arrogance. And that is indicated by what the Commander of the Faithful (Ali, may God honor his face) mentioned: 'Faith is ma'rifa, and ma'rifa is taslīm (submission), and taslīm is tasdiq.'
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is the fourth argument. The author notes that many great religious scholars use the term tasdiq interchangeably with ma'rifa (knowledge/cognition) and i'tiqad (belief). Therefore, tasdiq should be understood as the knowledge expressed by Kruwidan (the logical term for assent), and it is certain that tasdiq belongs to the genus of sciences and beliefs. However, in the context of faith (iman), it is conditioned by additional restrictions: acquisition, choice, and the abandonment of denial and arrogance. The author supports this by citing a saying attributed to Ali (may God honor his face): 'Faith is ma'rifa, and ma'rifa is taslīm, and taslīm is tasdiq.' This shows the equivalence in classical usage.

Correction: The author shows that classical scholars used these terms interchangeably, demonstrating that tasdiq is a type of knowledge or belief.

B) Ma'rifa is a part of tasdiq.
